TITLE	VOLUSPO
SUBTITLE	The Wise-Woman's Prophecy
POEM	1. Hearing I ask | from the holy races,
POEM	From Heimdall's sons, | both high and low;
POEM	Thou wilt, Valfather, | that well I relate
POEM	Old tales I remember | of men long ago.
POEM	
POEM	2. I remember yet | the giants of yore,
POEM	Who gave me bread | in the days gone by;
POEM	Nine worlds I knew, | the nine in the tree
POEM	With mighty roots | beneath the mold.
POEM	
POEM	3. Of old was the age | when Ymir lived;
POEM	Sea nor cool waves | nor sand there were;
POEM	Earth had not been, | nor heaven above,
POEM	But a yawning gap, | and grass nowhere.
POEM	
POEM	4. Then Bur's sons lifted | the level land,
POEM	Mithgarth the mighty | there they made;
POEM	The sun from the south | warmed the stones of earth,
POEM	And green was the ground | with growing leeks.
POEM	
POEM	5. The sun, the sister | of the moon, from the south
POEM	Her right hand cast | over heaven's rim;
POEM	No knowledge she had | where her home should be,
POEM	The moon knew not | what might was his,
POEM	The stars knew not | where their stations were.
POEM	
POEM	6. Then sought the gods | their assembly-seats,
POEM	The holy ones, | and council held;
POEM	Names then gave they | to noon and twilight,
POEM	Morning they named, | and the waning moon,
POEM	Night and evening, | the years to number.
POEM	
POEM	7. At Ithavoll met | the mighty gods,
POEM	Shrines and temples | they timbered high;
POEM	Forges they set, and | they smithied ore,
POEM	Tongs they wrought, | and tools they fashioned.
POEM	
POEM	8. In their dwellings at peace | they played at tables,
POEM	Of gold no lack | did the gods then know,--
POEM	Till thither came | up giant-maids three,
POEM	Huge of might, | out of Jotunheim.
POEM	
POEM	9. Then sought the gods | their assembly-seats,
POEM	The holy ones, | and council held,
POEM	To find who should raise | the race of dwarfs
POEM	Out of Brimir's blood | and the legs of Blain.
POEM	
POEM	10. There was Motsognir | the mightiest made
POEM	Of all the dwarfs, | and Durin next;
POEM	Many a likeness | of men they made,
POEM	The dwarfs in the earth, | as Durin said.
POEM	
POEM	11. Nyi and Nithi, | Northri and Suthri,
POEM	Austri and Vestri, | Althjof, Dvalin,
POEM	Nar and Nain, | Niping, Dain,
POEM	Bifur, Bofur, | Bombur, Nori,
POEM	An and Onar, | Ai, Mjothvitnir.
POEM	
POEM	12. Vigg and Gandalf) | Vindalf, Thrain,
POEM	Thekk and Thorin, | Thror, Vit and Lit,
POEM	Nyr and Nyrath,-- | now have I told--
POEM	Regin and Rathsvith-- | the list aright.
POEM	
POEM	13. Fili, Kili, | Fundin, Nali,
POEM	Heptifili, | Hannar, Sviur,
POEM	Frar, Hornbori, | Fræg and Loni,
POEM	Aurvang, Jari, | Eikinskjaldi.
POEM	
POEM	14. The race of the dwarfs | in Dvalin's throng
POEM	Down to Lofar | the list must I tell;
POEM	The rocks they left, | and through wet lands
POEM	They sought a home | in the fields of sand.
POEM	
POEM	15. There were Draupnir | and Dolgthrasir,
POEM	Hor, Haugspori, | Hlevang, Gloin,
POEM	Dori, Ori, | Duf, Andvari,
POEM	Skirfir, Virfir, | Skafith, Ai.
POEM	
POEM	16. Alf and Yngvi, | Eikinskjaldi,
POEM	Fjalar and Frosti, | Fith and Ginnar;
POEM	So for all time | shall the tale be known,
POEM	The list of all | the forbears of Lofar.
POEM	
POEM	17. Then from the throng | did three come forth,
POEM	From the home of the gods, | the mighty and gracious;
POEM	Two without fate | on the land they found,
POEM	Ask and Embla, | empty of might.
POEM	
POEM	18. Soul they had not, | sense they had not,
POEM	Heat nor motion, | nor goodly hue;
POEM	Soul gave Othin, | sense gave Hönir,
POEM	Heat gave Lothur | and goodly hue.
POEM	
POEM	19. An ash I know, | Yggdrasil its name,
POEM	With water white | is the great tree wet;
POEM	Thence come the dews | that fall in the dales,
POEM	Green by Urth's well | does it ever grow.
POEM	
POEM	20. Thence come the maidens | mighty in wisdom,
POEM	Three from the dwelling | down 'neath the tree;
POEM	Urth is one named, | Verthandi the next,--
POEM	On the wood they scored,-- | and Skuld the third.
POEM	Laws they made there, and life allotted
POEM	To the sons of men, and set their fates.
POEM	
POEM	21. The war I remember, | the first in the world,
POEM	When the gods with spears | had smitten Gollveig,
POEM	And in the hall | of Hor had burned her,
POEM	Three times burned, | and three times born,
POEM	Oft and again, | yet ever she lives.
POEM	
POEM	22. Heith they named her | who sought their home,
POEM	The wide-seeing witch, | in magic wise;
POEM	Minds she bewitched | that were moved by her magic,
POEM	To evil women | a joy she was.
POEM	
POEM	23. On the host his spear | did Othin hurl,
POEM	Then in the world | did war first come;
POEM	The wall that girdled | the gods was broken,
POEM	And the field by the warlike | Wanes was trodden.
POEM	
POEM	24. Then sought the gods | their assembly-seats,
POEM	The holy ones, | and council held,
POEM	Whether the gods | should tribute give,
POEM	Or to all alike | should worship belong.
POEM	
POEM	25. Then sought the gods | their assembly-seats,
POEM	The holy ones, | and council held,
POEM	To find who with venom | the air had filled,
POEM	Or had given Oth's bride | to the giants' brood.
POEM	
POEM	26. In swelling rage | then rose up Thor,--
POEM	Seldom he sits | when he such things hears,--
POEM	And the oaths were broken, | the words and bonds,
POEM	The mighty pledges | between them made.
POEM	
POEM	27. I know of the horn | of Heimdall, hidden
POEM	Under the high-reaching | holy tree;
POEM	On it there pours | from Valfather's pledge
POEM	A mighty stream: | would you know yet more?
POEM	
POEM	28. Alone I sat | when the Old One sought me,
POEM	The terror of gods, | and gazed in mine eyes:
POEM	What hast thou to ask? | why comest thou hither?
POEM	Othin, I know | where thine eye is hidden.
POEM	
POEM	29. I know where Othin's | eye is hidden,
POEM	Deep in the wide-famed | well of Mimir;
POEM	Mead from the pledge | of Othin each mom
POEM	Does Mimir drink: | would you know yet more?
POEM	
POEM	30. Necklaces had I | and rings from Heerfather,
POEM	Wise was my speech | and my magic wisdom;
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	Widely I saw | over all the worlds.
POEM	
POEM	31. On all sides saw I | Valkyries assemble,
POEM	Ready to ride | to the ranks of the gods;
POEM	Skuld bore the shield, | and Skogul rode next,
POEM	Guth, Hild, Gondul, | and Geirskogul.
POEM	Of Herjan's maidens | the list have ye heard,
POEM	Valkyries ready | to ride o'er the earth.
POEM	
POEM	32. I saw for Baldr, | the bleeding god,
POEM	The son of Othin, | his destiny set:
POEM	Famous and fair | in the lofty fields,
POEM	Full grown in strength | the mistletoe stood.
POEM	
POEM	33. From the branch which seemed | so slender and fair
POEM	Came a harmful shaft | that Hoth should hurl;
POEM	But the brother of Baldr | was born ere long,
POEM	And one night old | fought Othin's son.
POEM	
POEM	34. His hands he washed not, | his hair he combed not,
POEM	Till he bore to the bale-blaze | Baldr's foe.
POEM	But in Fensalir | did Frigg weep sore
POEM	For Valhall's need: | would you know yet more?
POEM	
POEM	35. One did I see | in the wet woods bound,
POEM	A lover of ill, | and to Loki like;
POEM	By his side does Sigyn | sit, nor is glad
POEM	To see her mate: | would you know yet more?
POEM	
POEM	36. From the east there pours | through poisoned vales
POEM	With swords and daggers | the river Slith.
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	
POEM	37. Northward a hall | in Nithavellir
POEM	Of gold there rose | for Sindri's race;
POEM	And in Okolnir | another stood,
POEM	Where the giant Brimir | his beer-hall had.
POEM	
POEM	38. A hall I saw, | far from the sun,
POEM	On Nastrond it stands, | and the doors face north,
POEM	Venom drops | through the smoke-vent down,
POEM	For around the walls | do serpents wind.
POEM	
POEM	39. I saw there wading | through rivers wild
POEM	Treacherous men | and murderers too,
POEM	And workers of ill | with the wives of men;
POEM	There Nithhogg sucked | the blood of the slain,
POEM	And the wolf tore men; | would you know yet more?
POEM	
POEM	40. The giantess old | in Ironwood sat,
POEM	In the east, and bore | the brood of Fenrir;
POEM	Among these one | in monster's guise
POEM	Was soon to steal | the sun from the sky.
POEM	
POEM	41. There feeds he full | on the flesh of the dead,
POEM	And the home of the gods | he reddens with gore;
POEM	Dark grows the sun, | and in summer soon
POEM	Come mighty storms: | would you know yet more?
POEM	
POEM	42. On a hill there sat, | and smote on his harp,
POEM	Eggther the joyous, | the giants' warder;
POEM	Above him the cock | in the bird-wood crowed,
POEM	Fair and red | did Fjalar stand.
POEM	
POEM	43. Then to the gods | crowed Gollinkambi,
POEM	He wakes the heroes | in Othin's hall;
POEM	And beneath the earth | does another crow,
POEM	The rust-red bird | at the bars of Hel.
POEM	
POEM	44. Now Garm howls loud | before Gnipahellir,
POEM	The fetters will burst, | and the wolf run free;
POEM	Much do I know, | and more can see
POEM	Of the fate of the gods, | the mighty in fight.
POEM	
POEM	45. Brothers shall fight | and fell each other,
POEM	And sisters' sons | shall kinship stain;
POEM	Hard is it on earth, | with mighty whoredom;
POEM	Axe-time, sword-time, | shields are sundered,
POEM	Wind-time, wolf-time, | ere the world falls;
POEM	Nor ever shall men | each other spare.
POEM	
POEM	46. Fast move the sons | of Mim, and fate
POEM	Is heard in the note | of the Gjallarhorn;
POEM	Loud blows Heimdall, | the horn is aloft,
POEM	In fear quake all | who on Hel-roads are.
POEM	
POEM	47. Yggdrasil shakes, | and shiver on high
POEM	The ancient limbs, | and the giant is loose;
POEM	To the head of Mim | does Othin give heed,
POEM	But the kinsman of Surt | shall slay him soon.
POEM	
POEM	48. How fare the gods? | how fare the elves?
POEM	All Jotunheim groans, | the gods are at council;
POEM	Loud roar the dwarfs | by the doors of stone,
POEM	The masters of the rocks: | would you know yet more?
POEM	
POEM	49. Now Garm howls loud | before Gnipahellir,
POEM	The fetters will burst, | and the wolf run free
POEM	Much do I know, | and more can see
POEM	Of the fate of the gods, | the mighty in fight.
POEM	
POEM	50. From the east comes Hrym | with shield held high;
POEM	In giant-wrath | does the serpent writhe;
POEM	O'er the waves he twists, | and the tawny eagle
POEM	Gnaws corpses screaming; | Naglfar is loose.
POEM	
POEM	51. O'er the sea from the north | there sails a ship
POEM	With the people of Hel, | at the helm stands Loki;
POEM	After the wolf | do wild men follow,
POEM	And with them the brother | of Byleist goes.
POEM	
POEM	52. Surt fares from the south | with the scourge of branches,
POEM	The sun of the battle-gods | shone from his sword;
POEM	The crags are sundered, | the giant-women sink,
POEM	The dead throng Hel-way, | and heaven is cloven.
POEM	
POEM	53. Now comes to Hlin | yet another hurt,
POEM	When Othin fares | to fight with the wolf,
POEM	And Beli's fair slayer | seeks out Surt,
POEM	For there must fall | the joy of Frigg.
POEM	
POEM	54. Then comes Sigfather's | mighty son,
POEM	Vithar, to fight | with the foaming wolf;
POEM	In the giant's son | does he thrust his sword
POEM	Full to the heart: | his father is avenged.
POEM	
POEM	55. Hither there comes | the son of Hlothyn,
POEM	The bright snake gapes | to heaven above;
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	Against the serpent | goes Othin's son.
POEM	
POEM	56. In anger smites | the warder of earth,--
POEM	Forth from their homes | must all men flee;-
POEM	Nine paces fares | the son of Fjorgyn,
POEM	And, slain by the serpent, | fearless he sinks.
POEM	
POEM	57. The sun turns black, | earth sinks in the sea,
POEM	The hot stars down | from heaven are whirled;
POEM	Fierce grows the steam | and the life-feeding flame,
POEM	Till fire leaps high | about heaven itself.
POEM	
POEM	58. Now Garm howls loud | before Gnipahellir,
POEM	The fetters will burst, | and the wolf run free;
POEM	Much do I know, | and more can see
POEM	Of the fate of the gods, | the mighty in fight.
POEM	
POEM	59. Now do I see | the earth anew
POEM	Rise all green | from the waves again;
POEM	The cataracts fall, | and the eagle flies,
POEM	And fish he catches | beneath the cliffs.
POEM	
POEM	60. The gods in Ithavoll | meet together,
POEM	Of the terrible girdler | of earth they talk,
POEM	And the mighty past | they call to mind,
POEM	And the ancient runes | of the Ruler of Gods.
POEM	
POEM	61. In wondrous beauty | once again
POEM	Shall the golden tables | stand mid the grass,
POEM	Which the gods had owned | in the days of old,
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	
POEM	62. Then fields unsowed | bear ripened fruit,
POEM	All ills grow better, | and Baldr comes back;
POEM	Baldr and Hoth dwell | in Hropt's battle-hall,
POEM	And the mighty gods: | would you know yet more?
POEM	
POEM	63. Then Hönir wins | the prophetic wand,
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	And the sons of the brothers | of Tveggi abide
POEM	In Vindheim now: | would you know yet more?
POEM	
POEM	64. More fair than the sun, | a hall I see,
POEM	Roofed with gold, | on Gimle it stands;
POEM	There shall the righteous | rulers dwell,
POEM	And happiness ever | there shall they have.
POEM	
POEM	65. There comes on high, | all power to hold,
POEM	A mighty lord, | all lands he rules.
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	
POEM	66. From below the dragon | dark comes forth,
POEM	Nithhogg flying | from Nithafjoll;
POEM	The bodies of men on | his wings he bears,
POEM	The serpent bright: | but now must I sink.
TITLE	HOVAMOL
SUBTITLE	The Ballad of the High One
POEM	1. Within the gates | ere a man shall go,
POEM	(Full warily let him watch,)
POEM	Full long let him look about him;
POEM	For little he knows | where a foe may lurk,
POEM	And sit in the seats within.
POEM	
POEM	2. Hail to the giver! | a guest has come;
POEM	Where shall the stranger sit?
POEM	Swift shall he be who, | with swords shall try
POEM	The proof of his might to make.
POEM	
POEM	3. Fire he needs | who with frozen knees
POEM	Has come from the cold without;
POEM	Food and clothes | must the farer have,
POEM	The man from the mountains come.
POEM	
POEM	4. Water and towels | and welcoming speech
POEM	Should he find who comes, to the feast;
POEM	If renown he would get, | and again be greeted,
POEM	Wisely and well must he act.
POEM	
POEM	5. Wits must he have | who wanders wide,
POEM	But all is easy at home;
POEM	At the witless man | the wise shall wink
POEM	When among such men he sits.
POEM	
POEM	6. A man shall not boast | of his keenness of mind,
POEM	But keep it close in his breast;
POEM	To the silent and wise | does ill come seldom
POEM	When he goes as guest to a house;
POEM	(For a faster friend | one never finds
POEM	Than wisdom tried and true.)
POEM	
POEM	7. The knowing guest | who goes to the feast,
POEM	In silent attention sits;
POEM	With his ears he hears, | with his eyes he watches,
POEM	Thus wary are wise men all.
POEM	
POEM	8. Happy the one | who wins for himself
POEM	Favor and praises fair;
POEM	Less safe by far | is the wisdom found
POEM	That is hid in another's heart.
POEM	
POEM	9. Happy the man | who has while he lives
POEM	Wisdom and praise as well,
POEM	For evil counsel | a man full oft
POEM	Has from another's heart.
POEM	
POEM	10. A better burden | may no man bear
POEM	For wanderings wide than wisdom;
POEM	It is better than wealth | on unknown ways,
POEM	And in grief a refuge it gives.
POEM	
POEM	11. A better burden | may no man bear
POEM	For wanderings wide than wisdom;
POEM	Worse food for the journey | he brings not afield
POEM	Than an over-drinking of ale.
POEM	
POEM	12. Less good there lies | than most believe
POEM	In ale for mortal men;
POEM	For the more he drinks | the less does man
POEM	Of his mind the mastery hold.
POEM	
POEM	13. Over beer the bird | of forgetfulness broods,
POEM	And steals the minds of men;
POEM	With the heron's feathers | fettered I lay
POEM	And in Gunnloth's house was held.
POEM	
POEM	14. Drunk I was, | I was dead-drunk,
POEM	When with Fjalar wise I was;
POEM	Tis the best of drinking | if back one brings
POEM	His wisdom with him home.
POEM	
POEM	15. The son of a king | shall be silent and wise,
POEM	And bold in battle as well;
POEM	Bravely and gladly | a man shall go,
POEM	Till the day of his death is come.
POEM	
POEM	16. The sluggard believes | he shall live forever,
POEM	If the fight he faces not;
POEM	But age shall not grant him | the gift of peace,
POEM	Though spears may spare his life.
POEM	
POEM	17. The fool is agape | when he comes to the feast,
POEM	He stammers or else is still;
POEM	But soon if he gets | a drink is it seen
POEM	What the mind of the man is like.
POEM	
POEM	18. He alone is aware | who has wandered wide,
POEM	And far abroad has fared,
POEM	How great a mind | is guided by him
POEM	That wealth of wisdom has.
POEM	
POEM	19. Shun not the mead, | but drink in measure;
POEM	Speak to the point or be still;
POEM	For rudeness none | shall rightly blame thee
POEM	If soon thy bed thou seekest.
POEM	
POEM	20. The greedy man, | if his mind be vague,
POEM	Will eat till sick he is;
POEM	The vulgar man, | when among the wise,
POEM	To scorn by his belly is brought.
POEM	
POEM	21. The herds know well | when home they shall fare,
POEM	And then from the grass they go;
POEM	But the foolish man | his belly's measure
POEM	Shall never know aright.
POEM	
POEM	22. A paltry man | and poor of mind
POEM	At all things ever mocks;
POEM	For never he knows, | what he ought to know,
POEM	That he is not free from faults.
POEM	
POEM	23. The witless man | is awake all night,
POEM	Thinking of many things;
POEM	Care-worn he is | when the morning comes,
POEM	And his woe is just as it was.
POEM	
POEM	24. The foolish man | for friends all those
POEM	Who laugh at him will hold;
POEM	When among the wise | he marks it not
POEM	Though hatred of him they speak.
POEM	
POEM	25. The foolish man | for friends all those
POEM	Who laugh at him will hold;
POEM	But the truth when he comes | to the council he learns,
POEM	That few in his favor will speak.
POEM	
POEM	26. An ignorant man | thinks that all he knows,
POEM	When he sits by himself in a corner;
POEM	But never what answer | to make he knows,
POEM	When others with questions come.
POEM	
POEM	27. A witless man, | when he meets with men,
POEM	Had best in silence abide;
POEM	For no one shall find | that nothing he knows,
POEM	If his mouth is not open too much.
POEM	(But a man knows not, | if nothing he knows,
POEM	When his mouth has been open too much.)
POEM	
POEM	28. Wise shall he seem | who well can question,
POEM	And also answer well;
POEM	Nought is concealed | that men may say
POEM	Among the sons of men.
POEM	
POEM	29. Often he speaks | who never is still
POEM	With words that win no faith;
POEM	The babbling tongue, | if a bridle it find not,
POEM	Oft for itself sings ill.
POEM	
POEM	30. In mockery no one | a man shall hold,
POEM	Although he fare to the feast;
POEM	Wise seems one oft, | if nought he is asked,
POEM	And safely he sits dry-skinned.
POEM	
POEM	31. Wise a guest holds it | to take to his heels,
POEM	When mock of another he makes;
POEM	But little he knows | who laughs at the feast,
POEM	Though he mocks in the midst of his foes.
POEM	
POEM	32. Friendly of mind | are many men,
POEM	Till feasting they mock at their friends;
POEM	To mankind a bane | must it ever be
POEM	When guests together strive.
POEM	
POEM	33. Oft should one make | an early meal,
POEM	Nor fasting come to the feast;
POEM	Else he sits and chews | as if he would choke,
POEM	And little is able to ask.
POEM	
POEM	34. Crooked and far | is the road to a foe,
POEM	Though his house on the highway be;
POEM	But wide and straight | is the way to a friend,
POEM	Though far away he fare.
POEM	
POEM	35. Forth shall one go, | nor stay as a guest
POEM	In a single spot forever;
POEM	Love becomes loathing | if long one sits
POEM	By the hearth in another's home.
POEM	
POEM	36. Better a house, | though a hut it be,
POEM	A man is master at home;
POEM	A pair of goats | and a patched-up roof
POEM	Are better far than begging.
POEM	
POEM	37. Better a house, | though a hut it be,
POEM	A man is master at home;
POEM	His heart is bleeding | who needs must beg
POEM	When food he fain would have.
POEM	
POEM	38. Away from his arms | in the open field
POEM	A man should fare not a foot;
POEM	For never he knows | when the need for a spear
POEM	Shall arise on the distant road.
POEM	
POEM	39. If wealth a man | has won for himself,
POEM	Let him never suffer in need;
POEM	Oft he saves for a foe | what he plans for a friend,
POEM	For much goes worse than we wish.
POEM	
POEM	40. None so free with gifts | or food have I found
POEM	That gladly he took not a gift,
POEM	Nor one who so widely | scattered his wealth
POEM	That of recompense hatred he had.
POEM	
POEM	41. Friends shall gladden each other | with arms and garments,
POEM	As each for himself can see;
POEM	Gift-givers' friendships | are longest found,
POEM	If fair their fates may be.
POEM	
POEM	42. To his friend a man | a friend shall prove,
POEM	And gifts with gifts requite;
POEM	But men shall mocking | with mockery answer,
POEM	And fraud with falsehood meet.
POEM	
POEM	43. To his friend a man | a friend shall prove,
POEM	To him and the friend of his friend;
POEM	But never a man | shall friendship make
POEM	With one of his foeman's friends.
POEM	
POEM	44. If a friend thou hast | whom thou fully wilt trust,
POEM	And good from him wouldst get,
POEM	Thy thoughts with his mingle, | and gifts shalt thou make,
POEM	And fare to find him oft.
POEM	
POEM	45. If another thou hast | whom thou hardly wilt trust,
POEM	Yet good from him wouldst get,
POEM	Thou shalt speak him fair, | but falsely think,
POEM	And fraud with falsehood requite.
POEM	
POEM	46. So is it with him | whom thou hardly wilt trust,
POEM	And whose mind thou mayst not know;
POEM	Laugh with him mayst thou, | but speak not thy mind,
POEM	Like gifts to his shalt thou give.
POEM	
POEM	47. Young was I once, | and wandered alone,
POEM	And nought of the road I knew;
POEM	Rich did I feel | when a comrade I found,
POEM	For man is man's delight.
POEM	
POEM	48. The lives of the brave | and noble are best,
POEM	Sorrows they seldom feed;
POEM	But the coward fear | of all things feels,
POEM	And not gladly the niggard gives.
POEM	
POEM	49. My garments once | in a field I gave
POEM	To a pair of carven poles;
POEM	Heroes they seemed | when clothes they had,
POEM	But the naked man is nought.
POEM	
POEM	50. On the hillside drear | the fir-tree dies,
POEM	All bootless its needles and bark;
POEM	It is like a man | whom no one loves,--
POEM	Why should his life be long?
POEM	
POEM	51. Hotter than fire | between false friends
POEM	Does friendship five days burn;
POEM	When the sixth day comes | the fire cools,
POEM	And ended is all the love.
POEM	
POEM	52. No great thing needs | a man to give,
POEM	Oft little will purchase praise;
POEM	With half a loaf | and a half-filled cup
POEM	A friend full fast I made.
POEM	
POEM	53. A little sand | has a little sea,
POEM	And small are the minds of men;
POEM	Though all men are not | equal in wisdom,
POEM	Yet half-wise only are all.
POEM	
POEM	54. A measure of wisdom | each man shall have,
POEM	But never too much let him know;
POEM	The fairest lives | do those men live
POEM	Whose wisdom wide has grown.
POEM	
POEM	55. A measure of wisdom | each man shall have,
POEM	But never too much let him know;
POEM	For the wise man's heart | is seldom happy,
POEM	If wisdom too great he has won.
POEM	
POEM	56. A measure of wisdom | each man shall have,
POEM	But never too much let him know;
POEM	Let no man the fate | before him see,
POEM	For so is he freest from sorrow.
POEM	
POEM	57. A brand from a brand | is kindled and burned,
POEM	And fire from fire begotten;
POEM	And man by his speech | is known to men,
POEM	And the stupid by their stillness.
POEM	
POEM	58. He must early go forth | who fain the blood
POEM	Or the goods of another would get;
POEM	The wolf that lies idle | shall win little meat,
POEM	Or the sleeping man success.
POEM	
POEM	59. He must early go forth | whose workers are few,
POEM	Himself his work to seek;
POEM	Much remains undone | for the morning-sleeper,
POEM	For the swift is wealth half won.
POEM	
POEM	60. Of seasoned shingles | and strips of bark
POEM	For the thatch let one know his need,
POEM	And how much of wood | he must have for a month,
POEM	Or in half a year he will use.
POEM	
POEM	61. Washed and fed | to the council fare,
POEM	But care not too much for thy clothes;
POEM	Let none be ashamed | of his shoes and hose,
POEM	Less still of the steed he rides,
POEM	(Though poor be the horse he has.)
POEM	
POEM	62. When the eagle comes | to the ancient sea,
POEM	He snaps and hangs his head;
POEM	So is a man | in the midst of a throng,
POEM	Who few to speak for him finds.
POEM	
POEM	63. To question and answer | must all be ready
POEM	Who wish to be known as wise;
POEM	Tell one thy thoughts, | but beware of two,--
POEM	All know what is known to three.
POEM	
POEM	64. The man who is prudent | a measured use
POEM	Of the might he has will make;
POEM	He finds when among | the brave he fares
POEM	That the boldest he may not be.
POEM	
POEM	65. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	Oft for the words | that to others one speaks
POEM	He will get but an evil gift.
POEM	
POEM	66. Too early to many | a meeting I came,
POEM	And some too late have I sought;
POEM	The beer was all drunk, | or not yet brewed;
POEM	Little the loathed man finds.
POEM	
POEM	67. To their homes men would bid | me hither and yon,
POEM	If at meal-time I needed no meat,
POEM	Or would hang two hams | in my true friend's house,
POEM	Where only one I had eaten.
POEM	
POEM	68. Fire for men | is the fairest gift,
POEM	And power to see the sun;
POEM	Health as well, | if a man may have it,
POEM	And a life not stained with sin.
POEM	
POEM	69. All wretched is no man, | though never so sick;
POEM	Some from their sons have joy,
POEM	Some win it from kinsmen, | and some from their wealth,
POEM	And some from worthy works.
POEM	
POEM	70. It is better to live | than to lie a corpse,
POEM	The live man catches the cow;
POEM	I saw flames rise | for the rich man's pyre,
POEM	And before his door he lay dead.
POEM	
POEM	71. The lame rides a horse, | the handless is herdsman,
POEM	The deaf in battle is bold;
POEM	The blind man is better | than one that is burned,
POEM	No good can come of a corpse.
POEM	
POEM	72. A son is better, | though late he be born,
POEM	And his father to death have fared;
POEM	Memory-stones | seldom stand by the road
POEM	Save when kinsman honors his kin.
POEM	
POEM	73. Two make a battle, | the tongue slays the head;
POEM	In each furry coat | a fist I look for.
POEM	
POEM	74. He welcomes the night | whose fare is enough,
POEM	(Short are the yards of a ship,)
POEM	Uneasy are autumn nights;
POEM	Full oft does the weather | change in a week,
POEM	And more in a month's time.
POEM	
POEM	75. A man knows not, | if nothing he knows,
POEM	That gold oft apes begets;
POEM	One man is wealthy | and one is poor,
POEM	Yet scorn for him none should know.
POEM	
POEM	76. Among Fitjung's sons | saw I well-stocked folds,--
POEM	Now bear they the beggar's staff;
POEM	Wealth is as swift | as a winking eye,
POEM	Of friends the falsest it is.
POEM	
POEM	77. Cattle die, | and kinsmen die,
POEM	And so one dies one's self;
POEM	But a noble name | will never die,
POEM	If good renown one gets.
POEM	
POEM	78. Cattle die, | and kinsmen die,
POEM	And so one dies one's self;
POEM	One thing now | that never dies,
POEM	The fame of a dead man's deeds.
POEM	
POEM	79. Certain is that | which is sought from runes,
POEM	That the gods so great have made,
POEM	And the Master-Poet painted;
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	. . . . . of the race of gods:
POEM	Silence is safest and best.
POEM	
POEM	80. An unwise man, | if a maiden's love
POEM	Or wealth he chances to win,
POEM	His pride will wax, but his wisdom never,
POEM	Straight forward he fares in conceit.
POEM	* * *
POEM	
POEM	81. Give praise to the day at evening, | to a woman on her pyre,
POEM	To a weapon which is tried, | to a maid at wed lock,
POEM	To ice when it is crossed, | to ale that is drunk.
POEM	
POEM	82. When the gale blows hew wood, | in fair winds seek the water;
POEM	Sport with maidens at dusk, | for day's eyes are many;
POEM	From the ship seek swiftness, | from the shield protection,
POEM	Cuts from the sword, | from the maiden kisses.
POEM	
POEM	83. By the fire drink ale, | over ice go on skates;
POEM	Buy a steed that is lean, | and a sword when tarnished,
POEM	The horse at home fatten, | the hound in thy dwelling.
POEM	* * *
POEM	
POEM	84. A man shall trust not | the oath of a maid,
POEM	Nor the word a woman speaks;
POEM	For their hearts on a whirling | wheel were fashioned,
POEM	And fickle their breasts were formed.
POEM	
POEM	85. In a breaking bow | or a burning flame,
POEM	A ravening wolf | or a croaking raven,
POEM	In a grunting boar, | a tree with roots broken,
POEM	In billowy seas | or a bubbling kettle,
POEM	
POEM	86. In a flying arrow | or falling waters,
POEM	In ice new formed | or the serpent's folds,
POEM	In a bride's bed-speech | or a broken sword,
POEM	In the sport of bears | or in sons of kings,
POEM	
POEM	87. In a calf that is sick | or a stubborn thrall,
POEM	A flattering witch | or a foe new slain.
POEM	
POEM	88. In a brother's slayer, | if thou meet him abroad,
POEM	In a half-burned house, | in a horse full swift--
POEM	One leg is hurt | and the horse is useless--
POEM	None had ever such faith | as to trust in them all.
POEM	
POEM	89. Hope not too surely | for early harvest,
POEM	Nor trust too soon in thy son;
POEM	The field needs good weather, | the son needs wisdom,
POEM	And oft is either denied.
POEM	* * *
POEM	
POEM	90. The love of women | fickle of will
POEM	Is like starting o'er ice | with a steed unshod,
POEM	A two-year-old restive | and little tamed,
POEM	Or steering a rudderless | ship in a storm,
POEM	Or, lame, hunting reindeer | on slippery rocks.
POEM	* * *
POEM	
POEM	91. Clear now will I speak, | for I know them both,
POEM	Men false to women are found;
POEM	When fairest we speak, | then falsest we think,
POEM	Against wisdom we work with deceit.
POEM	
POEM	92. Soft words shall he speak | and wealth shall he offer
POEM	Who longs for a maiden's love,
POEM	And the beauty praise | of the maiden bright;
POEM	He wins whose wooing is best.
POEM	
POEM	93. Fault for loving | let no man find
POEM	Ever with any other;
POEM	Oft the wise are fettered, | where fools go free,
POEM	By beauty that breeds desire.
POEM	
POEM	94. Fault with another | let no man find
POEM	For what touches many a man;
POEM	Wise men oft | into witless fools
POEM	Are made by mighty love.
POEM	
POEM	95. The head alone knows | what dwells near the heart,
POEM	A man knows his mind alone;
POEM	No sickness is worse | to one who is wise
POEM	Than to lack the longed-for joy.
POEM	
POEM	96. This found I myself, | when I sat in the reeds,
POEM	And long my love awaited;
POEM	As my life the maiden | wise I loved,
POEM	Yet her I never had.
POEM	
POEM	97. Billing's daughter | I found on her bed,
POEM	In slumber bright as the sun;
POEM	Empty appeared | an earl's estate
POEM	Without that form so fair.
POEM	
POEM	98. "Othin, again | at evening come,
POEM	If a woman thou wouldst win;
POEM	Evil it were | if others than we
POEM	Should know of such a sin."
POEM	
POEM	99. Away I hastened, | hoping for joy,
POEM	And careless of counsel wise;
POEM	Well I believed | that soon I should win
POEM	Measureless joy with the maid.
POEM	
POEM	100. So came I next | when night it was,
POEM	The warriors all were awake;
POEM	With burning lights | and waving brands
POEM	I learned my luckess way.
POEM	
POEM	101. At morning then, | when once more I came,
POEM	And all were sleeping still,
POEM	A dog found | in the fair one's place,
POEM	Bound there upon her bed.
POEM	
POEM	102. Many fair maids, | if a man but tries them,
POEM	False to a lover are found;
POEM	That did I learn | when I longed to gain
POEM	With wiles the maiden wise;
POEM	Foul scorn was my meed | from the crafty maid,
POEM	And nought from the woman I won.
POEM	* * *
POEM	
POEM	103. Though glad at home, | and merry with guests,
POEM	A man shall be wary and wise;
POEM	The sage and shrewd, | wide wisdom seeking,
POEM	Must see that his speech be fair;
POEM	A fool is he named | who nought can say,
POEM	For such is the way of the witless.
POEM	
POEM	104. I found the old giant, | now back have I fared,
POEM	Small gain from silence I got;
POEM	Full many a word, | my will to get,
POEM	I spoke in Suttung's hall.
POEM	
POEM	105. The mouth of Rati | made room for my passage,
POEM	And space in the stone he gnawed;
POEM	Above and below | the giants' paths lay,
POEM	So rashly I risked my head.
POEM	
POEM	106. Gunnloth gave | on a golden stool
POEM	A drink of the marvelous mead;
POEM	A harsh reward | did I let her have
POEM	For her heroic heart,
POEM	And her spirit troubled sore.
POEM	
POEM	107. The well-earned beauty | well I enjoyed,
POEM	Little the wise man lacks;
POEM	So Othrörir now | has up been brought
POEM	To the midst of the men of earth.
POEM	
POEM	108. Hardly, methinks, | would I home have come,
POEM	And left the giants' land,
POEM	Had not Gunnloth helped me, | the maiden good,
POEM	Whose arms about me had been.
POEM	
POEM	109. The day that followed, | the frost-giants came,
POEM	Some word of Hor to win,
POEM	(And into the hall of Hor;)
POEM	Of Bolverk they asked, | were he back midst the gods,
POEM	Or had Suttung slain him there?
POEM	
POEM	110. On his ring swore Othin | the oath, methinks;
POEM	Who now his troth shall trust?
POEM	Suttung's betrayal | he sought with drink,
POEM	And Gunnloth to grief he left.
POEM	* * *
POEM	
POEM	111. It is time to chant | from the chanter's stool;
POEM	By the wells of Urth I was,
POEM	I saw and was silent, | I saw and thought,
POEM	And heard the speech of Hor.
POEM	(Of runes heard I words, | nor were counsels wanting,
POEM	At the hall of Hor,
POEM	In the hall of Hor;
POEM	Such was the speech I heard.)
POEM	
POEM	112. I rede thee, Loddfafnir! | and hear thou my rede,---
POEM	Profit thou hast if thou hearest,
POEM	Great thy gain if thou learnest:
POEM	Rise not at night, | save if news thou seekest,
POEM	Or fain to the outhouse wouldst fare.
POEM	
POEM	113. I rede thee, Loddfafnir! | and hear thou my rede,--
POEM	Profit thou hast if thou hearest,
POEM	Great thy gain if thou learnest:
POEM	Beware of sleep | on a witch's bosom,
POEM	Nor let her limbs ensnare thee.
POEM	
POEM	114. Such is her might | that thou hast no mind
POEM	For the council or meeting of men;
POEM	Meat thou hatest, | joy thou hast not,
POEM	And sadly to slumber thou farest.
POEM	
POEM	115. I rede thee, Loddfafnir! | and hear thou my rede,--
POEM	Profit thou hast if thou hearest,
POEM	Great thy gain if thou learnest:
POEM	Seek never to win | the wife of another,
POEM	Or long for her secret love.
POEM	
POEM	116. I rede thee, Loddfafnir! | and hear thou my rede,--
POEM	Profit thou hast if thou hearest,
POEM	Great thy gain if thou learnest:
POEM	If o'er mountains or gulfs | thou fain wouldst go,
POEM	Look well to thy food for the way.
POEM	
POEM	117. I rede thee, Loddfafnir! | and hear thou my rede,--
POEM	Profit thou hast if thou hearest,
POEM	Great thy gain if thou learnest:
POEM	An evil man | thou must not let
POEM	Bring aught of ill to thee;
POEM	For an evil man | will never make
POEM	Reward for a worthy thought.
POEM	
POEM	118. I saw a man | who was wounded sore
POEM	By an evil woman's word;
POEM	A lying tongue | his death-blow launched,
POEM	And no word of truth there was.
POEM	
POEM	119. I rede thee, Loddfafnir! | and hear thou my rede,--
POEM	Profit thou hast if thou hearest,
POEM	Great thy gain if thou learnest:
POEM	If a friend thou hast | whom thou fully wilt trust,
POEM	Then fare to find him oft;
POEM	For brambles grow | and waving grass
POEM	On the rarely trodden road.
POEM	
POEM	120. I rede thee, Loddfafnir! | and hear thou my rede,--
POEM	Profit thou hast if thou hearest,
POEM	Great thy gain if thou learnest:
POEM	A good man find | to hold in friendship,
POEM	And give heed to his healing charms.
POEM	
POEM	121. I rede thee, Loddfafnir! | and hear thou my rede,-
POEM	Profit thou hast if thou hearest,
POEM	Great thy gain if thou learnest:
POEM	Be never the first | to break with thy friend
POEM	The bond that holds you both;
POEM	Care eats the heart | if thou canst not speak
POEM	To another all thy thought.
POEM	
POEM	122. I rede thee, Loddfafnir! | and hear thou my rede,--
POEM	Profit thou hast if thou hearest,
POEM	Great thy gain if thou learnest:
POEM	Exchange of words | with a witless ape
POEM	Thou must not ever make.
POEM	
POEM	123. For never thou mayst | from an evil man
POEM	A good requital get;
POEM	But a good man oft | the greatest love
POEM	Through words of praise will win thee.
POEM	
POEM	124. Mingled is love | when a man can speak
POEM	To another all his thought;
POEM	Nought is so bad | as false to be,
POEM	No friend speaks only fair.
POEM	
POEM	125. I rede thee, Loddfafnir! | and hear thou my rede,--
POEM	Profit thou hast if thou hearest,
POEM	Great thy gain if thou learnest:
POEM	With a worse man speak not | three words in dispute,
POEM	Ill fares the better oft
POEM	When the worse man wields a sword.
POEM	
POEM	126. I rede thee, Loddfafnir! | and hear thou my rede,-
POEM	Profit thou hast if thou hearest,
POEM	Great thy gain if thou learnest:
POEM	A shoemaker be, | or a maker of shafts,
POEM	For only thy single self;
POEM	If the shoe is ill made, | or the shaft prove false,
POEM	Then evil of thee men think.
POEM	
POEM	127. I rede thee, Loddfafnir! | and hear thou my rede,--
POEM	Profit thou hast if thou hearest,
POEM	Great thy gain if thou learnest:
POEM	If evil thou knowest, | as evil proclaim it,
POEM	And make no friendship with foes.
POEM	
POEM	128. I rede thee, Loddfafnir! | and hear thou my rede,--
POEM	Profit thou hast if thou hearest,
POEM	Great thy gain if thou learnest:
POEM	In evil never | joy shalt thou know,
POEM	But glad the good shall make thee.
POEM	
POEM	129. I rede thee, Loddfafnir! | and hear thou my rede,--
POEM	Profit thou hast if thou hearest,
POEM	Great thy gain if thou learnest:
POEM	Look not up | when the battle is on,--
POEM	(Like madmen the sons | of men become,--)
POEM	Lest men bewitch thy wits.
POEM	
POEM	130. I rede thee, Loddfafnir! | and hear thou my rede,-
POEM	Profit thou hast if thou hearest,
POEM	Great thy gain if thou learnest:
POEM	If thou fain wouldst win | a woman's love,
POEM	And gladness get from her,
POEM	Fair be thy promise | and well fulfilled;
POEM	None loathes what good he gets.
POEM	
POEM	131. I rede thee, Loddfafnir! | and hear thou my rede,-
POEM	Profit thou hast if thou hearest,
POEM	Great thy gain if thou learnest:
POEM	I bid thee be wary, | but be not fearful;
POEM	(Beware most with ale or another's wife,
POEM	And third beware | lest a thief outwit thee.)
POEM	
POEM	132. I rede thee, Loddfafnir! | and hear thou my rede,-
POEM	Profit thou hast if thou hearest,
POEM	Great thy gain if thou learnest:
POEM	Scorn or mocking | ne'er shalt thou make
POEM	Of a guest or a journey-goer.
POEM	
POEM	133. Oft scarcely he knows | who sits in the house
POEM	What kind is the man who comes;
POEM	None so good is found | that faults he has not,
POEM	Nor so wicked that nought he is worth.
POEM	
POEM	134. I rede thee, Loddfafnir! | and hear thou my rede,--
POEM	Profit thou hast if thou hearest,
POEM	Great thy gain if thou learnest:
POEM	Scorn not ever | the gray-haired singer,
POEM	Oft do the old speak good;
POEM	(Oft from shrivelled skin | come skillful counsels,
POEM	Though it hang with the hides,
POEM	And flap with the pelts,
POEM	And is blown with the bellies.)
POEM	
POEM	135. I rede thee, Loddfafnir! | and hear thou my rede,--
POEM	Profit thou hast if thou hearest,
POEM	Great thy gain if thou learnest:
POEM	Curse not thy guest, | nor show him thy gate,
POEM	Deal well with a man in want.
POEM	
POEM	136. Strong is the beam | that raised must be
POEM	To give an entrance to all;
POEM	Give it a ring, | or grim will be
POEM	The wish it would work on thee.
POEM	
POEM	137. I rede thee, Loddfafnir! | and hear thou my rede,--
POEM	Profit thou hast if thou hearest,
POEM	Great thy gain if thou learnest:
POEM	When ale thou drinkest) | seek might of earth,
POEM	(For earth cures drink, | and fire cures ills,
POEM	The oak cures tightness, | the ear cures magic,
POEM	Rye cures rupture, | the moon cures rage,
POEM	Grass cures the scab, | and runes the sword-cut;)
POEM	The field absorbs the flood.
POEM	
POEM	138. Now are Hor's words | spoken in the hall,
POEM	Kind for the kindred of men,
POEM	Cursed for the kindred of giants:
POEM	Hail to the speaker, | and to him who learns!
POEM	Profit be his who has them!
POEM	Hail to them who hearken!
POEM	* * *
POEM	
POEM	139. I ween that I hung | on the windy tree,
POEM	Hung there for nights full nine;
POEM	With the spear I was wounded, | and offered I was
POEM	To Othin, myself to myself,
POEM	On the tree that none | may ever know
POEM	What root beneath it runs.
POEM	
POEM	140. None made me happy | with loaf or horn,
POEM	And there below I looked;
POEM	I took up the runes, | shrieking I took them,
POEM	And forthwith back I fell.
POEM	
POEM	141. Nine mighty songs | I got from the son
POEM	Of Bolthorn, Bestla's father;
POEM	And a drink I got | of the goodly mead
POEM	Poured out from Othrörir.
POEM	
POEM	142. Then began I to thrive, | and wisdom to get,
POEM	I grew and well I was;
POEM	Each word led me on | to another word,
POEM	Each deed to another deed.
POEM	
POEM	143. Runes shalt thou find, | and fateful signs,
POEM	That the king of singers colored,
POEM	And the mighty gods have made;
POEM	Full strong the signs, | full mighty the signs
POEM	That the ruler of gods doth write.
POEM	
POEM	144. Othin for the gods, | Dain for the elves,
POEM	And Dvalin for the dwarfs,
POEM	Alsvith for giants | and all mankind,
POEM	And some myself I wrote.
POEM	
POEM	145. Knowest how one shall write, | knowest how one shall rede?
POEM	Knowest how one shall tint, | knowest how one makes trial?
POEM	Knowest how one shall ask, | knowest how one shall offer?
POEM	Knowest how one shall send, | knowest how one shall sacrifice?
POEM	
POEM	146. Better no prayer | than too big an offering,
POEM	By thy getting measure thy gift;
POEM	Better is none | than too big a sacrifice,
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	So Thund of old wrote | ere man's race began,
POEM	Where he rose on high | when home he came.
POEM	* * *
POEM	
POEM	147. The songs I know | that king's wives know not,
POEM	Nor men that are sons of men;
POEM	The first is called help, | and help it can bring thee
POEM	In sorrow and pain and sickness.
POEM	
POEM	148. A second I know, | that men shall need
POEM	Who leechcraft long to use;
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	
POEM	149. A third I know, | if great is my need
POEM	Of fetters to hold my foe;
POEM	Blunt do I make | mine enemy's blade,
POEM	Nor bites his sword or staff.
POEM	
POEM	150. A fourth I know, | if men shall fasten
POEM	Bonds on my bended legs;
POEM	So great is the charm | that forth I may go,
POEM	The fetters spring from my feet,
POEM	Broken the bonds from my hands.
POEM	
POEM	152. A fifth I know, | if I see from afar
POEM	An arrow fly 'gainst the folk;
POEM	It flies not so swift | that I stop it not,
POEM	If ever my eyes behold it.
POEM	
POEM	152. A sixth I know, | if harm one seeks
POEM	With a sapling's roots to send me;
POEM	The hero himself | who wreaks his hate
POEM	Shall taste the ill ere I.
POEM	
POEM	153. A seventh I know, | if I see in flames
POEM	The hall o'er my comrades' heads;
POEM	It burns not so wide | that I will not quench it,
POEM	I know that song to sing.
POEM	
POEM	154. An eighth I know, | that is to all
POEM	Of greatest good to learn;
POEM	When hatred grows | among heroes' sons,
POEM	I soon can set it right.
POEM	
POEM	155. A ninth I know, | if need there comes
POEM	To shelter my ship on the flood;
POEM	The wind I calm | upon the waves,
POEM	And the sea I put to sleep.
POEM	
POEM	156. A tenth I know, | what time I see
POEM	House-riders flying on high;
POEM	So can I work | that wildly they go,
POEM	Showing their true shapes,
POEM	Hence to their own homes.
POEM	
POEM	157. An eleventh I know, | if needs I must lead
POEM	To the fight my long-loved friends;
POEM	I sing in the shields, | and in strength they go
POEM	Whole to the field of fight,
POEM	Whole from the field of fight,
POEM	And whole they come thence home.
POEM	
POEM	158. A twelfth I know, | if high on a tree
POEM	I see a hanged man swing;
POEM	So do I write | and color the runes
POEM	That forth he fares,
POEM	And to me talks.
POEM	
POEM	159. A thirteenth I know, | if a thane full young
POEM	With water I sprinkle well;
POEM	He shall not fall, | though he fares mid the host,
POEM	Nor sink beneath the swords.
POEM	
POEM	160. A fourteenth I know, | if fain I would name
POEM	To men the mighty gods;
POEM	All know I well | of the gods and elves,
POEM	Few be the fools know this.
POEM	
POEM	161. A fifteenth I know, | that before the doors
POEM	Of Delling sang Thjothrörir the dwarf;
POEM	Might he sang for the gods, | and glory for elves,
POEM	And wisdom for Hroptatyr wise.
POEM	
POEM	162. A sixteenth I know, | if I seek delight
POEM	To win from a maiden wise;
POEM	The mind I turn | of the white-armed maid,
POEM	And thus change all her thoughts.
POEM	
POEM	163. A seventeenth I know, | so that seldom shall go
POEM	A maiden young from me;
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	
POEM	164. Long these songs | thou shalt, Loddfafnir,
POEM	Seek in vain to sing;
POEM	Yet good it were | if thou mightest get them,
POEM	Well, if thou wouldst them learn,
POEM	Help, if thou hadst them.
POEM	
POEM	165. An eighteenth I know, | that ne'er will I tell
POEM	To maiden or wife of man,--
POEM	The best is what none | but one's self doth know,
POEM	So comes the end of the songs,--
POEM	Save only to her | in whose arms I lie,
POEM	Or who else my sister is.
TITLE	VAFTHRUTHNISMOL
SUBTITLE	The Ballad of Vafthruthnir
POEM	1. "Counsel me, Frigg, for I long to fare,
POEM	And Vafthruthnir fain would find;
POEM	fit wisdom old with the giant wise
POEM	Myself would I seek to match."
POEM	
POEM	Frigg spake:
POEM	2. "Heerfather here at home would I keep,
POEM	Where the gods together dwell;
POEM	Amid all the giants an equal in might
POEM	To Vafthruthnir know I none."
POEM	
POEM	Othin spake:
POEM	3. "Much have I fared, much have I found.
POEM	Much have I got from the gods;
POEM	And fain would I know how Vafthruthnir now
POEM	Lives in his lofty hall."
POEM	
POEM	Frigg spake:
POEM	4. "Safe mayst thou go, safe come again,
POEM	And safe be the way thou wendest!
POEM	Father of men, let thy mind be keen
POEM	When speech with the giant thou seekest."
POEM	
POEM	5. The wisdom then of the giant wise
POEM	Forth did he fare to try;
POEM	He found the hall | of the father of Im,
POEM	And in forthwith went Ygg.
POEM	
POEM	Othin spake:
POEM	6. "Vafthruthnir, hail! | to thy hall am I come,
POEM	For thyself I fain would see;
POEM	And first would I ask | if wise thou art,
POEM	Or, giant, all wisdom hast won."
POEM	
POEM	Vafthruthnir spake:
POEM	7. "Who is the man | that speaks to me,
POEM	Here in my lofty hall?
POEM	Forth from our dwelling | thou never shalt fare,
POEM	Unless wiser than I thou art."
POEM	
POEM	Othin spake:
POEM	8. "Gagnrath they call me, | and thirsty I come
POEM	From a journey hard to thy hall;
POEM	Welcome I look for, | for long have I fared,
POEM	And gentle greeting, giant."
POEM	
POEM	Vafthruthnir spake:
POEM	9. "Why standest thou there | on the floor whilst thou speakest?
POEM	A seat shalt thou have in my hall;
POEM	Then soon shall we know | whose knowledge is more,
POEM	The guest's or the sage's gray."
POEM	
POEM	Othin spake:
POEM	10. "If a poor man reaches | the home of the rich,
POEM	Let him wisely speak or be still;
POEM	For to him who speaks | with the hard of heart
POEM	Will chattering ever work ill."
POEM	
POEM	Vafthruthnir spake:
POEM	11. "Speak forth now, Gagnrath, | if there from the floor
POEM	Thou wouldst thy wisdom make known:
POEM	What name has the steed | that each morn anew
POEM	The day for mankind doth draw?"
POEM	
POEM	Othin spake:
POEM	12. "Skinfaxi is he, | the steed who for men
POEM	The glittering day doth draw;
POEM	The best of horses | to heroes he seems,
POEM	And brightly his mane doth burn."
POEM	
POEM	Vafthruthnir spake:
POEM	13. "Speak forth now, Gagnrath, | if there from the floor
POEM	Thou wouldst thy wisdom make known:
POEM	What name has the steed | that from East anew
POEM	Brings night for the noble gods?"
POEM	
POEM	Othin spake:
POEM	14. "Hrimfaxi name they | the steed that anew
POEM	Brings night for the noble gods;
POEM	Each morning foam | from his bit there falls,
POEM	And thence come the dews in the dales."
POEM	
POEM	Vafthruthnir spake:
POEM	15. "Speak forth now, Gagnrath, | if there from the floor
POEM	Thou wouldst thy wisdom make known:
POEM	What name has the river | that 'twixt the realms
POEM	Of the gods and the giants goes?"
POEM	Othin spoke:
POEM	
POEM	16. "Ifing is the river | that 'twixt the realms
POEM	Of the gods and the giants goes;
POEM	For all time ever | open it flows,
POEM	No ice on the river there is."
POEM	
POEM	Vafthruthnir spake:
POEM	17. "Speak forth now, Gagnrath, | if there from the floor
POEM	Thou wouldst thy wisdom make known:
POEM	What name has the field | where in fight shall meet
POEM	Surt and the gracious gods?"
POEM	
POEM	Othin spake:
POEM	18. "Vigrith is the field | where in fight shall meet
POEM	Surt and the gracious gods;
POEM	A hundred miles | each way does it measure.
POEM	And so are its boundaries set."
POEM	
POEM	Vafthruthnir spake:
POEM	19. "Wise art thou, guest! | To my bench shalt thou go,
POEM	In our seats let us speak together;
POEM	Here in the hall | our heads, O guest,
POEM	Shall we wager our wisdom upon."
POEM	
POEM	Othin spake:
POEM	20. "First answer me well, | if thy wisdom avails,
POEM	And thou knowest it, Vafthruthnir, now:
POEM	In earliest time | whence came the earth,
POEM	Or the sky, thou giant sage?"
POEM	
POEM	Vafthruthnir spake:
POEM	21. "Out of Ymir's flesh | was fashioned the earth,
POEM	And the mountains were made of his bones;
POEM	The sky from the frost-cold | giant's skull,
POEM	And the ocean out of his blood."
POEM	
POEM	Othin spake:
POEM	22. "Next answer me well, | if thy wisdom avails,
POEM	And thou knowest it, Vafthruthnir, now:
POEM	Whence came the moon, | o'er the world of men
POEM	That fares, and the flaming sun?"
POEM	
POEM	Vafthruthnir spake:
POEM	23. "Mundilferi is he | who begat the moon,
POEM	And fathered the flaming sun;
POEM	The round of heaven | each day they run,
POEM	To tell the time for men."
POEM	
POEM	Othin spake:
POEM	24. "Third answer me well, | if wise thou art called,
POEM	If thou knowest it, Vafthruthnir, now:
POEM	Whence came the day, | o'er mankind that fares,
POEM	Or night with the narrowing moon?"
POEM	
POEM	Vafthruthnir spake:
POEM	25. "The father of day | is Delling called,
POEM	And the night was begotten by Nor;
POEM	Full moon and old | by the gods were fashioned,
POEM	To tell the time for men."
POEM	
POEM	Othin spake:
POEM	26. "Fourth answer me well, | if wise thou art called,
POEM	If thou knowest it, Vafthruthnir, now:
POEM	Whence did winter come, | or the summer warm,
POEM	First with the gracious gods?"
POEM	
POEM	Vafthruthnir spake:
POEM	27. "Vindsval he was | who was winter's father,
POEM	And Svosuth summer begat;"
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	
POEM	Othin spake:
POEM	28. "Fifth answer me well, | if wise thou art called,
POEM	If thou knowest it, Vafthruthnir, now:
POEM	What giant first | was fashioned of old,
POEM	And the eldest of Ymir's kin?"
POEM	
POEM	Vafthruthnir spake:
POEM	29. "Winters unmeasured | ere earth was made
POEM	Was the birth of Bergelmir;
POEM	Thruthgelmir's son | was the giant strong,
POEM	And Aurgelmir's grandson of old."
POEM	
POEM	Othin spake:
POEM	30. "Sixth answer me well, | if wise thou art called,
POEM	If thou knowest it, Vafthruthnir, now:
POEM	Whence did Aurgelmir come | with the giants' kin,
POEM	Long since, thou giant sage?"
POEM	
POEM	Vafthruthnir spake:
POEM	31. "Down from Elivagar | did venom drop,
POEM	And waxed till a giant it was;
POEM	And thence arose | our giants' race,
POEM	And thus so fierce are we found."
POEM	
POEM	Othin spake:
POEM	32. "Seventh answer me well, | if wise thou art called,
POEM	If thou knowest it, Vafthruthnir, now:
POEM	How begat he children, | the giant grim,
POEM	Who never a giantess knew?"
POEM	
POEM	Vafthruthnir spake:
POEM	33. "They say 'neath the arms | of the giant of ice
POEM	Grew man-child and maid together;
POEM	And foot with foot | did the wise one fashion
POEM	A son that six heads bore."
POEM	
POEM	Othin spake:
POEM	34. "Eighth answer me well, | if wise thou art called,
POEM	If thou knowest it, Vafthruthnir, now:
POEM	What farthest back | dost thou bear in mind?
POEM	For wide is thy wisdom, giant!"
POEM	
POEM	Vafthruthnir spake:
POEM	35. "Winters unmeasured | ere earth was made
POEM	Was the birth of Bergelmir;
POEM	This first knew I well, | when the giant wise
POEM	In a boat of old was borne."
POEM	
POEM	Othin spake:
POEM	36. "Ninth answer me well, | if wise thou art called
POEM	If thou knowest it, Vafthruthnir, now:
POEM	Whence comes the wind | that fares o'er the waves
POEM	Yet never itself is seen?"
POEM	
POEM	Vafthruthnir spake:
POEM	37. "In an eagle's guise | at the end of heaven
POEM	Hræsvelg sits, they say;
POEM	And from his wings | does the wind come forth
POEM	To move o'er the world of men."
POEM	
POEM	Othin spake:
POEM	38. "Tenth answer me now, | if thou knowest all
POEM	The fate that is fixed for the gods:
POEM	Whence came up Njorth | to the kin of the gods,--
POEM	(Rich in temples | and shrines he rules,--)
POEM	Though of gods he was never begot?"
POEM	
POEM	Vafthruthnir spake:
POEM	39. "In the home of the Wanes | did the wise ones create him,
POEM	And gave him as pledge to the gods;
POEM	At the fall of the world | shall he fare once more
POEM	Home to the Wanes so wise."
POEM	
POEM	Othin spake:
POEM	40. "Eleventh answer me well, | . . . . . . .
POEM	. . . . . . . . . . . .
POEM	What men . . . . . . | in . . . . . . home
POEM	Each day to fight go forth?"
POEM	
POEM	Vafthruthnir spake:
POEM	41. "The heroes all | in Othin's hall
POEM	Each day to fight go forth;
POEM	They fell each other, | and fare from the fight
POEM	All healed full soon to sit."
POEM	
POEM	Othin spake:
POEM	42. "Twelfth answer me now | how all thou knowest
POEM	Of the fate that is fixed for the gods;
POEM	Of the runes of the gods | and the giants' race
POEM	The truth indeed dost thou tell,
POEM	(And wide is thy wisdom, giant!)"
POEM	
POEM	Vafthruthnir spake:
POEM	43. "Of the runes of the gods | and the giants' race
POEM	The truth indeed can I tell,
POEM	(For to every world have I won;)
POEM	To nine worlds came I, | to Niflhel beneath,
POEM	The home where dead men dwell."
POEM	
POEM	Othin spake:
POEM	44. "Much have I fared, | much have I found,
POEM	Much have I got of the gods:
POEM	What shall live of mankind | when at last there comes
POEM	The mighty winter to men?"
POEM	
POEM	Vafthruthnir spake:
POEM	45. "In Hoddmimir's wood | shall hide themselves
POEM	Lif and Lifthrasir then;
POEM	The morning dews | for meat shall they have,
POEM	Such food shall men then find."
POEM	
POEM	Othin spake:
POEM	46. "Much have I fared, | much have I found,
POEM	Much have I got of the gods:
POEM	Whence comes the sun | to the smooth sky back,
POEM	When Fenrir has snatched it forth?"
POEM	
POEM	Vafthruthnir spake:
POEM	47. "A daughter bright | Alfrothul bears
POEM	Ere Fenrir snatches her forth;
POEM	Her mother's paths | shall the maiden tread
POEM	When the gods to death have gone."
POEM	
POEM	Othin spake:
POEM	48. "Much have I fared, | much have I found,
POEM	Much have I got of the gods:
POEM	What maidens are they, | so wise of mind.
POEM	That forth o'er the sea shall fare?"
POEM	
POEM	Vafthruthnir spake:
POEM	49. "O'er Mogthrasir's hill | shall the maidens pass,
POEM	And three are their throngs that come;
POEM	They all shall protect | the dwellers on earth,
POEM	Though they come of the giants' kin."
POEM	
POEM	Othin spake:
POEM	50. "Much have I fared, | much have I found,
POEM	Much have I got of the gods:
POEM	Who then shall rule | the realm of the gods,
POEM	When the fires of Surt have sunk?"
POEM	
POEM	Vafthruthnir spake:
POEM	51. "In the gods' home Vithar | and Vali shall dwell,
POEM	When the fires of Surt have sunk;
POEM	Mothi and Magni | shall Mjollnir have
POEM	When Vingnir falls in fight."
POEM	
POEM	Othin spake:
POEM	52. "Much have I fared, | much have I found,
POEM	Much have I got of the gods:
POEM	What shall bring the doom | of death to Othin,
POEM	When the gods to destruction go?"
POEM	
POEM	Vafthruthnir spake:
POEM	53. "The wolf shall fell | the father of men,
POEM	And this shall Vithar avenge;
POEM	The terrible jaws | shall he tear apart,
POEM	And so the wolf shall he slay."
POEM	
POEM	Othin spake:
POEM	54. "Much have I fared, | much have I found,
POEM	Much have I got from the gods:
POEM	What spake Othin himself | in the ears of his son,
POEM	Ere in the bale-fire he burned?"
POEM	
POEM	Vafthruthnir spake:
POEM	55. "No man can tell | what in olden time
POEM	Thou spak'st in the ears of thy son;
POEM	With fated mouth | the fall of the gods
POEM	And mine olden tales have I told;
POEM	With Othin in knowledge | now have I striven,
POEM	And ever the wiser thou art."
TITLE	GRIMNISMOL
SUBTITLE	The Ballad of Grimnir
POEM	1. Hot art thou, fire! | too fierce by far;
POEM	Get ye now gone, ye flames!
POEM	The mantle is burnt, | though I bear it aloft,
POEM	And the fire scorches the fur.
POEM	
POEM	2. 'Twixt the fires now | eight nights have I sat,
POEM	And no man brought meat to me,
POEM	Save Agnar alone, | and alone shall rule
POEM	Geirröth's son o'er the Goths.
POEM	
POEM	3. Hail to thee, Agnar! | for hailed thou art
POEM	By the voice of Veratyr;
POEM	For a single drink | shalt thou never receive
POEM	A greater gift as reward.
POEM	
POEM	4. The land is holy | that lies hard by
POEM	The gods and the elves together;
POEM	And Thor shall ever | in Thruthheim dwell,
POEM	Till the gods to destruction go.
POEM	
POEM	5. Ydalir call they | the place where Ull
POEM	A hall for himself hath set;
POEM	And Alfheim the gods | to Freyr once gave
POEM	As a tooth-gift in ancient times.
POEM	
POEM	6. A third home is there, | with silver thatched
POEM	By the hands of the gracious gods:
POEM	Valaskjolf is it, | in days of old
POEM	Set by a god for himself.
POEM	
POEM	7. Sökkvabekk is the fourth, | where cool waves flow,
POEM	And amid their murmur it stands;
POEM	There daily do Othin | and Saga drink
POEM	In gladness from cups of gold.
POEM	
POEM	8. The fifth is Glathsheim, | and gold-bright there
POEM	Stands Valhall stretching wide;
POEM	And there does Othin | each day choose
POEM	The men who have fallen in fight.
POEM	
POEM	9. Easy is it to know | for him who to Othin
POEM	Comes and beholds the hall;
POEM	Its rafters are spears, | with shields is it roofed,
POEM	On its benches are breastplates strewn.
POEM	
POEM	10. Easy is it to know | for him who to Othin
POEM	Comes and beholds the hall;
POEM	There hangs a wolf | by the western door,
POEM	And o'er it an eagle hovers.
POEM	
POEM	11. The sixth is Thrymheim, | where Thjazi dwelt,
POEM	The giant of marvelous might;
POEM	Now Skathi abides, | the god's fair bride,
POEM	In the home that her father had.
POEM	
POEM	12. The seventh is Breithablik; | Baldr has there
POEM	For himself a dwelling set,
POEM	In the land I know | that lies so fair,
POEM	And from evil fate is free.
POEM	
POEM	13. Himinbjorg is the eighth, | and Heimdall there
POEM	O'er men holds sway, it is said;
POEM	In his well-built house | does the warder of heaven
POEM	The good mead gladly drink.
POEM	
POEM	14. The ninth is Folkvang, | where Freyja decrees
POEM	Who shall have seats in the hall;
POEM	The half of the dead | each day does she choose,
POEM	And half does Othin have.
POEM	
POEM	15. The tenth is Glitnir; | its pillars are gold,
POEM	And its roof with silver is set;
POEM	There most of his days | does Forseti dwell,
POEM	And sets all strife at end.
POEM	
POEM	16. The eleventh is Noatun; | there has Njorth
POEM	For himself a dwelling set;
POEM	The sinless ruler | of men there sits
POEM	In his temple timbered high.
POEM	
POEM	17. Filled with growing trees | and high-standing grass
POEM	Is Vithi, Vithar's land;
POEM	But there did the son | from his steed leap down,
POEM	When his father he fain would avenge.
POEM	
POEM	18. In Eldhrimnir | Andhrimnir cooks
POEM	Sæhrimnir's seething flesh,--
POEM	The best of food, | but few men know
POEM	On what fare the warriors feast.
POEM	
POEM	19. Freki and Geri | does Heerfather feed,
POEM	The far-famed fighter of old:
POEM	But on wine alone | does the weapon-decked god,
POEM	Othin, forever live.
POEM	
POEM	20. O'er Mithgarth Hugin | and Munin both
POEM	Each day set forth to fly;
POEM	For Hugin I fear | lest he come not home,
POEM	But for Munin my care is more.
POEM	
POEM	21. Loud roars Thund, | and Thjothvitnir's fish
POEM	joyously fares in the flood;
POEM	Hard does it seem | to the host of the slain
POEM	To wade the torrent wild.
POEM	
POEM	22. There Valgrind stands, | the sacred gate,
POEM	And behind are the holy doors;
POEM	Old is the gate, | but few there are
POEM	Who can tell how it tightly is locked.
POEM	
POEM	23. Five hundred doors | and forty there are,
POEM	I ween, in Valhall's walls;
POEM	Eight hundred fighters | through one door fare
POEM	When to war with the wolf they go.
POEM	
POEM	24. Five hundred rooms | and forty there are
POEM	I ween, in Bilskirnir built;
POEM	Of all the homes | whose roofs I beheld,
POEM	My son's the greatest meseemed.
POEM	
POEM	25. Heithrun is the goat | who stands by Heerfather's hall,
POEM	And the branches of Lærath she bites;
POEM	The pitcher she fills | with the fair, clear mead,
POEM	Ne'er fails the foaming drink.
POEM	
POEM	26. Eikthyrnir is the hart | who stands by Heerfather's hall
POEM	And the branches of Lærath he bites;
POEM	From his horns a stream | into Hvergelmir drops,
POEM	Thence all the rivers run.
POEM	
POEM	27. Sith and Vith, | Sækin and Ækin,
POEM	Svol and Fimbulthul, | Gunnthro, and Fjorm,
POEM	Rin and Rinnandi,
POEM	Gipul and Gopul, | Gomul and Geirvimul,
POEM	That flow through the fields of the gods;
POEM	Thyn and Vin, | Thol and Hol,
POEM	Groth and Gunnthorin.
POEM	
POEM	28. Vino is one, | Vegsvin another,
POEM	And Thjothnuma a third;
POEM	Nyt and Not, | Non and Hron,
POEM	Slith and Hrith, | Sylg and Ylg,
POEM	Vith and Von, | Vond and Strond,
POEM	Gjol and Leipt, | that go among men,
POEM	And hence they fall to Hel.
POEM	
POEM	29. Kormt and Ormt | and the Kerlaugs twain
POEM	Shall Thor each day wade through,
POEM	(When dooms to give | he forth shall go
POEM	To the ash-tree Yggdrasil;)
POEM	For heaven's bridge | burns all in flame,
POEM	And the sacred waters seethe.
POEM	
POEM	30. Glath and Gyllir, | Gler and Skeithbrimir,
POEM	Silfrintopp and Sinir,
POEM	Gisl and Falhofnir, | Golltopp and Lettfeti,
POEM	On these steeds the gods shall go
POEM	When dooms to give | each day they ride
POEM	To the ash-tree Yggdrasil.
POEM	
POEM	31. Three roots there are | that three ways run
POEM	Neath the ash-tree Yggdrasil;
POEM	Neath the first lives Hel, | 'neath the second the frost-giants,
POEM	Neath the last are the lands of men.
POEM	
POEM	32. Ratatosk is the squirrel | who there shall run
POEM	On the ash-tree Yggdrasil;
POEM	From above the words | of the eagle he bears,
POEM	And tells them to Nithhogg beneath.
POEM	
POEM	33. Four harts there are, | that the highest twigs
POEM	Nibble with necks bent back;
POEM	Dain and Dvalin, | . . . . . .
POEM	Duneyr and Dyrathror.
POEM	
POEM	34. More serpents there are | beneath the ash
POEM	Than an unwise ape would think;
POEM	Goin and Moin, | Grafvitnir's sons,
POEM	Grabak and Grafvolluth,
POEM	Ofnir and Svafnir | shall ever, methinks,
POEM	Gnaw at the twigs of the tree.
POEM	
POEM	35. Yggdrasil's ash | great evil suffers,
POEM	Far more than men do know;
POEM	The hart bites its top, | its trunk is rotting,
POEM	And Nithhogg gnaws beneath.
POEM	
POEM	36. Hrist and Mist | bring the horn at my will,
POEM	Skeggjold and Skogul;
POEM	Hild and Thruth, | Hlok and Herfjotur,
POEM	Gol and Geironul,
POEM	Randgrith and Rathgrith | and Reginleif
POEM	Beer to the warriors bring.
POEM	
POEM	37. Arvak and Alsvith | up shall drag
POEM	Weary the weight of the sun;
POEM	But an iron cool | have the kindly gods
POEM	Of yore set under their yokes.
POEM	
POEM	38. In front of the sun | does Svalin stand,
POEM	The shield for the shining god;
POEM	Mountains and sea | would be set in flames
POEM	If it fell from before the sun.
POEM	
POEM	39. Skoll is the wolf | that to Ironwood
POEM	Follows the glittering god,
POEM	And the son of Hrothvitnir, | Hati, awaits
POEM	The burning bride of heaven.
POEM	
POEM	40. Out of Ymir's flesh | was fashioned the earth,
POEM	And the ocean out of his blood;
POEM	Of his bones the hills, | of his hair the trees,
POEM	Of his skull the heavens high.
POEM	
POEM	41. Mithgarth the gods | from his eyebrows made,
POEM	And set for the sons of men;
POEM	And out of his brain | the baleful clouds
POEM	They made to move on high.
POEM	
POEM	42. His the favor of Ull | and of all the gods
POEM	Who first in the flames will reach;
POEM	For the house can be seen | by the sons of the gods
POEM	If the kettle aside were cast.
POEM	
POEM	43. In days of old | did Ivaldi's sons
POEM	Skithblathnir fashion fair,
POEM	The best of ships | for the bright god Freyr,
POEM	The noble son of Njorth.
POEM	
POEM	44. The best of trees | must Yggdrasil be,
POEM	Skithblathnir best of boats;
POEM	Of all the gods | is Othin the greatest,
POEM	And Sleipnir the best of steeds;
POEM	Bifrost of bridges, | Bragi of skalds,
POEM	Hobrok of hawks, | and Garm of hounds.
POEM	
POEM	45. To the race of the gods | my face have I raised,
POEM	And the wished-for aid have I waked;
POEM	For to all the gods | has the message gone
POEM	That sit in Ægir's seats,
POEM	That drink within Ægir's doors.
POEM	
POEM	46. Grim is my name, | Gangleri am 1,
POEM	Herjan and Hjalmberi,
POEM	Thekk and Thrithi, | Thuth and Uth,
POEM	Helblindi and Hor;
POEM	
POEM	47. Sath and Svipal | and Sanngetal,
POEM	Herteit and Hnikar,
POEM	Bileyg, Baleyg, | Bolverk, Fjolnir,
POEM	Grim and Grimnir, | Glapsvith, Fjolsvith.
POEM	
POEM	48. Sithhott, Sithskegg, | Sigfather, Hnikuth,
POEM	Allfather, Valfather, | Atrith, Farmatyr:
POEM	A single name | have I never had
POEM	Since first among men I fared.
POEM	
POEM	49. Grimnir they call me | in Geirröth's hall,
POEM	With Asmund Jalk am I;
POEM	Kjalar I was | when I went in a sledge,
POEM	At the council Thror am I called,
POEM	As Vithur I fare to the fight;
POEM	Oski, Biflindi, | Jafnhor and Omi,
POEM	Gondlir and Harbarth midst gods.
POEM	So. I deceived the giant | Sokkmimir old
POEM	As Svithur and Svithrir of yore;
POEM	Of Mithvitnir's son | the slayer I was
POEM	When the famed one found his doom.
POEM	
POEM	51. Drunk art thou, Geirröth, | too much didst thou drink,
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	Much hast thou lost, | for help no more
POEM	From me or my heroes thou hast.
POEM	
POEM	52. Small heed didst thou take | to all that I told,
POEM	And false were the words of thy friends;
POEM	For now the sword | of my friend I see,
POEM	That waits all wet with blood.
POEM	
POEM	53. Thy sword-pierced body | shall Ygg have soon,
POEM	For thy life is ended at last;
POEM	The maids are hostile; | now Othin behold!
POEM	Now come to me if thou canst!
POEM	
POEM	54. Now am I Othin, | Ygg was I once,
POEM	Ere that did they call me Thund;
POEM	Vak and Skilfing, | Vofuth and Hroptatyr,
POEM	Gaut and Jalk midst the gods;
POEM	Ofnir and Svafnir, | and all, methinks,
POEM	Are names for none but me.
POEM	King Geirröth sat and had his sword on his knee, half drawn from its sheath. But when he heard that
POEM	Othin was come thither, then he rose up and sought to take Othin from the fire. The sword slipped from
POEM	his hand, and fell with the hilt down. The king stumbled and fell forward, and the sword pierced him
POEM	through, and slew him. Then Othin vanished, but Agnar long ruled there as king.
TITLE	SKIRNISMOL
SUBTITLE	The Ballad of Skirnir
POEM	1. "Go now, Skirnir! | and seek to gain
POEM	Speech from my son;
POEM	And answer to win, | for whom the wise one
POEM	Is mightily moved."
POEM	
POEM	Skirnir spake:
POEM	2. "Ill words do I now | await from thy son,
POEM	If I seek to get speech with him,
POEM	And answer to win, | for whom the wise one
POEM	Is mightily moved."
POEM	
POEM	Skirnir spake:
POEM	3. "Speak prithee, Freyr, | foremost of the gods,
POEM	For now I fain would know;
POEM	Why sittest thou here | in the wide halls,
POEM	Days long, my prince, alone?"
POEM	
POEM	Freyr spake:
POEM	4. "How shall I tell thee, | thou hero young,
POEM	Of all my grief so great?
POEM	Though every day | the elfbeam dawns,
POEM	It lights my longing never."
POEM	
POEM	Skirnir spake:
POEM	5. "Thy longings, methinks, | are not so large
POEM	That thou mayst not tell them to me;
POEM	Since in days of yore | we were young together,
POEM	We two might each other trust."
POEM	
POEM	Freyr spake:
POEM	6. "From Gymir's house | I beheld go forth
POEM	A maiden dear to me;
POEM	Her arms glittered, | and from their gleam
POEM	Shone all the sea and sky.
POEM	
POEM	7. "To me more dear | than in days of old
POEM	Was ever maiden to man;
POEM	But no one of gods | or elves will grant
POEM	That we both together should be."
POEM	
POEM	Skirnir spake:
POEM	8. "Then give me the horse | that goes through the dark
POEM	And magic flickering flames;
POEM	And the sword as well | that fights of itself
POEM	Against the giants grim."
POEM	
POEM	Freyr spake:
POEM	9. "The horse will I give thee | that goes through the dark
POEM	And magic flickering flames,
POEM	And the sword as well | that will fight of itself
POEM	If a worthy hero wields it."
POEM	
POEM	Skirnir spake to the horse:
POEM	10. "Dark is it without, | and I deem it time
POEM	To fare through the wild fells,
POEM	(To fare through the giants' fastness;)
POEM	We shall both come back, | or us both together
POEM	The terrible giant will take."
POEM	Skirnir rode into Jotunheim to Gymir's house. There were fierce dogs bound before the gate of the fence
POEM	which was around Gerth's hall. He rode to where a herdsman sat on a hill, and said:
POEM	
POEM	11. "Tell me, herdsman, | sitting on the hill,
POEM	And watching all the ways,
POEM	How may I win | a word with the maid
POEM	Past the hounds of Gymir here?"
POEM	
POEM	The herdsman spake:
POEM	12. "Art thou doomed to die | or already dead,
POEM	Thou horseman that ridest hither?
POEM	Barred from speech | shalt thou ever be
POEM	With Gymir's daughter good."
POEM	
POEM	Skirnir spake:
POEM	13. "Boldness is better | than plaints can be
POEM	For him whose feet must fare;
POEM	To a destined day has mine age been doomed,
POEM	And my life's span thereto laid."
POEM	
POEM	Gerth spake:
POEM	14. "What noise is that which now so loud
POEM	I hear within our house?
POEM	The ground shakes, and the home of Gymir
POEM	Around me trembles too."
POEM	
POEM	The Serving-Maid spake:
POEM	15. "One stands without who has leapt from his steed,
POEM	And lets his horse loose to graze;"
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	
POEM	Gerth spake:
POEM	16. "Bid the man come in, and drink good mead
POEM	Here within our hall;
POEM	Though this I fear, that there without
POEM	My brother's slayer stands.
POEM	
POEM	17. "Art thou of the elves | or the offspring of gods,
POEM	Or of the wise Wanes?
POEM	How camst thou alone | through the leaping flame
POEM	Thus to behold our home?"
POEM	
POEM	Skirnir spake:
POEM	18. "I am not of the elves, | nor the offspring of gods,
POEM	Nor of the wise Wanes;
POEM	Though I came alone | through the leaping flame
POEM	Thus to behold thy home.
POEM	
POEM	19. "Eleven apples, | all of gold,
POEM	Here will I give thee, Gerth,
POEM	To buy thy troth | that Freyr shall be
POEM	Deemed to be dearest to you."
POEM	
POEM	Gerth spake:
POEM	20. "I will not take | at any man's wish
POEM	These eleven apples ever;
POEM	Nor shall Freyr and I | one dwelling find
POEM	So long as we two live."
POEM	
POEM	Skirnir spake:
POEM	21. "Then do I bring thee | the ring that was burned
POEM	Of old with Othin's son;
POEM	From it do eight | of like weight fall
POEM	On every ninth night."
POEM	
POEM	Gerth spake:
POEM	22. "The ring I wish not, | though burned it was
POEM	Of old with Othin's son;
POEM	In Gymir's home | is no lack of gold
POEM	In the wealth my father wields."
POEM	
POEM	Skirnir spake:
POEM	23. "Seest thou, maiden, | this keen, bright sword
POEM	That I hold here in my hand?
POEM	Thy head from thy neck | shall I straightway hew,
POEM	If thou wilt not do my will."
POEM	
POEM	Gerth spake:
POEM	24. "For no man's sake | will I ever suffer
POEM	To be thus moved by might;
POEM	But gladly, methinks, | will Gymir seek
POEM	To fight if he finds thee here."
POEM	
POEM	Skirnir spake:
POEM	25. "Seest thou, maiden, | this keen, bright sword
POEM	That I hold here in my hand?
POEM	Before its blade the | old giant bends,--
POEM	Thy father is doomed to die.
POEM	
POEM	26. "I strike thee, maid, | with my magic staff,
POEM	To tame thee to work my will;
POEM	There shalt thou go | where never again
POEM	The sons of men shall see thee.
POEM	
POEM	27. "On the eagle's hill | shalt thou ever sit,
POEM	And gaze on the gates of Hel;
POEM	More loathsome to thee | than the light-hued snake
POEM	To men, shall thy meat become.
POEM	
POEM	28. "Fearful to see, | if thou comest forth,
POEM	Hrimnir will stand and stare,
POEM	(Men will marvel at thee;)
POEM	More famed shalt thou grow | than the watchman of the gods!
POEM	Peer forth, then, from thy prison,
POEM	
POEM	29. "Rage and longing, | fetters and wrath,
POEM	Tears and torment are thine;
POEM	Where thou sittest down | my doom is on thee
POEM	Of heavy heart
POEM	And double dole.
POEM	
POEM	30. "In the giants' home | shall vile things harm thee
POEM	Each day with evil deeds;
POEM	Grief shalt thou get | instead of gladness,
POEM	And sorrow to suffer with tears.
POEM	
POEM	31. "With three-headed giants | thou shalt dwell ever,
POEM	Or never know a husband;
POEM	(Let longing grip thee, | let wasting waste thee,--)
POEM	Be like to the thistle | that in the loft
POEM	Was cast and there was crushed.
POEM	
POEM	32. "I go to the wood, | and to the wet forest,
POEM	To win a magic wand;
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	I won a magic wand.
POEM	
POEM	33. "Othin grows angry, | angered is the best of the gods,
POEM	Freyr shall be thy foe,
POEM	Most evil maid, | who the magic wrath
POEM	Of gods hast got for thyself.
POEM	
POEM	34. "Give heed, frost-rulers, | hear it, giants.
POEM	Sons of Suttung,
POEM	And gods, ye too,
POEM	How I forbid | and how I ban
POEM	The meeting of men with the maid,
POEM	(The joy of men with the maid.)
POEM	
POEM	35. "Hrimgrimnir is he, | the giant who shall have thee
POEM	In the depth by the doors of Hel;
POEM	To the frost-giants' halls | each day shalt thou fare,
POEM	Crawling and craving in vain,
POEM	(Crawling and having no hope.)
POEM	
POEM	36. "Base wretches there | by the root of the tree
POEM	Will hold for thee horns of filth;
POEM	A fairer drink | shalt thou never find,
POEM	Maid, to meet thy wish,
POEM	(Maid, to meet my wish.)
POEM	
POEM	37. "I write thee a charm | and three runes therewith,
POEM	Longing and madness and lust;
POEM	But what I have writ | I may yet unwrite
POEM	If I find a need therefor."
POEM	
POEM	Gerth spake:
POEM	38. "Find welcome rather, | and with it take
POEM	The frost-cup filled with mead;
POEM	Though I did not believe | that I should so love
POEM	Ever one of the Wanes."
POEM	
POEM	Skirnir spake:
POEM	39. "My tidings all | must I truly learn
POEM	Ere homeward hence I ride:
POEM	How soon thou wilt | with the mighty son
POEM	Of Njorth a meeting make."
POEM	
POEM	Gerth spake:
POEM	40. Barri there is, | which we both know well,
POEM	A forest fair and still;
POEM	And nine nights hence | to the son of Njorth
POEM	Will Gerth there grant delight."
POEM	Then Skirnir rode home. Freyr stood without, and spoke to him, and asked for tidings:
POEM	
POEM	41. "Tell me, Skimir, | ere thou take off the saddle,
POEM	Or farest forward a step:
POEM	What hast thou done | in the giants' dwelling
POEM	To make glad thee or me?"
POEM	Skirnir spoke:
POEM	
POEM	42. "Barri there is, | which we both know well,
POEM	A forest fair and still;
POEM	And nine nights hence | to the son of Njorth
POEM	Will Gerth there grant delight."
POEM	
POEM	Freyr spake:
POEM	43. "Long is one night, | longer are two;
POEM	How then shall I bear three?
POEM	Often to me | has a month seemed less
POEM	Than now half a night of desire."
TITLE	HARBARTHSLJOTH
SUBTITLE	The Poem of Harbarth
POEM	1. "Who is the fellow yonder, | on the farther shore of the sound?"
POEM	
POEM	The ferryman spake:
POEM	2. "What kind of a peasant is yon, | that calls o'er the bay?"
POEM	
POEM	Thor spake:
POEM	3. "Ferry me over the sound; | I will feed thee therefor in the morning;
POEM	A basket I have on my back, | and food therein, none better;
POEM	At leisure I ate, | ere the house I left,
POEM	Of herrings and porridge, | so plenty I had."
POEM	
POEM	The ferryman spake:
POEM	4. "Of thy morning feats art thou proud, | but the future thou knowest not wholly;
POEM	Doleful thine home-coming is: | thy mother, me thinks, is dead."
POEM	
POEM	Thor spake:
POEM	5. "Now hast thou said | what to each must seem
POEM	The mightiest grief, | that my mother is dead."
POEM	
POEM	The ferryman spake:
POEM	6. "Three good dwellings, | methinks, thou hast not;
POEM	Barefoot thou standest, | and wearest a beggar's dress;
POEM	Not even hose dost thou have."
POEM	
POEM	Thor spake:
POEM	7. "Steer thou hither the boat; | the landing here shall I show thee;
POEM	But whose the craft | that thou keepest on the shore?"
POEM	
POEM	The ferryman spake:
POEM	8. "Hildolf is he | who bade me have it,
POEM	A hero wise; | his home is at Rathsey's sound.
POEM	He bade me no robbers to steer, | nor stealers of steeds,
POEM	But worthy men, | and those whom well do I know.
POEM	Say now thy name, | if over the sound thou wilt fare."
POEM	
POEM	Thor spake:
POEM	9. "My name indeed shall I tell, | though in danger I am,
POEM	And all my race; | I am Othin's son,
POEM	Meili's brother, | and Magni's father,
POEM	The strong one of the gods; | with Thor now speech canst thou get.
POEM	And now would I know | what name thou hast."
POEM	
POEM	The ferryman spake:
POEM	10. "Harbarth am I, | and seldom I hide my name."
POEM	
POEM	Thor spake:
POEM	11. "Why shouldst thou hide thy name, | if quarrel thou hast not?"
POEM	
POEM	Harbarth spake:
POEM	12. "And though I had a quarrel, | from such as thou art
POEM	Yet none the less | my life would I guard,
POEM	Unless I be doomed to die."
POEM	
POEM	Thor spake:
POEM	13. "Great trouble, methinks, | would it be to come to thee,
POEM	To wade the waters across, | and wet my middle;
POEM	Weakling, well shall I pay | thy mocking words,
POEM	if across the sound I come."
POEM	
POEM	Harbarth spake:
POEM	14. "Here shall I stand | and await thee here;
POEM	Thou hast found since Hrungnir died | no fiercer man."
POEM	
POEM	Thor spake:
POEM	15. "Fain art thou to tell | how with Hrungnir I fought,
POEM	The haughty giant, | whose head of stone was made;
POEM	And yet I felled him, | and stretched him before me.
POEM	What, Harbarth, didst thou the while?"
POEM	
POEM	Harbarth spake:
POEM	16. "Five full winters | with Fjolvar was I,
POEM	And dwelt in the isle | that is Algrön called;
POEM	There could we fight, | and fell the slain,
POEM	Much could we seek, | and maids could master."
POEM	
POEM	Thor spake:
POEM	17. "How won ye success with your women?"
POEM	
POEM	Harbarth spake:
POEM	18. "Lively women we had, | if they wise for us were;
POEM	Wise were the women we had, | if they kind for us were;
