POEM	And Slagfith south | to seek for Swan-White;
POEM	Völund alone | in Ulfdalir lay,
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	
POEM	8. Red gold he fashioned | with fairest gems,
POEM	And rings he strung | on ropes of bast;
POEM	So for his wife | he waited long,
POEM	If the fair one home | might come to him.
POEM	
POEM	9. This Nithuth learned, | the lord of the Njars,
POEM	That Völund alone | in Ulfdalir lay;
POEM	By night went his men, | their mail-coats were studded,
POEM	Their shields in the waning | moonlight shone.
POEM	
POEM	10. From their saddles the gable | wall they sought,
POEM	And in they went | at the end of the hall;
POEM	Rings they saw there | on ropes of bast,
POEM	Seven hundred | the hero had.
POEM	
POEM	11. Off they took them, | but all they left
POEM	Save one alone | which they bore away.
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	
POEM	12. Völund home | from his hunting came,
POEM	From a weary way, | the weather-wise bowman;
POEM	A brown bear's flesh | would he roast with fire;
POEM	Soon the wood so dry | was burning well,
POEM	(The wind-dried wood | that Völund's was).
POEM	
POEM	13. On the bearskin he rested, | and counted the rings,
POEM	The master of elves, | but one he missed;
POEM	That Hlothver's daughter | had it he thought,
POEM	And the all-wise maid | had come once more.
POEM	
POEM	14. So long he sat | that he fell asleep,
POEM	His waking empty | of gladness was;
POEM	Heavy chains | he saw on his hands,
POEM	And fetters bound | his feet together.
POEM	
POEM	Völund spake:
POEM	15. "What men are they | who thus have laid
POEM	Ropes of bast | to bind me now?"
POEM	Then Nithuth called, | the lord of the Njars:
POEM	How gottest thou, Völund, | greatest of elves,
POEM	These treasures of ours | in Ulfdalir?
POEM	
POEM	Völund spake:
POEM	16. "The gold was not | on Grani's way,
POEM	Far, methinks, is our realm | from the hills of the Rhine;
POEM	I mind me that treasures | more we had
POEM	When happy together | at home we were."
POEM	
POEM	17. Without stood the wife | of Nithuth wise,
POEM	And in she came | from the end of the hall;
POEM	On the floor she stood, | and softly spoke:
POEM	Not kind does he look | who comes from the wood.
POEM	King Nithuth gave to his daughter Bothvild the gold ring that he had taken from the bast rope in Völund's
POEM	
POEM	house, and he himself wore the sword that Völund had had. The queen spake:
POEM	18. "The glow of his eyes | is like gleaming snakes,
POEM	His teeth he gnashes | if now is shown
POEM	The sword, or Bothvild's | ring he sees;
POEM	Let them straightway cut | his sinews of strength,
POEM	And set him then | in Sævarstath."
POEM	So was it done: the sinews in his knee-joints were cut, and he was set in an island which was near the
POEM	mainland, and was called Sævarstath. There he smithied for the king all kinds of precious things. No man
POEM	
POEM	dared to go to him, save only the king himself. Völund spake:
POEM	19. "At Nithuth's girdle | gleams the sword
POEM	That I sharpened keen | with cunningest craft,
POEM	(And hardened the steel | with highest skill;)
POEM	The bright blade far | forever is borne,
POEM	(Nor back shall I see it | borne to my smithy;)
POEM	Now Bothvild gets | the golden ring
POEM	(That was once my bride's,-- | ne'er well shall it be.)"
POEM	
POEM	20. He sat, nor slept, | and smote with his hammer,
POEM	Fast for Nithuth | wonders he fashioned;
POEM	Two boys did go | in his door to gaze,
POEM	Nithuth's sons, | into Sævarstath.
POEM	
POEM	21. They came to the chest, | and they craved the keys,
POEM	The evil was open | when in they looked;
POEM	To the boys it seemed | that gems they saw,
POEM	Gold in plenty | and precious stones.
POEM	
POEM	Völund spake:
POEM	22. "Come ye alone, | the next day come,
POEM	Gold to you both | shall then be given;
POEM	Tell not the maids | or the men of the hall,
POEM	To no one say | that me you have sought."
POEM	
POEM	23. . . . . . . . . . .
POEM	Early did brother | to brother call:
POEM	Swift let us go | the rings to see.
POEM	
POEM	24. They came to the chest, | and they craved the keys,
POEM	The evil was open | when in they looked;
POEM	He smote off their heads, | and their feet he hid
POEM	Under the sooty | straps of the bellows.
POEM	
POEM	25. Their skulls, once hid | by their hair, he took,
POEM	Set them in silver | and sent them to Nithuth;
POEM	Gems full fair | from their eyes he fashioned,
POEM	To Nithuth's wife | so wise he gave them.
POEM	
POEM	26. And from the teeth | of the twain he wrought
POEM	A brooch for the breast, | to Bothvild he sent it;
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	
POEM	27. Bothvild then | of her ring did boast,
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	. . . . . | "The ring I have broken,
POEM	I dare not say it | save to thee."
POEM	
POEM	Völund spake:
POEM	28. 'I shall weld the break | in the gold so well
POEM	That fairer than ever | thy father shall find it,
POEM	And better much | thy mother shall think it,
POEM	And thou no worse | than ever it was."
POEM	
POEM	29. Beer he brought, | he was better in cunning,
POEM	Until in her seat | full soon she slept.
POEM	
POEM	Völund spake:
POEM	Now vengeance I have | for all my hurts,
POEM	Save one alone, | on the evil woman.
POEM	
POEM	30. . . . . . . . . . .
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	Quoth Völund: "Would | that well were the sinews
POEM	Maimed in my feet | by Nithuth's men."
POEM	
POEM	31. Laughing Völund | rose aloft,
POEM	Weeping Bothvild | went from the isle,
POEM	For her lover's flight | and her father's wrath.
POEM	
POEM	32. Without stood the wife | of Nithuth wise,
POEM	And in she came | from the end of the hall;
POEM	But he by the wall | in weariness sat:
POEM	Wakest thou, Nithuth, | lord of the Njars?
POEM	
POEM	Nithuth spake:
POEM	33. "Always I wake, | and ever joyless,
POEM	Little I sleep | since my sons were slain;
POEM	Cold is my head, | cold was thy counsel,
POEM	One thing, with Völund | to speak, I wish.
POEM	
POEM	34. . . . . . . . . . .
POEM	Answer me, Völund, | greatest of elves,
POEM	What happed with my boys | that hale once were?
POEM	
POEM	Völund spake:
POEM	35. "First shalt thou all | the oaths now swear,
POEM	By the rail of ship, | and the rim of shield,
POEM	By the shoulder of steed, | and the edge of sword,
POEM	That to Völund's wife | thou wilt work no ill,
POEM	Nor yet my bride | to her death wilt bring,
POEM	Though a wife I should have | that well thou knowest,
POEM	And a child I should have | within thy hall.
POEM	
POEM	36. "Seek the smithy | that thou didst set,
POEM	Thou shalt find the bellows | sprinkled with blood;
POEM	I smote off the heads | of both thy sons,
POEM	And their feet 'neath the sooty | straps I hid.
POEM	
POEM	37. "Their skulls, once hid | by their hair, I took,
POEM	Set them in silver | and sent them to Nithuth;
POEM	Gems full fair | from their eyes I fashioned,
POEM	To Nithuth's wife | so wise I gave them.
POEM	
POEM	38. "And from the teeth | of the twain I wrought
POEM	A brooch for the breast, | to Bothvild I gave it;
POEM	Now big with child | does Bothvild go,
POEM	The only daughter | ye two had ever."
POEM	
POEM	Nithuth spake:
POEM	39. "Never spakest thou word | that worse could hurt me,
POEM	Nor that made me, Völund, | more bitter for vengeance;
POEM	There is no man so high | from thy horse to take thee,
POEM	Or so doughty an archer | as down to shoot thee,
POEM	While high in the clouds | thy course thou takest."
POEM	
POEM	40. Laughing Völund | rose aloft,
POEM	But left in sadness | Nithuth sat.
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	
POEM	41. Then spake Nithuth, | lord of the Njars:
POEM	Rise up, Thakkrath, | best of my thralls,
POEM	Bid Bothvild come, | the bright-browed maid,
POEM	Bedecked so fair, | with her father to speak.
POEM	
POEM	42. . . . . . . . . . .
POEM	Is it true, Bothvild, | that which was told me;
POEM	Once in the isle | with Völund wert thou?
POEM	
POEM	Bothvild spake:
POEM	43. "True is it, Nithuth, | that which was told thee,
POEM	Once in the isle | with Völund was I,
POEM	An hour of lust, | alas it should be!
POEM	Nought was my might | with such a man,
POEM	Nor from his strength | could I save myself."
TITLE	HELGAKVITHA HJORVARTHSSONAR
SUBTITLE	The Lay of Helgi the Son of Hjorvarth
POEM	1. "Sawest thou Sigrlin, | Svafnir's daughter,
POEM	The fairest maid | in her home-land found?
POEM	Though Hjorvath's wives | by men are held
POEM	Goodly to see | in Glasir's wood."
POEM	
POEM	Atli spake:
POEM	2. "Now with Atli, | Ithmund's son,
POEM	Wilt thou say more, | thou bird so wise?"
POEM	
POEM	The bird spake:
POEM	I may if the prince | an offering makes,
POEM	And I have what I will | from the house of the king.
POEM	
POEM	Atli spake:
POEM	3. "Choose not Hjorvarth, | nor sons of his,
POEM	Nor the wives so fair | of the famous chief;
POEM	Ask not the brides | that the prince's are;
POEM	Fair let us deal | in friendly wise."
POEM	
POEM	The bird spake:
POEM	4. "A fane will I ask, | and altars many,
POEM	Gold-horned cattle | the prince shall give me,
POEM	If Sigrlin yet | shall sleep in his arms,
POEM	Or free of will | the hero shall follow."
POEM	This was before Atli went on his journey; but when he came home, and the king asked his tidings, he
POEM	said:
POEM	
POEM	5. "Trouble we had, | but tidings none,
POEM	Our horses failed | in the mountains high,
POEM	The waters of Sæmorn | we needs must wade;
POEM	Svafnir's daughter, | with rings bedecked,
POEM	She whom we sought, | was still denied us."
POEM	The king bade that they should go another time, and he went with them himself, But when they came up
POEM	on the mountain, they saw Svavaland burning and mighty dust-clouds from many steeds. The king rode
POEM	from the mountain forward into the land, and made a night's stay hard by a stream. Atli kept watch and
POEM	went over the stream; he found there a house. A great bird sat on the housetop to guard it, but he was
POEM	asleep. Atli hurled his spear at the bird and slew it, and in the house he found Sigrlin the king's daughter
POEM	and Alof the jarl's daughter, and he brought them both thence with him. Jarl Franmar had changed
POEM	himself into the likeness of an eagle, and guarded them from the enemy host by magic. Hrothmar was the
POEM	name of a king, a wooer of Sigrlin; he slew the
POEM	king of Svavaland and had plundered and burned his land. King Hjorvarth took Sigrlin, and Atli took
POEM	Alof.
POEM	(II)
POEM	Hjorvarth and Sigrlin had a son, mighty and of noble stature; he was a silent man, and no name stuck fast
POEM	
POEM	to him. He sat on a hill, and saw nine Valkyries riding; one of them was the fairest of all. She spake:
POEM	6. "Late wilt thou, Helgi, | have hoard of rings,
POEM	Thou battle-tree fierce, | or of shining fields,--
POEM	The eagle screams soon,-- | if never thou speakest,
POEM	Though, hero, hard | thy heart may cry."
POEM	
POEM	Helgi spake:
POEM	7. "What gift shall I have | with Helgi's name,
POEM	Glorious maid, | for the giving is thine?
POEM	All thy words | shall I think on well,
POEM	But I want them not | if I win not thee."
POEM	
POEM	The Valkyrie spake:
POEM	8. "Swords I know lying | in Sigarsholm,
POEM	Fifty there are | save only four;
POEM	One there is | that is best of all,
POEM	The shield-destroyer, | with gold it shines.
POEM	
POEM	9. "In the hilt is fame, | in the haft is courage,
POEM	In the point is fear, | for its owner's foes;
POEM	On the blade there lies | a blood-flecked snake,
POEM	And a serpent's tail | round the flat is twisted."
POEM	Eylimi was the name of a king, whose daughter was Svava; she was a Valkyrie, and rode air and sea. She
POEM	
POEM	gave Helgi this name, and shielded him oft thereafter in battle. Helgi spake:
POEM	10. "Hjorvarth, king, | unwholesome thy counsels,
POEM	Though famed thou art | in leading the folk,
POEM	Letting fire the homes of heroes eat,
POEM	Who evil deed had never done thee.
POEM	
POEM	11. "Yet Hrothmar still the hoard doth hold,
POEM	The wealth that once our kinsmen wielded;
POEM	Full seldom care the king disturbs,
POEM	Heir to dead men he deems himself."
POEM	Hjorvarth answered that he would give Helgi a following if he fain would avenge his mother's father.
POEM	Then Helgi got the sword that Svava had told him of. So he went, and Atli with him, and they slew
POEM	Hrothmar, and they did many great deeds.
POEM	(III)
POEM	He slew the giant Hati, whom he found sitting on a certain mountain. Helgi and Atli lay with their ships
POEM	
POEM	in Hatafjord. Atli kept watch during the first part of the night. Hrimgerth, Hati's daughter, spake:
POEM	12. "Who are the heroes | in Hatafjord?
POEM	The ships are covered with shields;
POEM	Bravely ye look, | and little ye fear,
POEM	The name of the king would I know."
POEM	
POEM	Atli spake:
POEM	13. "Helgi his name, | and never thou mayst
POEM	Harm to the hero bring;
POEM	With iron is fitted | the prince's fleet,
POEM	Nor can witches work us ill."
POEM	
POEM	Hrimgerth spake:
POEM	14. "Who now, thou mighty | man, art thou?
POEM	By what name art thou known to men?
POEM	He trusts thee well, | the prince who wills
POEM	That thou stand at the stem of his ship."
POEM	
POEM	Atli spake:
POEM	15. "Atli am I, | and ill shalt thou find me,
POEM	Great hate for witches I have;
POEM	Oft have I been | in the dripping bows,
POEM	And to dusk-riders death have brought.
POEM	
POEM	16. "Corpse-hungry giantess, | how art thou called?
POEM	Say, witch, who thy father was!
POEM	Nine miles deeper | down mayst thou sink,
POEM	And a tree grow tall on thy bosom."
POEM	
POEM	Hrimgerth spake:
POEM	17. "Hrimgerth am I, | my father was Hati,
POEM	Of giants the most in might;
POEM	Many a woman | he won from her home,
POEM	Ere Helgi hewed him down."
POEM	
POEM	Atli spake:
POEM	18. "Witch, in front | of the ship thou wast,
POEM	And lay before the fjord;
POEM	To Ron wouldst have given | the ruler's men,
POEM	If a spear had not stuck in thy flesh."
POEM	
POEM	Hrimgerth spake:
POEM	19. "Dull art thou, Atli, | thou dreamest, methinks,
POEM	The lids lie over thine eyes;
POEM	By the leader's ships | my mother lay,
POEM	Hlothvarth's sons on the sea I slew.
POEM	
POEM	20. "Thou wouldst neigh, Atli, | but gelded thou art,
POEM	See, Hrimgerth hoists her tail;
POEM	In thy hinder end | is thy heart, methinks,
POEM	Though thy speech is a stallion's cry."
POEM	
POEM	Atli spake:
POEM	21. "A stallion I seem | if thou seekest to try me,
POEM	And I leap to land from the sea;
POEM	I shall smite thee to bits, | if so I will,
POEM	And heavy sinks Hrimgerth's tail."
POEM	
POEM	Hrimgerth spake:
POEM	22. "Go ashore then, Atli, | if sure of thy might,
POEM	Let us come to Varin's cove;
POEM	Straight shall thy rounded | ribs be made
POEM	If thou comest within my claws."
POEM	
POEM	Atli spake:
POEM	23. "I will not go | till the warriors wake,
POEM	Again their chief to guard;
POEM	I should wonder not, | foul witch, if up
POEM	From beneath our keel thou shouldst come."
POEM	
POEM	Hrimgerth spake:
POEM	24. "Awake now, Helgi, | and Hrimgerth requite,
POEM	That Hati to death thou didst hew;
POEM	If a single night | she can sleep by the prince,
POEM	Then requited are all her ills."
POEM	
POEM	Helgi spake:
POEM	25. " 'Tis Lothin shall have thee,-- | thou'rt loathsome to men,--
POEM	His home in Tholley he has;
POEM	Of the wild-dwellers worst | is the giant wise,
POEM	He is meet as a mate for thee."
POEM	
POEM	Hrimgerth spake:
POEM	26. "More thou lovest her | who scanned the harbor,
POEM	Last night among the men;
POEM	(The gold-decked maid | bore magic, rnethinks,
POEM	When the land from the sea she sought,
POEM	And fast she kept your fleet;)
POEM	She alone is to blame | that I may not bring
POEM	Death to the monarch's men."
POEM	
POEM	Helgi spake:
POEM	27. "Hrimgerth, mark, | if thy hurts I requite,
POEM	Tell now the truth to the king;
POEM	Was there one who the ships | of the warrior warded,
POEM	Or did many together go?"
POEM	
POEM	Hrimgerth spake:
POEM	28. "Thrice nine there were, | but one rode first,
POEM	A helmed maid white of hue;
POEM	Their horses quivered, | there came from their manes
POEM	Dew in the dales so deep,
POEM	(Hail on the woods so high,
POEM	Thence men their harvest have,
POEM	But ill was the sight I saw.)"
POEM	
POEM	Atli spake:
POEM	29. "Look eastward, Hrimgerth, | for Helgi has struck thee
POEM	Down with the runes of death;
POEM	Safe in harbor floats | the prince's fleet,
POEM	And safe are the monarch's men."
POEM	
POEM	Helgi spake:
POEM	30. "It is day, Hrimgerth, | for Atli held thee
POEM	Till now thy life thou must lose;
POEM	As a harbor mark | men shall mock at thee,
POEM	Where in stone thou shalt ever stand."
POEM	(IV)
POEM	King Helgi was a mighty warrior. He came to King Eylimi and sought the hand of his daughter, Svava.
POEM	Then Helgi and Svava exchanged vows, and greatly they loved each other. Svava was at home with her
POEM	father, while Helgi was in the field; Svava was still a Valkyrie as before.
POEM	Hethin was at home with his father, King Hjorvarth, in Norway. Hethin was coming home alone from the
POEM	forest one Yule-eve, and found a troll-woman; she rode
POEM	on a wolf, and had snakes in place of a bridle. She asked Hethin for his company. "Nay," said he. She
POEM	said, "Thou shalt pay for this at the king's toast." That evening the great vows were taken; the sacred boar
POEM	was brought in, the men laid their hands thereon, and took their vows at the king's toast. Hethin vowed
POEM	that he would have Svava, Eylimi's daughter, the beloved of his brother Helgi; then such great grief
POEM	seized him that he went forth on wild paths southward over the land, and found Helgi, his brother. Helgi
POEM	said:
POEM	3 1. "Welcome, Hethin! | what hast thou to tell
POEM	Of tidings new | that from Norway come?
POEM	Wherefore didst leave | thy land, O prince,
POEM	And fared alone | to find us here?"
POEM	
POEM	Hethin spake:
POEM	32. "A deed more evil | I have done
POEM	Than, brother mine, | thou e'er canst mend;
POEM	For I have chosen | the child of the king,
POEM	Thy bride, for mine | at the monarch's toast."
POEM	
POEM	Helgi spake:
POEM	33. "Grieve not, Hethin, | for true shall hold
POEM	The words we both | by the beer have sworn;
POEM	To the isle a warrior | wills that I go,
POEM	(There shall I come | the third night hence;)
POEM	And doubtful must be | my coming back,
POEM	(So may all be well, | if fate so wills.)"
POEM	
POEM	Hethin spake:
POEM	34. "Thou saidst once, Helgi, | that Hethin was
POEM	A friend full good, | and gifts didst give him;
POEM	More seemly it were | thy sword to redden,
POEM	Than friendship thus | to thy foe to-give."
POEM	Helgi spoke thus because he foresaw his death, for his following-spirits had met Hethin when he saw the
POEM	woman riding on the wolf. Alf was the name of a king, the son of Hrothmar, who had marked out a
POEM	battle-place with
POEM	
POEM	Helgi at Sigarsvoll after a stay of three nights. Then Helgi spake:
POEM	35. "On a wolf there rode, | when dusk it was,
POEM	A woman who fain | would have him follow;
POEM	Well she knew | that now would fall
POEM	Sigrlin's son | at Sigarsvoll."
POEM	There was a great battle, and there Helgi got a mortal wound.
POEM	
POEM	36. Sigar riding | did Helgi send
POEM	To seek out Eylimi's | only daughter:
POEM	Bid her swiftly | ready to be,
POEM	If her lover | alive she would find.
POEM	
POEM	Sigar spake:
POEM	37. "Hither now | has Helgi sent me,
POEM	With thee, Svava, | thyself to speak;
POEM	The hero said | he fain would see thee
POEM	Ere life the nobly | born should leave."
POEM	
POEM	Svava spake:
POEM	A "What chanced with Helgi, | Hjorvarth's son?
POEM	Hard to me | is harm now come;
POEM	If the sea smote him, | or sword bit him,
POEM	Ill shall I bring | to all his foes."
POEM	
POEM	Sigar spake:
POEM	39. "In the morn he fell | at Frekastein,
POEM	The king who was noblest | beneath the sun;
POEM	Alf has the joy | of victory all,
POEM	Though need therefor | is never his."
POEM	
POEM	Helgi spake:
POEM	40. "Hail to thee, Svava! | thy sorrow rule,
POEM	Our meeting last | in life is this;
POEM	Hard the wounds | of the hero bleed,
POEM	And close to my heart | the sword has come.
POEM	
POEM	41. "I bid thee, Svava,-- | weep not, bride,--
POEM	If thou wilt hearken | to these my words,
POEM	The bed for Hethin | have thou ready,
POEM	And yield thy love | to the hero young."
POEM	
POEM	Svava spake:
POEM	42. "A vow I had | in my dear-loved home,
POEM	When Helgi sought | with rings to have me,
POEM	That not of my will, | if the warrior died,
POEM	Would I fold in my arms | a man unfamed."
POEM	
POEM	Hethin spake:
POEM	43. "Kiss me, Svava, | I come not back,
POEM	Rogheim to see, | or Rothulsfjoll,
POEM	Till vengeance I have | for the son of Hjorvarth,
POEM	The king who was noblest | beneath the sun."
POEM	Of Helgi and Svava it is said that they were born again.
TITLE	HELGAKVITHA HUNDINGSBANA I
SUBTITLE	The First Lay of Helgi Hundingsbane
POEM	1. In olden days, | when eagles screamed,
POEM	And holy streams | from heaven's crags fell,
POEM	Was Helgi then, | the hero-hearted,
POEM	Borghild's son, | in Bralund born.
POEM	
POEM	2. 'Twas night in the dwelling, | and Norns there came,
POEM	Who shaped the life | of the lofty one;
POEM	They bade him most famed | of fighters all
POEM	And best of princes | ever to be.
POEM	
POEM	3. Mightily wove they | the web of fate,
POEM	While Bralund's towns | were trembling all;
POEM	And there the golden | threads they wove,
POEM	And in the moon's hall | fast they made them.
POEM	
POEM	4. East and west | the ends they hid,
POEM	In the middle the hero | should have his land;
POEM	And Neri's kinswoman | northward cast
POEM	A chain, and bade it | firm ever to be.
POEM	
POEM	5. Once sorrow had | the Ylfings' son,
POEM	And grief the bride | who the loved one had borne.
POEM	* * * * * *
POEM	Quoth raven to raven, | on treetop resting,
POEM	Seeking for food, | "There is something I know.
POEM	
POEM	6. "In mail-coat stands | the son of Sigmund,
POEM	A half-day old; | now day is here;
POEM	His eyes flash sharp | as the heroes' are,
POEM	He is friend of the wolves; | full glad are we."
POEM	
POEM	7. The warrior throng | a ruler thought him,
POEM	Good times, they said, | mankind should see;
POEM	The king himself | from battle-press came,
POEM	To give the prince | a leek full proud.
POEM	
POEM	8. Helgi he named him, | and Hringstathir gave him,
POEM	Solfjoll, Snæfjoll, | and Sigarsvoll,
POEM	Hringstoth, Hotun, | and Himinvangar,
POEM	And a blood-snake bedecked | to Sinfjotli's brother.
POEM	
POEM	9. Mighty he grew | in the midst of his friends,
POEM	The fair-born elm, | in fortune's glow;
POEM	To his comrades gold | he gladly gave,
POEM	The hero spared not | the blood-flecked hoard.
POEM	
POEM	10. Short time for war | the chieftain waited,
POEM	When fifteen winters | old he was;
POEM	Hunding he slew, | the hardy wight
POEM	Who long had ruled | o'er lands and men.
POEM	
POEM	11. Of Sigmund's son | then next they sought
POEM	Hoard and rings, | the sons of Hunding;
POEM	They bade the prince | requital pay
POEM	For booty stolen | and father slain.
POEM	
POEM	12. The prince let not | their prayers avail,
POEM	Nor gold for their dead | did the kinsmen get;
POEM	Waiting, he said, | was a mighty storm
POEM	Of lances gray | and Othin's grimness.
POEM	
POEM	13. The warriors forth | to the battle went,
POEM	The field they chose | at Logafjoll;
POEM	Frothi's peace | midst foes they broke,
POEM	Through the isle went hungrily | Vithrir's hounds.
POEM	
POEM	14. The king then sat, | when he had slain
POEM	Eyjolf and Alf, | 'neath the eagle-stone;
POEM	Hjorvarth and Hovarth, | Hunding's sons,
POEM	The kin of the spear-wielder, | all had he killed.
POEM	
POEM	15. Then glittered light | from Logafjoll,
POEM	And from the light | the flashes leaped;
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	
POEM	16. . . . . . . . . . .
POEM	High under helms | on heaven's field;
POEM	Their byrnies all | with blood were red,
POEM	And from their spears | the sparks flew forth.
POEM	
POEM	17. Early then | in wolf-wood asked
POEM	The mighty king | of the southern maid,
POEM	If with the hero | home would she
POEM	Come that night; | the weapons clashed.
POEM	
POEM	18. Down from her horse | sprang Hogni's daughter,--
POEM	
POEM	The shields were still,-- | and spake to the hero:
POEM	Other tasks | are ours, methinks,
POEM	Than drinking beer | with the breaker of rings.
POEM	
POEM	19. My father has pledged | his daughter fair
POEM	As bride to Granmar's | son so grim;
POEM	But, Helgi, I | once Hothbrodd called
POEM	As fine a king | as the son of a cat.
POEM	
POEM	20. "Yet the hero will come | a few nights hence,
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	Unless thou dost bid him | the battle-ground seek,
POEM	Or takest the maid | from the warrior mighty."
POEM	
POEM	Helgi spake:
POEM	21. "Fear him not, | though Isung he felled,
POEM	First must our courage | keen be tried,
POEM	Before unwilling | thou fare with the knave;
POEM	Weapons will clash, | if to death I come not."
POEM	
POEM	22. Messengers sent | the mighty one then,
POEM	By land and by sea, | a host to seek,
POEM	Store of wealth | of the water's gleam,
POEM	And men to summon, | and sons of men.
POEM	
POEM	23. "Bid them straightway | seek the ships,
POEM	And off Brandey | ready to be!"
POEM	There the chief waited | till thither were come
POEM	Men by hundreds | from Hethinsey.
POEM	
POEM	24. Soon off Stafnsnes | stood the ships,
POEM	Fair they glided | and gay with gold;
POEM	
POEM	Then Helgi spake | to Hjorleif asking:
POEM	Hast thou counted | the gallant host?
POEM	
POEM	25. The young king answered | the other then:
POEM	Long were it to tell | from Tronueyr
POEM	The long-stemmed ships | with warriors laden
POEM	That come from without | into Orvasund.
POEM	
POEM	26. . . . . . . . . . .
POEM	There are hundreds twelve | of trusty men,
POEM	But in Hotun lies | the host of the king,
POEM	Greater by half; | I have hope of battle."
POEM	
POEM	27. The ship's-tents soon | the chieftain struck,
POEM	And waked the throng | of warriors all;
POEM	(The heroes the red | of dawn beheld;)
POEM	And on the masts | the gallant men
POEM	Made fast the sails | in Varinsfjord.
POEM	
POEM	28. There was beat of oars | and clash of iron,
POEM	Shield smote shield | as the ships'-folk rowed;
POEM	Swiftly went | the warrior-laden
POEM	Fleet of the ruler | forth from the land.
POEM	
POEM	29. So did it sound, | when together the sisters
POEM	Of Kolga struck | with the keels full long,
POEM	As if cliffs were broken | with beating surf,
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	
POEM	30. Helgi bade higher | hoist the sails,
POEM	Nor did the ships'-folk | shun the waves,
POEM	Though dreadfully | did Ægir's daughters
POEM	Seek the steeds | of the sea to sink.
POEM	
POEM	31. But from above | did Sigrun brave
POEM	Aid the men and | all their faring;
POEM	Mightily came | from the claws of Ron
POEM	The leader's sea-beast | off Gnipalund.
POEM	
POEM	32. At evening there | in Unavagar
POEM	Floated the fleet | bedecked full fair;
POEM	But they who saw | from Svarin's hill,
POEM	Bitter at heart | the host beheld.
POEM	
POEM	33. Then Gothmund asked, | goodly of birth,
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	Who is the monarch | who guides the host,
POEM	And to the land | the warriors leads?
POEM	
POEM	34. Sinfjotli answered, | and up on an oar
POEM	Raised a shield all red | with golden rim;
POEM	A sea-sentry was he, | skilled to speak,
POEM	And in words with princes | well to strive.
POEM	
POEM	35. "Say tonight | when you feed the swine,
POEM	And send your bitches | to seek their swill,
POEM	That out of the East | have the Ylfings come,
POEM	Greedy for battle, | to Gnipalund.
POEM	
POEM	36. "There will Hothbrodd | Helgi find,
POEM	In the midst of the fleet, | and flight he scorns;
POEM	Often has he | the eagles gorged,
POEM	Whilst thou at the quern | wert slave-girls kissing."
POEM	
POEM	Gothmund spake:
POEM	37. "Hero, the ancient | sayings heed,
POEM	And bring not lies | to the nobly born.
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	
POEM	38. "Thou hast eaten | the entrails of wolves,
POEM	And of thy brothers | the slayer been;
POEM	Oft wounds to suck | thy cold mouth sought,
POEM	And loathed in rocky | dens didst lurk."
POEM	
POEM	Sinfjotli spake:
POEM	39. "A witch in Varin's | isle thou wast,
POEM	A woman false, | and lies didst fashion;
POEM	Of the mail-clad heroes | thou wouldst have
POEM	No other, thou saidst, | save Sinfjotli only.
POEM	
POEM	40. "A Valkyrie wast thou, | loathly Witch,
POEM	Evil and base, | in Allfather's home;
POEM	The warriors all | must ever fight,
POEM	Woman subtle, | for sake of thee.
POEM	
POEM	41. ". . . . . . . . . .
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	Nine did we | in Sogunes
POEM	Of wolf-cubs have; | I their father was."
POEM	
POEM	Gothmund spake:
POEM	42. "Thou didst not father | Fenrir's-wolves,
POEM	Though older thou art | than all I know;
POEM	For they gelded thee | in Gnipalund,
POEM	The giant-women | at Thorsnes once.
POEM	
POEM	43. "Under houses the stepson | of Siggeir lay,
POEM	Fain of the wolf's cry | out in the woods;
POEM	Evil came then all | to thy hands,
POEM	When thy brothers' | breasts thou didst redden,
POEM	Fame didst thou win | for foulest deeds.
POEM	
POEM	44. "In Bravoll wast thou | Grani's bride,
POEM	Golden-bitted | and ready to gallop;
POEM	I rode thee many | a mile, and down
POEM	Didst sink, thou giantess, | under the saddle."
POEM	
POEM	Sinfjotli spake:
POEM	45. "A brainless fellow | didst seem to be,
POEM	When once for Gollnir | goats didst milk,
POEM	And another time | when as Imth's daughter
POEM	In rags thou wentest; | wilt longer wrangle?"
POEM	
POEM	Gothmund spake:
POEM	46. "Sooner would I | at Frekastein
POEM	Feed the ravens | with flesh of thine
POEM	Than send your bitches | to seek their swill,
POEM	Or feed the swine; | may the fiends take you!"
POEM	
POEM	Helgi spake:
POEM	47. "Better, Sinfjotli, | thee 'twould beseem
POEM	Battle to give | and eagles to gladden,
POEM	Than vain and empty | words to utter,
POEM	Though ring-breakers oft | in speech do wrangle.
POEM	
POEM	48. "Good I find not | the sons of Granmar,
POEM	But for heroes 'tis seemly | the truth to speak;
POEM	At Moinsheimar | proved the men
POEM	That hearts for the wielding | of swords they had."
POEM	
POEM	49. Mightily then | they made to run
POEM	Sviputh and Sveggjuth | to Solheimar;
POEM	(By dewy dales | and chasms dark,
POEM	Mist's horse shook | where the men went by;)
POEM	The king they found | at his courtyard gate,
POEM	And told him the foeman | fierce was come.
POEM	
POEM	50. Forth stood Hothbrodd, | helmed for battle,
POEM	Watched the riding | of his warriors;
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	Why are the Hniflungs | white with fear?
POEM	
POEM	Gothmund spake:
POEM	51. "Swift keels lie | hard by the land,
POEM	(Mast-ring harts | and mighty yards,
POEM	Wealth of shields | and well-planed oars;)
POEM	The king's fair host, | the Ylfings haughty;
POEM	Fifteen bands | to land have fared,
POEM	But out in Sogn | are seven thousand.
POEM	
POEM	52. "At anchor lying | off Gnipalund
POEM	Are fire-beasts black, | all fitted with gold;
POEM	There wait most | of the foeman's men,
POEM	Nor will Helgi long | the battle delay."
POEM	
POEM	Hothbrodd spake:
POEM	53. "Bid the horses run | to the Reginthing,
POEM	Melnir and Mylnir | to Myrkwood now,
POEM	(And Sporvitnir | to Sparinsheith;)
POEM	Let no man seek | henceforth to sit
POEM	Who the flame of wounds | knows well to wield.
POEM	
POEM	54. "Summon Hogni, | the sons of Hring,
POEM	Atli and Yngvi | and Alf the Old;
POEM	Glad they are | of battle ever;
POEM	Against the Volsungs | let us go."
POEM	
POEM	55. Swift as a storm | there smote together
POEM	The flashing blades | at Frekastein;
POEM	Ever was Helgi, | Hunding's slayer,
POEM	First in the throng | where warriors fought;
POEM	(Fierce in battle, | slow to fly,
POEM	Hard the heart | of the hero was.)
POEM	
POEM	56. From heaven there came | the maidens helmed,--
POEM	The weapon-clang grew,-- | who watched o'er the king;
POEM	Spake Sigrun fair,-- | the wound-givers flew,
POEM	And the horse of the giantess | raven's-food had:--
POEM	
POEM	57. "Hail to thee, hero! | full happy with men,
POEM	Offspring of Yngvi, | shalt ever live,
POEM	For thou the fearless | foe hast slain
POEM	Who to many the dread | of death had brought.
POEM	
POEM	58. "Warrior, well | for thyself hast won
POEM	Red rings bright | and the noble bride;
POEM	Both now, warrior, | thine shall be,
POEM	Hogni's daughter | and Hringstathir,
POEM	Wealth and triumph; | the battle wanes."
TITLE	HELGAKVITHA HUNDINGSBANA II
SUBTITLE	The Second Lay of Helgi Hundingsbane
POEM	1. "Say to Hæming | that Helgi knows
POEM	Whom the heroes | in armor hid;
POEM	A gray wolf had they | within their hall,
POEM	Whom King Hunding | Hamal thought."
POEM	Hamal was the name of Hagal's son. King Hunding
POEM	sent men to Hagal to seek Helgi, and Helgi could not save himself in any other way, so he put on the
POEM	clothes of a bond-woman and set to work at the mill. They sought Helgi but found him not.
POEM	
POEM	2. Then Blind spake out, | the evil-minded:
POEM	 Of Hagal's bond-woman | bright are the eyes;
POEM	Yon comes not of churls | who stands at the quern;
POEM	The millstones break, | the boards are shattered.
POEM	
POEM	3. The hero has | a doom full hard,
POEM	That barley now | he needs must grind;
POEM	Better befits | his hand to feel
POEM	The hilt of the sword | than the millstone's handle."
POEM	Hagal answered and said:
POEM	
POEM	4. "Small is the wonder | if boards are splintered
POEM	By a monarch's daughter | the mill is turned;
POEM	Once through clouds | she was wont to ride,
POEM	And battles fought | like fighting men,
POEM	(Till Helgi a captive | held her fast;
POEM	Sister she is | of Sigar and Hogni,
POEM	Thus bright are the eyes | of the Ylfings' maid.)"
POEM	Helgi escaped and went to a fighting ship. He slew King Hunding, and thenceforth was called Helgi
POEM	Hundingsbane.
POEM	(II)
POEM	He lay with his host in Brunavagar, and they had there a strand-slaughtering, and ate the flesh raw. Hogni
POEM	was the name of a king. His daughter was Sigrun; she was a Valkyrie and rode air and water; she was
POEM	Svava reborn. Sigrun rode to Helgi's ship and said:
POEM	
POEM	5. "Who rules the ship | by the shore so steep?
POEM	Where is the home | ye warriors have?
POEM	Why do ye bide | in Brunavagar,
POEM	Or what the way | that ye wish to try?"
POEM	
POEM	Helgi spake:
POEM	6 "Hamal's the ship | by the shore so steep,
POEM	Our home in Hlesey | do we have;
POEM	For fair wind bide we | in Brunavagar,
POEM	Eastward the way | that we wish to try."
POEM	
POEM	Sigrun spake:
POEM	7. "Where hast thou, warrior, | battle wakened,
POEM	Or gorged the birds | of the sisters of Guth?
POEM	Why is thy byrnie | spattered with blood,
POEM	Why helmed dost feast | on food uncooked?"
POEM	
POEM	Helgi spake:
POEM	8. "Latest of all, | the Ylfings' son
POEM	On the western sea, | if know thou wilt,
POEM	Captured bears | in Bragalund,
POEM	And fed the eagles | with edge of sword.
POEM	Now is it shown | why our shirts are bloody,
POEM	And little our food | with fire is cooked."
POEM	
POEM	Sigrun spake:
POEM	9. "Of battle thou tellest, | and there was bent
POEM	Hunding the king | before Helgi down;
POEM	There was carnage when thou | didst avenge thy kin,
POEM	And blood flowed fast | on the blade of the sword."
POEM	
POEM	Helgi spake:
POEM	10. "How didst thou know | that now our kin,
POEM	Maiden wise, | we have well avenged?
POEM	Many there are | of the sons of the mighty
POEM	Who share alike | our lofty race."
POEM	
POEM	Sigrun spake:
POEM	11. "Not far was I | from the lord of the folk,
POEM	Yester morn, | when the monarch was slain;
POEM	Though crafty the son | of Sigmund, methinks,
POEM	When he speaks of the fight | in slaughter-runes.
POEM	
POEM	12. "On the long-ship once | I saw thee well,
POEM	When in the blood-stained | bow thou wast,
POEM	(And round thee icy | waves were raging;)
POEM	Now would the hero | hide from me,
POEM	But to Hogni's daughter | is Helgi known."
POEM	(III)
POEM	Granmar was the name of a mighty king, who dwelt at Svarin's hill. He had many sons; one was named
POEM	Hothbrodd, another Gothmund, a third Starkath. Hothbrodd was in a kings' meeting, and he won the
POEM	promise of having Sigrun, Hogni's daughter, for his wife. But when she heard this, she rode with the
POEM	Valkyries over air and sea to seek Helgi. Helgi was then at Logafjoll, and had fought with Hunding's
POEM	sons; there he killed Alf and Eyolf, Hjorvarth and Hervarth. He was all weary with battle, and sat under
POEM	the eagle-stone. There Sigrun found him, and ran to throw her arms about his neck, and kissed him, and
POEM	told him her tidings, as is set forth in the old Volsung lay:
POEM	
POEM	13. Sigrun the joyful | chieftain sought,
POEM	Forthwith Helgi's | hand she took;
POEM	She greeted the hero | helmed and kissed him,
POEM	The warrior's heart | to the woman turned.
POEM	
POEM	14. From her heart the daughter | of Hogni spake,
POEM	Dear was Helgi, | she said, to her;
POEM	Long with all | my heart I loved
POEM	Sigmund's son | ere ever I saw him.
POEM	
POEM	15. At the meeting to Hothbrodd | mated I was,
POEM	But another hero | I fain would have;
POEM	Though, king, the wrath | of my kin I fear,
POEM	Since I broke my father's | fairest wish."
POEM	
POEM	Helgi spake:
POEM	16. "Fear not ever | Hogni's anger,
POEM	Nor yet thy kinsmen's | cruel wrath;
POEM	Maiden, thou | with me shalt live,
POEM	Thy kindred, fair one, | I shall not fear."
POEM	(IV)
POEM	Helgi then assembled a great sea-host and went to Frekastein. On the sea he met a perilous storm;
POEM	lightning flashed overhead and the bolts struck the ship. They saw in the air that nine Valkyries were
POEM	riding, and recognized Sigrun among them. Then the storm abated, and they came safe and sound to land.
POEM	Granmar's sons sat on a certain mountain as the ships sailed toward the land. Gothmund leaped on a
POEM	horse and rode for news to a promontory near the harbor; the Volsungs were even then lowering their
POEM	sails. Then Gothmund said, as is written before in the Helgi lay:
POEM	Who is the king | who captains the fleet,
POEM	And to the land | the warriors leads?
POEM	Sinfjotli, Sigmund's son, answered him, and that too is written.
POEM	Gothmund rode home with his tidings of the host;
POEM	then Granmar's sons summoned an army. Many kings came there; there were Hogni, Sigrun's father, and
POEM	his sons Bragi and Dag. There was a great battle, and all Granmar's sons were slain and all their allies;
POEM	only Dag, Hogni's son, was spared, and he swore loyalty to the Volsungs. Sigrun went among the dead
POEM	and found Hothbrodd at the coming of death. She said:
POEM	
POEM	17. "Never shall Sigrun | from Sevafjoll,
POEM	Hothbrodd king, | be held in thine arms;
POEM	Granmar's sons | full cold have grown,
POEM	And the giant-steeds gray | on corpses gorge."
POEM	Then she sought out Helgi, and was full of joy He said:
POEM	
POEM	18. "Maid, not fair | is all thy fortune,
POEM	The Norris I blame | that this should be;
POEM	This morn there fell | at Frekastein
POEM	Bragi and Hogni | beneath my hand.
POEM	
POEM	19. "At Hlebjorg fell | the sons of Hrollaug,
POEM	Starkath the king | at Styrkleifar;
POEM	Fighters more noble | saw I never,
POEM	The body fought | when the head had fallen.
POEM	
POEM	20. "On the ground full low | the slain are lying,
POEM	Most are there | of the men of thy race;
POEM	Nought hast thou won, | for thy fate it was
POEM	Brave men to bring | to the battle-field."
POEM	Then Sigrun wept. | Helgi said:
POEM	
POEM	21. "Grieve not, Sigrun, | the battle is gained,
POEM	The fighter can shun not his fate."
POEM	
POEM	Sigrun spake:
POEM	To life would I call | them who slaughtered lie,
POEM	If safe on thy breast I might be.
POEM	(V)
POEM	This Gothmund the son of Granmar spoke:
POEM	
POEM	22. "What hero great | is guiding the ships?
POEM	A golden flag | on the stem he flies;
POEM	I find not peace in | the van of your faring,
POEM	And round the fighters | is battle-light red."
POEM	
POEM	Sinfjotli spake:
POEM	23. "Here may Hothbrodd | Helgi find,
POEM	The hater of flight, | in the midst of the fleet;
POEM	The home of all | thy race he has,
POEM	And over the realm | of the fishes he rules."
POEM	
POEM	Gothmund spake:
POEM	24. "First shall swords | at Frekastein
POEM	Prove our worth | in place of words;
POEM	Time is it, Hothbrodd, | vengeance to have,
POEM	If in battle worsted | once we were."
POEM	
POEM	Sinfjotli spake:
POEM	25. "Better, Gothmund, | to tend the goats,
POEM	And climb the rocks | of the mountain cliffs;
POEM	A hazel switch | to hold in thy hand
POEM	More seemly were | than the hilt of a sword."
POEM	
POEM	Helgi spake:
POEM	26. "Better, Sinfjotli, | thee 'twould beseem
POEM	Battles to give, | and eagles to gladden,
POEM	Than vain and empty | speech to utter,
POEM	Though warriors oft | with words do strive.
POEM	
POEM	27. "Good I find not | the sons of Granmar,
POEM	But for heroes 'tis seemly | the truth to speak;
POEM	At Moinsheimar | proved the men
POEM	That hearts for the wielding | of swords they had,
POEM	(And ever brave | the warriors are.)"
POEM	(VI)
POEM	Helgi took Sigrun to wife, and they had sons. Helgi did not reach old age. Dag, the son of Hogni, offered
POEM	sacrifice to Othin to be avenged for his father's death; Othin gave Dag his spear. Dag found Helgi, his
POEM	brother-in-law, at a place which is called Fjoturlund. He thrust the spear through Helgi's body. Then
POEM	Helgi fell, and Dag rode to Sevafjoll and told Sigrun the tidings:
POEM	
POEM	28. "Sad am I, sister, | sorrow to tell thee,
POEM	Woe to my kin | unwilling I worked;
POEM	In the morn there fell | at Fjoturlund
POEM	The noblest prince | the world has known,
POEM	(And his heel he set | on the heroes' necks.)"
POEM	
POEM	Sigrun spake:
POEM	29. "Now may every | oath thee bite
POEM	That with Helgi | sworn thou hast,
POEM	By the water | bright of Leipt,
POEM	And the ice-cold | stone of Uth.
POEM	
POEM	30. "The ship shall sail not | in which thou sailest,
POEM	Though a favoring wind | shall follow after;
POEM	The horse shall run not | whereon thou ridest,
POEM	Though fain thou art | thy foe to flee.
POEM	
POEM	31. . . . . . . . . . .
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	The sword shall bite not | which thou bearest,
POEM	Till thy head itself | it sings about.
POEM	
POEM	32. Vengeance were mine | for Helgi's murder,
POEM	Wert thou a wolf | in the woods without,
POEM	Possessing nought | and knowing no joy,
POEM	Having no food | save corpses to feed on."
POEM	
POEM	Dag spake:
POEM	33. "Mad art thou, sister, | and wild of mind,
POEM	Such a curse | on thy brother to cast;
POEM	Othin is ruler | of every ill,
POEM	Who sunders kin | with runes of spite.
POEM	
POEM	34. "Thy brother rings | so red will give thee,
POEM	All Vandilsve | and Vigdalir;
POEM	Take half my land | to pay the harm,
POEM	Ring-decked maid, | and as meed for thy sons."
POEM	
POEM	Sigrun spake:
POEM	35. "I shall sit not happy | at Sevafjoll,
POEM	Early or late, | my life to love,
POEM	If the light cannot show, | in the leader's band,
POEM	Vigblær bearing him | back to his home,
POEM	(The golden-bitted; | I shall greet him never.)
POEM	
POEM	36. "Such the fear | that Helgi's foes
POEM	Ever felt, | and all their kin,
POEM	As makes the goats | with terror mad
POEM	Run from the wolf | among the rocks.
POEM	
POEM	37. "Helgi rose | above heroes all
POEM	Like the lofty ash | above lowly thorns,
POEM	Or the noble stag, | with dew besprinkled,
POEM	Bearing his head | above all beasts,
POEM	(And his horns gleam bright | to heaven itself.)
POEM	A hill was made in Helgi's memory. And when he
POEM	came to Valhall, then Othin bade him rule over every thing with himself.
POEM	(VII)
POEM	Helgi said:
POEM	A "Thou shalt, Hunding, | of every hero
POEM	Wash the feet, | and kindle the fire,
POEM	Tie up dogs, | and tend the horses,
POEM	And feed the swine | ere to sleep thou goest."
POEM	(VIII)
POEM	One of Sigrun's maidens went one evening to Helgi's hill, and saw that Helgi rode to the hill with many
POEM	men, The maiden said:
POEM	
POEM	39. "Is this a dream | that methinks I see,
POEM	Or the doom of the gods, | that dead men ride,
POEM	And hither spurring | urge your steeds,
POEM	Or is home-coming now | to the heroes granted?"
POEM	
POEM	Helgi spake:
POEM	40. "No dream is this | that thou thinkest to see,
POEM	Nor the end of the world, | though us thou beholdest,
POEM	And hither spurring | we urge our steeds,
POEM	Nor is home-coming now | to the heroes granted."
POEM	The maiden went home and said to Sigrun:
POEM	
POEM	41. "Go forth, Sigrun, | from Sevafjoll,
POEM	If fain the lord | of the folk wouldst find;
POEM	(The hill is open, | Helgi is come;)
POEM	The sword-tracks bleed; | the monarch bade
POEM	That thou his wounds | shouldst now make well."
POEM	Sigrun went in the hill to Helgi, and said:
POEM	
POEM	42. "Now am I glad | of our meeting together,
POEM	As Othin's hawks, | so eager for prey,
POEM	When slaughter and flesh | all warm they scent,
POEM	Or dew-wet see | the red of day.
POEM	
POEM	43. "First will I kiss | the lifeless king,
POEM	Ere off the bloody | byrnie thou cast;
POEM	With frost thy hair | is heavy, Helgi,
POEM	And damp thou art | with the dew of death;
POEM	(Ice-cold hands | has Hogni's kinsman,
POEM	What, prince, can I | to bring thee ease?)"
POEM	
POEM	Helgi spake:
POEM	44. "Thou alone, Sigrun | of Sevafjoll,
POEM	Art cause that Helgi | with dew is heavy;
POEM	Gold-decked maid, | thy tears are grievous,
POEM	(Sun-bright south-maid, | ere thou sleepest;)
POEM	Each falls like blood | on the hero's breast,
POEM	(Burned-out, cold, | and crushed with care.)
POEM	
POEM	45. "Well shall we drink | a noble draught,
POEM	Though love and lands | are lost to me;
POEM	No man a song | of sorrow shall sing,
POEM	Though bleeding wounds | are on my breast;
POEM	Now in the hill | our brides we hold,
POEM	The heroes' loves, | by their husbands dead."
POEM	Sigrun made ready a bed in the hill.
POEM	
POEM	46. "Here a bed | I have made for thee, Helgi,
POEM	To rest thee from care, | thou kin of the Ylfings;
POEM	I will make thee sink | to sleep in my arms,
POEM	As once I lay | with the living king."
POEM	
POEM	Helgi spake:
POEM	47. "Now do I say | that in Sevafjoll
POEM	Aught may happen, | early or late,
POEM	Since thou sleepest clasped | in a corpse's arms,
POEM	So fair in the hill, | the daughter of Hogni!
POEM	(Living thou comest, | a daughter of kings.)
POEM	
POEM	48. "Now must I ride | the reddened ways,
POEM	And my bay steed set | to tread the sky;
POEM	Westward I go | to wind-helm's bridges,
POEM	Ere Salgofnir wakes | the warrior throng."
POEM	Then Helgi and his followers rode on their way, and
POEM	the women went home to the dwelling. Another evening Sigrun bade the maiden keep watch at the hill.
POEM	And at sunset when Sigrun came to the hill she said:
POEM	
POEM	49. "Now were he come, | if come he might,
POEM	Sigmund's son, | from Othin's seat;
POEM	Hope grows dim | of the hero's return
POEM	When eagles sit | on the ash-tree boughs,
POEM	And men are seeking | the meeting of dreams."
POEM	The Maiden said:
POEM	
POEM	50. "Mad thou wouldst seem | alone to seek,
POEM	Daughter of heroes, | the house of the dead;
POEM	For mightier now | at night are all
POEM	The ghosts of the dead | than when day is bright."
POEM	Sigrun was early dead of sorrow and grief. It was believed in olden times that people were born again,
POEM	but that is now called old wives' folly. Of Helgi and Sigrun it is said that they were born again; he
POEM	became Helgi Haddingjaskati, and she Kara the daughter of Halfdan, as is told in the Lay of Kara, and
POEM	she was a Valkyrie.
TITLE	GRIPISSPO
SUBTITLE	Gripir's Prophecy
POEM	1. "Who is it has | this dwelling here,
POEM	Or what do men call | the people's king?"
POEM	
POEM	Geitir spake:
POEM	Gripir the name | of the chieftain good
POEM	Who holds the folk | and the firm-ruled land.
POEM	
POEM	Sigurth spake:
POEM	2. "Is the king all-knowing | now within,
POEM	Will the monarch come | with me to speak?
POEM	A man unknown | his counsel needs,
POEM	And Gripir fain | I soon would find."
POEM	
POEM	Geitir spake:
POEM	3. "The ruler glad | of Geitir will ask
POEM	Who seeks with Gripir | speech to have."
POEM	
POEM	Sigurth spake:
POEM	Sigurth am I, | and Sigmund's son,
POEM	And Hjordis the name | of the hero's mother.
POEM	
POEM	4. Then Geitir went | and to Gripir spake:
POEM	A stranger comes | and stands without;
POEM	Lofty he is | to look upon,
POEM	And, prince, thyself | he fain would see.
POEM	
POEM	5. From the hall the ruler | of heroes went,
POEM	And greeted well | the warrior come:
POEM	Sigurth, welcome | long since had been thine;
POEM	Now, Geitir, shalt thou | Grani take.
POEM	
POEM	6. Then of many | things they talked,
POEM	When thus the men | so wise had met.
POEM	
POEM	Sigurth spake:
POEM	To me, if thou knowest, | my mother's brother,
POEM	Say what life | will Sigurth's be.
POEM	
POEM	Gripir spake:
POEM	7. "Of men thou shalt be | on earth the mightiest,
POEM	And higher famed | than all the heroes;
POEM	Free of gold-giving, | slow to flee,
POEM	Noble to see, | and sage in speech."
POEM	
POEM	Sigurth spake:
POEM	8. "Monarch wise, | now more I ask;
POEM	To Sigurth say, | if thou thinkest to see,
POEM	What first will chance | of my fortune fair,
POEM	When hence I go | from out thy home?"
POEM	
POEM	Gripir spake:
POEM	9. "First shalt thou, prince, | thy father avenge,
POEM	And Eylimi, | their ills requiting;
POEM	The hardy sons | of Hunding thou
POEM	Soon shalt fell, | and victory find."
POEM	
POEM	Sigurth spake:
POEM	10. "Noble king, | my kinsman, say
POEM	Thy meaning true, | for our minds we speak:
POEM	For Sigurth mighty | deeds dost see,
POEM	The highest beneath | the heavens all?"
POEM	
POEM	Gripir spake:
POEM	IT. "The fiery dragon | alone thou shalt fight
POEM	That greedy lies | at Gnitaheith;
POEM	Thou shalt be of Regin | and Fafnir both
POEM	The slayer; truth | doth Gripir tell thee."
POEM	
POEM	Sigurth spake:
POEM	12. "Rich shall I be | if battles I win
POEM	With such as these, | as now thou sayest;
POEM	Forward look, | and further tell:
POEM	What the life | that I shall lead?"
POEM	
POEM	Gripir spake:
POEM	13. "Fafnir's den | thou then shalt find,
POEM	And all his treasure | fair shalt take;
POEM	Gold shalt heap | on Grani's back,
POEM	And, proved in fight, | to Gjuki fare."
POEM	
POEM	Sigurth spake:
POEM	14. "To the warrior now | in words. so wise,
POEM	Monarch noble, | more shalt tell;
POEM	I am Gjuki's guest, | and thence I go:
POEM	What the life | that I shall lead?"
POEM	
POEM	Gripir spake:
POEM	15. "On the rocks there sleeps | the ruler's daughter,
POEM	Fair in armor, | since Helgi fell;
POEM	Thou shalt cut | with keen-edged sword,
POEM	And cleave the byrnie | with Fafnir's killer."
POEM	
POEM	Sigurth spake:
POEM	16. "The mail-coat is broken, | the maiden speaks,
POEM	The woman who | from sleep has wakened;
POEM	What says the maid | to Sigurth then
POEM	That happy fate | to the hero brings?"
POEM	
POEM	Gripir spake:
POEM	17. "Runes to the warrior | will she tell,
POEM	All that men | may ever seek,
POEM	And teach thee to speak | in all men's tongues,
POEM	And life with health; | thou'rt happy, king!"
POEM	
POEM	Sigurth spake:
POEM	18. "Now is it ended, | the knowledge is won,
POEM	And ready I am | forth thence to ride;
POEM	Forward look | and further tell:
POEM	What the life | that I shall lead?"
POEM	
POEM	Gripir spake:
POEM	19. "Then to Heimir's | home thou comest,
POEM	And glad shalt be | the guest of the king;
POEM	Ended, Sigurth, | is all I see,
POEM	No further aught | of Gripir ask."
POEM	
POEM	Sigurth spake:
POEM	20. "Sorrow brings me | the word thou sayest,
POEM	For, monarch, forward | further thou seest;
POEM	Sad the grief | for Sigurth thou knowest,
POEM	Yet nought to me, Gripir, | known wilt make."
POEM	
POEM	Gripir spake:
POEM	21. "Before me lay | in clearest light
POEM	All of thy youth | for mine eyes to see;
POEM	Not rightly can I | wise be called,
POEM	Nor forward-seeing; | my wisdom is fled."
POEM	
POEM	Sigurth spake:
POEM	22. "No man, Gripir, | on earth I know
POEM	Who sees the future | as far as thou;
POEM	Hide thou nought, | though hard it be,
POEM	And base the deeds | that I shall do."
POEM	
POEM	Gripir spake:
POEM	2Z. "With baseness never | thy life is burdened,
POEM	Hero noble, | hold that sure;
POEM	Lofty as long | as the world shall live,
POEM	Battle-bringer, | thy name shall be."
POEM	
POEM	Sigurth spake:
POEM	24. "Nought could seem worse, | but now must part
POEM	The prince and Sigurth, | since so it is,
POEM	My road I ask,-- | the future lies open,--
POEM	Mighty one, speak, | my mother's brother."
POEM	
POEM	Gripir spake:
POEM	25. "Now to Sigurth | all shall I say,
POEM	For to this the warrior | bends my will;
POEM	Thou knowest well | that I will not lie,--
POEM	A day there is | when thy death is doomed."
POEM	
POEM	Sigurth spake:
POEM	26. "No scorn I know | for the noble king,
POEM	But counsel good | from Gripir I seek;
POEM	Well will I know, | though evil awaits,
POEM	What Sigurth may | before him see."
POEM	
POEM	Gripir spake:
POEM	27. "A maid in Heimir's | home there dwells,
POEM	Brynhild her name | to men is known,
POEM	Daughter of Buthli, | the doughty king,
POEM	And Heimir fosters | the fearless maid."
POEM	
POEM	Sigurth spake:
POEM	28. "What is it to me, | though the maiden be
POEM	So fair, and of Heimir | the fosterling is?
POEM	Gripir, truth | to me shalt tell,
POEM	For all of fate | before me thou seest."
POEM	
POEM	Gripir spake:
POEM	29. "Of many a joy | the maiden robs thee,
POEM	Fair to see, | whom Heimir fosters;
POEM	Sleep thou shalt find not, | feuds thou shalt end not,
POEM	Nor seek out men, | if the maid thou seest not."
POEM	
POEM	Sigurth spake:
POEM	30. "What may be had | for Sigurth's healing?
POEM	Say now, Gripir, | if see thou canst;
POEM	May I buy the maid | with the marriage-price,
POEM	The daughter fair | of the chieftain famed?"
POEM	
POEM	Gripir spake:
POEM	31. "Ye twain shall all | the oaths then swear
POEM	That bind full fast; | few shall ye keep;
POEM	One night when Gjuki's | guest thou hast been,
POEM	Will Heimir's fosterling | fade from thy mind."
POEM	
POEM	Sigurth spake:
POEM	32. "What sayst thou, Gripir? | give me the truth,
POEM	Does fickleness hide | in the hero's heart?
POEM	Can it be that troth | I break with the maid,
POEM	With her I believed | I loved so dear?"
POEM	
POEM	Gripir spake:
POEM	33. "Tricked by another, | prince, thou art,
POEM	And the price of Grimhild's | wiles thou must pay;
POEM	Fain of thee | for the fair-haired maid,
POEM	Her daughter, she is, | and she drags thee down."
POEM	
POEM	Sigurth spake:
POEM	34. "Might I with Gunnar | kinship make,
POEM	And Guthrun win | to be my wife,
POEM	Well the hero | wedded would be,
POEM	If my treacherous deed | would trouble me not."
POEM	
POEM	Gripir spake:
POEM	35. "Wholly Grimhild | thy heart deceives,
POEM	She will bid thee go | and Brynhild woo
POEM	For Gunnar's wife, | the lord of the Goths;
POEM	And the prince's mother | thy promise shall win."
POEM	
POEM	Sigurth spake:
POEM	36. "Evil waits me, | well I see it,
POEM	And gone is Sigurth's | wisdom good,
POEM	If I shall woo | for another to win
POEM	The maiden fair | that so fondly I loved."
POEM	
POEM	Gripir spake:
POEM	37. "Ye three shall | all the oaths then take,
POEM	Gunnar and Hogni, | and, hero, thou;
POEM	Your forms ye shall change, | as forth ye tare,
POEM	Gunnar and thou; | for Gripir lies not."
POEM	
POEM	Sigurth spake:
POEM	38. "How meanest thou? | Why make we the change
POEM	Of shape and form | as forth we fare?
POEM	There must follow | another falsehood
POEM	Grim in all ways; | speak on, Gripir!"
POEM	
POEM	Gripir spake:
POEM	39. "The form of Gunnar | and shape thou gettest,
POEM	But mind and voice | thine own remain;
POEM	The hand of the fosterling | noble of Heimir
POEM	Now dost thou win, | and none can prevent."
POEM	
POEM	Sigurth spake:
POEM	40. "Most evil it seems, | and men will say
POEM	Base is Sigurth | that so he did;
POEM	Not of my will shall | I cheat with wiles
POEM	The heroes' maiden | whom noblest I hold."
POEM	
POEM	Gripir spake:
POEM	41. "Thou dwellest, leader | lofty of men,
POEM	With the maid as if | thy mother she were;
POEM	Lofty as long | as the world shall live,
POEM	Ruler of men, | thy name shall remain."
POEM	
POEM	Sigurth spake:
POEM	42. "Shall Gunnar have | a goodly wife,
POEM	Famed among men,-- | speak forth now, Gripir!
POEM	Although at my side | three nights she slept,
POEM	The warrior's bride? | Such ne'er has been."
POEM	
POEM	Gripir spake:
POEM	43. "The marriage draught | will be drunk for both,
POEM	For Sigurth and Gunnar, | in Gjuki's hall;
POEM	Your forms ye change, | when home ye fare,
POEM	But the mind of each | to himself remains."
POEM	
POEM	Sigurth spake:
POEM	44. "Shall the kinship new | thereafter come
POEM	To good among us? | Tell me, Gripir!
POEM	To Gunnar joy | shall it later give,
POEM	Or happiness send | for me myself?"
POEM	
POEM	Gripir spake:
POEM	45. "Thine oaths remembering, | silent thou art,
POEM	And dwellest with Guthrun | in wedlock good;
POEM	But Brynhild shall deem | she is badly mated,
POEM	And wiles she seeks, | herself to avenge."
POEM	
POEM	Sigurth spake:
POEM	46. "What may for the bride | requital be,
POEM	The wife we won | with subtle wiles?
POEM	From me she has | the oaths I made,
POEM	And kept not long; | they gladdened her little."
POEM	
POEM	Gripir spake:
POEM	47. "To Gunnar soon | his bride will say
POEM	That ill didst thou | thine oath fulfill,
POEM	When the goodly king, | the son of Gjuki,
POEM	With all his heart | the hero trusted."
POEM	
POEM	Sigurth spake:
POEM	48. "What sayst thou, Gripir? | give me the truth!
POEM	Am I guilty so | as now is said,
POEM	Or lies does the far-famed | queen put forth
POEM	Of me and herself? | Yet further speak."
POEM	
POEM	Gripir spake:
POEM	49. "In wrath and grief | full little good
POEM	The noble bride | shall work thee now;
POEM	No shame thou gavest | the goodly one,
POEM	Though the monarch's wife | with wiles didst cheat."
POEM	
POEM	Sigurth spake:
POEM	50. "Shall Gunnar the wise | to the woman's words,
POEM	And Gotthorm and Hogni, | then give heed?
POEM	Shall Gjuki's sons, | now tell me, Gripir,
POEM	Redden their blades | with their kinsman's blood?"
POEM	
POEM	Gripir spake:
POEM	51. "Heavy it lies | on Guthrun's heart,
POEM	When her brothers all | shall bring thee death;
POEM	Never again | shall she happiness know,
POEM	The woman so fair; | 'tis Grimhild's work."
POEM	
POEM	Sigurth spake:
POEM	52. "Now fare thee well! | our fates we shun not;
POEM	And well has Gripir | answered my wish;
POEM	More of joy | to me wouldst tell
POEM	Of my life to come | if so thou couldst."
POEM	
POEM	Gripir spake:
POEM	53 "Ever remember, ruler of men,
POEM	That fortune lies in the hero's life;
POEM	A nobler man shall never live
POEM	Beneath the sun than Sigurth shall seem."
TITLE	REGINSMOL
SUBTITLE	The Ballad of Regin
POEM	1. "What is the fish | that runs in the flood,
POEM	And itself from ill cannot save?
POEM	If thy head thou wouldst | from hell redeem,
POEM	Find me the water's flame."
POEM	
POEM	Andvari spake:
POEM	2. "Andvari am I, | and Oin my father,
POEM	In many a fall have I fared;
POEM	An evil Norn | in olden days
POEM	Doomed me In waters to dwell."
POEM	
POEM	Loki spake:
POEM	3. "Andvari, say, | if thou seekest still
POEM	To live in the land of men,
POEM	What payment is set | for the sons of men
POEM	Who war with lying words?"
POEM	
POEM	Andvari spake:
POEM	4. "A mighty payment | the men must make
POEM	Who in Valthgelmir's waters wade;
POEM	On a long road lead | the lying words
POEM	That one to another utters."
POEM	Loki saw all the gold that Andvari had. But when
POEM	he had brought forth all the gold, he held back one ring, and Loki took this from him. The dwarf went
POEM	into his rocky hole and said:
POEM	
POEM	5. "Now shall the gold | that Gust once had
POEM	Bring their death | to brothers twain,
POEM	And evil be | for heroes eight;
POEM	joy of my wealth | shall no man win."
POEM	The gods gave Hreithmar the gold, and filled up the otter-skin, and stood it on its feet. Then the gods had
POEM	to heap up gold and hide it. And when that was done, Hreithmar came forward and saw a single whisker,
POEM	and bade them cover it. Then Othin brought out the ring Andvaranaut and covered the hair. Then Loki
POEM	said:
POEM	
POEM	6. "The gold is given, | and great the price
POEM	Thou hast my head to save;
POEM	But fortune thy sons | shall find not there,
POEM	The bane of ye both it is."
POEM	
POEM	Hreithmar spake:
POEM	7. "Gifts ye gave, | but ye gave not kindly,
POEM	Gave not with hearts that were whole;
POEM	Your lives ere this | should ye all have lost,
POEM	If sooner this fate I had seen."
POEM	
POEM	Loki spake:
POEM	8. "Worse is this | that methinks I see,
POEM	For a maid shall kinsmen clash;
POEM	Heroes unborn | thereby shall be,
POEM	I deem, to hatred doomed."
POEM	
POEM	Hreithmar spake:
POEM	9. "The gold so red | shall I rule, methinks,
POEM	So long as I shall live;
POEM	Nought of fear | for thy threats I feel,
POEM	So get ye hence to your homes."
POEM	Fafnir and Regin asked Hreithmar for a share of the wealth that was paid for the slaying of their brother,
POEM	Otr. This he refused, and Fafnir thrust his sword through the
POEM	body of his father, Hreithmar, while he was sleeping. Hreithmar called to his daughters:
POEM	
POEM	10. "Lyngheith and Lofnheith, | fled is my life,
POEM	And mighty now is my need!"
POEM	
POEM	Lyngheith spake:
POEM	Though a sister loses | her father, seldom
POEM	Revenge on her brother she brings.
POEM	
POEM	Hreithmar spake:
POEM	11. "A daughter, woman | with wolf's heart, bear,
POEM	If thou hast no son | with the hero brave;
POEM	If one weds the maid, | for the need is mighty,
POEM	Their son for thy hurt | may vengeance seek."
POEM	Then Hreithmar died, and Fafnir took all the gold. Thereupon Regin asked to have his inheritance from
POEM	his father, but Fafnir refused this. Then Regin asked counsel
POEM	of Lyngheith, his sister, how he should win his inheritance. She said:
POEM	
POEM	12. "In friendly wise | the wealth shalt thou ask
POEM	Of thy brother, and better will;
POEM	Not seemly is it | to seek with the sword
POEM	Fafnir's treasure to take."
POEM	All these happenings did Regin tell to Sigurth.
POEM	One day, when he came to Regin's house, he was gladly welcomed. Regin said:
POEM	
POEM	13. "Hither the son | of Sigmund is come,
POEM	The hero eager, | here to our hall;
POEM	His courage is more | than an ancient man's,
POEM	And battle I hope | from the hardy wolf.
POEM	
POEM	14. "Here shall I foster | the fearless prince,
POEM	Now Yngvi's heir | to us is come;
POEM	The noblest hero | beneath the sun,
POEM	The threads of his fate | all lands enfold."
POEM	Sigurth was there continually with Regin, who said to Sigurth that Fafnir lay at Gnitaheith, and was in
POEM	the shape of a dragon. He had a fear-helm, of which all living creatures were terrified. Regin made
POEM	Sigurth the sword which was called Gram; it was so sharp that when he thrust it down into the Rhine, and
POEM	let a strand of wool drift against it with the stream, it cleft the strand asunder as if it were water. With this
POEM	sword Sigurth cleft asunder Regin's anvil. After that Regin egged Sigurth on to slay Fafnir, but he said:
POEM	
POEM	15. "Loud will the sons | of Hunding laugh,
POEM	Who low did Eylimi | lay in death,
POEM	If the hero sooner | seeks the red
POEM	Rings to find | than his father's vengeance."
POEM	King Hjalprek gave Sigurth a fleet for the avenging
POEM	of his father. They ran into a great storm, and were off a certain headland. A man stood on the mountain,
POEM	and said:
POEM	
POEM	16. "Who yonder rides | on Rævil's steeds,
POEM	O'er towering waves | and waters wild?
POEM	The sail-horses all | with sweat are dripping,
POEM	Nor can the sea-steeds | the gale withstand."
POEM	Regin answered:
POEM	
POEM	17. "On the sea-trees here | are Sigurth and I,
POEM	The storm wind drives us | on to our death;
POEM	The waves crash down | on the forward deck,
POEM	And the roller-steeds sink; | who seeks our names?"
POEM	
POEM	The Man spake:
POEM	18. "Hnikar I was | when Volsung once
POEM	Gladdened the ravens | and battle gave;
POEM	Call me the Man | from the Mountain now,
POEM	Feng or Fjolnir; | with you will I fare."
POEM	
POEM	They sailed to the land, and the man went on board the ship, and the storm subsided. Sigurth spake:
POEM	19. "Hnikar, say, | for thou seest the fate
POEM	That to gods and men is given;
POEM	What sign is fairest | for him who fights,
POEM	And best for the swinging of swords?"
POEM	
POEM	Hnikar spake:
POEM	20. "Many the signs, | if men but knew,
POEM	That are good for the swinging of swords;
POEM	It is well, methinks, | if the warrior meets
POEM	A raven black on his road.
POEM	
POEM	21. "Another it is | if out thou art come,
POEM	And art ready forth to fare,
POEM	To behold on the path | before thy house
POEM	Two fighters greedy of fame.
POEM	
POEM	22. "Third it is well | if a howling wolf
POEM	Thou hearest under the ash;
POEM	And fortune comes | if thy foe thou seest
POEM	Ere thee the hero beholds.
POEM	
POEM	23. "A man shall fight not | when he must face
POEM	The moon's bright sister setting late;
POEM	Win he shall | who well can see,
POEM	And wedge-like forms | his men for the fray.
POEM	
POEM	24. "Foul is the sign | if thy foot shall stumble
POEM	As thou goest forth to fight;
POEM	Goddesses baneful | at both thy sides
POEM	Will that wounds thou shalt get.
POEM	
POEM	25. "Combed and washed | shall the wise man go,
POEM	And a meal at mom shall take;
POEM	For unknown it is | where at eve he may be;
POEM	It is ill thy luck to lose."
POEM	Sigurth had a great battle with Lyngvi, the son of
POEM	Hunding, and his brothers; there Lyngvi fell, and his two brothers with him. After the battle Regin said:
POEM	
POEM	26. "Now the bloody eagle | with biting sword
POEM	Is carved on the back | of Sigmund's killer;
POEM	Few were more fierce | in fight than his son,
POEM	Who reddened the earth | and gladdened the ravens."
POEM	Sigurth went home to Hjalprek's house; thereupon Regin egged him on to fight with Fafnir.
TITLE	FAFNISMOL
SUBTITLE	The Ballad of Fafnir
POEM	1. "Youth, oh, youth! | of whom then, youth, art thou born?
POEM	Say whose son thou art,
POEM	Who in Fafnir's blood | thy bright blade reddened,
POEM	And struck thy sword to my heart."
POEM	Sigurth concealed his name because it was believed in olden times that the word of a dying man might
POEM	have great power if he cursed his foe by his name. He said:
POEM	
POEM	2. "The Noble Hart | my name, and I go
POEM	A motherless man abroad;
POEM	Father I had not, | as others have,
POEM	And lonely ever I live."
POEM	
POEM	Fafnir spake:
POEM	3. "If father thou hadst not, | as others have,
POEM	By what wonder wast thou born?
POEM	(Though thy name on the day | of my death thou hidest,
POEM	Thou knowest now thou dost lie.)"
POEM	
POEM	Sigurth spake:
POEM	4. "My race, methinks, | is unknown to thee,
POEM	And so am I myself;
POEM	Sigurth my name, | and Sigmund's son,
POEM	Who smote thee thus with the sword."
POEM	
POEM	Fafnir spake:
POEM	5. "Who drove thee on? | why wert thou driven
POEM	My life to make me lose?
POEM	A father brave | had the bright-eyed youth,
POEM	For bold in boyhood thou art."
POEM	
POEM	Sigurth spake:
POEM	6. "My heart did drive me, | my hand fulfilled,
POEM	And my shining sword so sharp;
POEM	Few are keen | when old age comes,
POEM	Who timid in boyhood be."
POEM	
POEM	Fafnir spake:
POEM	7. "If thou mightest grow | thy friends among,
POEM	One might see thee fiercely fight;
POEM	But bound thou art, | and in battle taken,
POEM	And to fear are prisoners prone."
POEM	
POEM	Sigurth spake:
POEM	8. "Thou blamest me, Fafnir, | that I see from afar
POEM	The wealth that my father's was;
POEM	Not bound am I, | though in battle taken,
POEM	Thou hast found that free I live."
POEM	
POEM	Fafnir spake:
POEM	9. "In all I say | dost thou hatred see,
POEM	Yet truth alone do I tell;
POEM	The sounding gold, | the glow-red wealth,
POEM	And the rings thy bane shall be."
POEM	
POEM	Sigurth spake:
POEM	10. "Some one the hoard | shall ever hold,
POEM	Till the destined day shall come;
POEM	For a time there is | when every man
POEM	Shall journey hence to hell."
POEM	
POEM	Fafnir spake:
POEM	11. "The fate of the Norns | before the headland
POEM	Thou findest, and doom of a fool;
POEM	In the water shalt drown | if thou row 'gainst the wind,
POEM	All danger is near to death."
POEM	
POEM	Sigurth spake:
POEM	12. "Tell me then, Fafnir, | for wise thou art famed,
POEM	And much thou knowest now:
POEM	Who are the Norns | who are helpful in need,
POEM	And the babe from the mother bring?"
POEM	
POEM	Fafnir spake:
POEM	13. "Of many births | the Norns must be,
POEM	Nor one in race they were;
POEM	Some to gods, others | to elves are kin,
POEM	And Dvalin's daughters some."
POEM	
POEM	Sigurth spake:
POEM	14. "Tell me then, Fafnir, | for wise thou art famed,
POEM	And much thou knowest now:
POEM	How call they the isle | where all the gods
POEM	And Surt shall sword-sweat mingle?"
POEM	
POEM	Fafnir spake:
POEM	15. "Oskopnir is it, | where all the gods
POEM	Shall seek the play of swords;
POEM	Bilrost breaks | when they cross the bridge,
POEM	And the steeds shall swim in the flood.
POEM	
POEM	16. "The fear-helm I wore | to afright mankind,
POEM	While guarding my gold I lay;
POEM	Mightier seemed I | than any man,
POEM	For a fiercer never I found."
POEM	
POEM	Sigurth spake:
POEM	17. "The fear-helm surely | no man shields
POEM	When he faces a valiant foe;
POEM	Oft one finds, | when the foe he meets,
POEM	That he is not the bravest of all."
POEM	
POEM	Fafnir spake:
POEM	18. "Venom I breathed | when bright I lay
POEM	By the hoard my father had;
POEM	(There was none so mighty | as dared to meet me,
POEM	And weapons nor wiles I feared.)"
POEM	
POEM	Sigurth spake:
POEM	19. "Glittering worm, | thy hissing was great,
POEM	And hard didst show thy heart;
POEM	But hatred more | have the sons of men
POEM	For him who owns the helm."
POEM	
POEM	Fafnir spake:
POEM	20. "I counsel thee, Sigurth, | heed my speech,
POEM	And ride thou homeward hence,
POEM	The sounding gold, | the glow-red wealth,
POEM	And the rings thy bane shall be."
POEM	
POEM	Sigurth spake:
POEM	21. "Thy counsel is given, | but go I shall
POEM	To the gold in the heather hidden;
POEM	And, Fafnir, thou | with death dost fight,
POEM	Lying where Hel shall have thee."
POEM	
POEM	Fafnir spake:
POEM	22. "Regin betrayed me, | and thee will betray,
POEM	Us both to death will he bring;
POEM	His life, methinks, | must Fafnir lose,
POEM	For the mightier man wast thou."
POEM	Regin had gone to a distance while Sigurth fought Fafnir, and came back while Sigurth was wiping the
POEM	blood from his sword. Regin said:
POEM	
POEM	23. "Hail to thee, Sigurth! | Thou victory hast,
POEM	And Fafnir in fight hast slain;
POEM	Of all the men | who tread the earth,
POEM	Most fearless art thou, methinks."
POEM	
POEM	Sigurth spake:
POEM	24. "Unknown it is, | when all are together,
POEM	(The sons of the glorious gods,)
POEM	Who bravest born shall seem;
POEM	Some are valiant | who redden no sword
POEM	In the blood of a foeman's breast."
POEM	
POEM	Regin spake:
POEM	25. "Glad art thou, Sigurth, | of battle gained,
POEM	As Gram with grass thou cleansest;
POEM	My brother fierce | in fight hast slain,
POEM	And somewhat I did myself."
POEM	
POEM	Sigurth spake:
POEM	26. "Afar didst thou go | while Fafnir reddened
POEM	With his blood my blade so keen;
POEM	With the might of the dragon | my strength I matched,
POEM	While thou in the heather didst hide."
POEM	
POEM	Regin spake:
POEM	27. "Longer wouldst thou | in the heather have let
POEM	Yon hoary giant hide,
POEM	Had the weapon availed not | that once I forged,
POEM	The keen-edged blade thou didst bear."
POEM	
POEM	Sigurth spake:
POEM	28. "Better is heart | than a mighty blade
POEM	For him who shall fiercely fight;
POEM	The brave man well | shall fight and win,
POEM	Though dull his blade may be.
POEM	
POEM	29. "Brave men better | than cowards be,
POEM	When the clash of battle comes;
POEM	And better the glad | than the gloomy man
POEM	Shall face what before him lies.
POEM	
POEM	30. "Thy rede it was | that I should ride
POEM	Hither o'er mountains high;
POEM	The glittering worm | would have wealth and life
POEM	If thou hadst not mocked at my might."
POEM	Then Regin went up to Fafnir and cut out his heart with his sword, that was named Rithil, and then he
POEM	drank blood from the wounds. Regin said:
POEM	
POEM	31. "Sit now, Sigurth, | for sleep will I,
POEM	Hold Fafnir's heart to the fire;
POEM	For all his heart | shall eaten be,
POEM	Since deep of blood I have drunk."
POEM	Sigurth took Fafnir's heart and cooked it on a spit. When he thought that it was fully cooked, and the
POEM	blood foamed out of the heart, then he tried it with his finger to see whether it was fully cooked. He
POEM	burned his finger, and put it in his mouth. But when Fafnir's heart's-blood came on his tongue, he
POEM	understood the speech of birds. He heard nut-hatches chattering in the thickets. A nut hatch said:
POEM	
POEM	32. "There sits Sigurth, | sprinkled with blood,
POEM	And Fafnir's heart | with fire he cooks;
POEM	Wise were the breaker | of rings, I ween,
POEM	To eat the life-muscles | all so bright."
POEM	
POEM	A second spake:
POEM	33. "There Regin lies, | and plans he lays
POEM	The youth to betray | who trusts him well;
POEM	Lying words | with wiles will he speak,
POEM	Till his brother the maker | of mischief avenges."
POEM	
POEM	A third spake:
POEM	34. "Less by a head | let the chatterer hoary
POEM	Go from here to hell;
POEM	Then all of the wealth | he alone can wield,
POEM	The gold that Fafnir guarded."
POEM	
POEM	A fourth spake:
POEM	35. "Wise would he seem | if so he would heed
POEM	The counsel good | we sisters give;
POEM	Thought he would give, | and the ravens gladden,
POEM	There is ever a wolf | where his ears I spy."
POEM	
POEM	A fifth spake:
POEM	36. "Less wise must be | the tree of battle
POEM	Than to me would seem | the leader of men,
POEM	If forth he lets | one brother fare,
POEM	When he of the other | the slayer is."
POEM	
POEM	A sixth spake:
POEM	37. "Most foolish he seems | if he shall spare
POEM	His foe, the bane of the folk,
POEM	There Regin lies, | who hath wronged him so,
POEM	Yet falsehood knows he not."
POEM	
POEM	A seventh spake:
POEM	38. "Let the head from the frost-cold | giant be hewed,
POEM	And let him of rings be robbed;
POEM	Then all the wealth | which Fafnir's was
POEM	Shall belong to thee alone."
POEM	
POEM	Sigurth spake:
POEM	39. "Not so rich a fate | shall Regin have
POEM	As the tale of my death to tell;
POEM	For soon the brothers | both shall die,
POEM	And hence to hell shall go."
POEM	Sigurth hewed off Regin's head, and then he ate Fafnir's heart, and drank the blood of both Regin and
POEM	Fafnir. Then Sigurth heard what the nut-hatch said:
POEM	
POEM	40. "Bind, Sigurth, the golden | rings together,
POEM	Not kingly is it | aught to fear;
POEM	I know a maid, | there is none so fair,
POEM	Rich in gold, | if thou mightest get her.
POEM	
POEM	41. "Green the paths | that to Gjuki lead,
POEM	And his fate the way | to the wanderer shows;
POEM	The doughty king | a daughter has,
POEM	That thou as a bride | mayst, Sigurth, buy."
POEM	
POEM	Another spake:
POEM	42. "A hall stands high | on Hindarfjoll,
POEM	All with flame | is it ringed without;
POEM	Warriors wise | did make it once
POEM	Out of the flaming | light of the flood.
POEM	
POEM	43. "On the mountain sleeps | a battle-maid,
POEM	And about her plays | the bane of the wood;
POEM	Ygg with the thorn | hath smitten her thus,
POEM	For she felled the fighter | he fain would save.
POEM	
POEM	44. "There mayst thou behold | the maiden helmed,
POEM	Who forth on Vingskornir | rode from the fight;
POEM	The victory-bringer | her sleep shall break not,
POEM	Thou heroes' son, | so the Norns have set."
POEM	Sigurth rode along Fafnir's trail to his lair, and found it open. The gate-posts were of iron, and the gates;
POEM	of iron, too, were all the beams in the house, which was dug down into the earth. There Sigurth found a
POEM	mighty store of gold, and he filled two chests full thereof; he took the fear-helm and a golden mail-coat
POEM	and the sword Hrotti, and many other precious things, and loaded Grani with them, but the horse would
POEM	not go forward until Sigurth mounted on his back.
TITLE	SIGRDRIFUMOL
SUBTITLE	The Ballad of The Victory-Bringer
POEM	1. "What bit through the byrnie? | how was broken my sleep?
POEM	Who made me free | of the fetters pale?"
POEM	He answered:
POEM	Sigmund's son, | with Sigurth's sword,
POEM	That late with flesh | hath fed the ravens.
POEM	Sigurth sat beside her and asked her name. She took a horn full of mead and gave him a
POEM	memory-draught.
POEM	
POEM	2. "Hail, day! | Hail, sons of day!
POEM	And night and her daughter now!
POEM	Look on us here | with loving eyes,
POEM	That waiting we victory win.
POEM	
POEM	3. "Hail to the gods! | Ye goddesses, hail,
POEM	And all the generous earth!
POEM	Give to us wisdom | and goodly speech,
POEM	And healing hands, life-long.
POEM	
POEM	4. "Long did I sleep, | my slumber was long,
POEM	And long are the griefs of life;
POEM	Othin decreed | that I could not break
POEM	The heavy spells of sleep."
POEM	Her name was Sigrdrifa, and she was a Valkyrie. She said that two kings fought in battle; one was called
POEM	Hjalmgunnar, an old man but a mighty warrior, and Othin had promised him the victory, and
POEM	The other was Agnar, | brother of Autha,
POEM	None he found | who fain would shield him.
POEM	Sigrdrifa, slew Hjalmgunnar in the battle, and Othin pricked her with the sleep-thorn in punishment for
POEM	this, and said that she should never thereafter win victory in battle, but that she should be wedded. "And I
POEM	said to him that I had made a vow in my turn, that I would
POEM	never marry a man who knew the meaning of fear." Sigurth answered and asked her to teach him
POEM	wisdom, if she knew of what took place in all the worlds. Sigrdrifa said:
POEM	
POEM	5. "Beer I bring thee, | tree of battle,
POEM	Mingled of strength | and mighty fame;
POEM	Charms it holds | and healing signs,
POEM	Spells full good, | and gladness-runes."
POEM	* * * * * *
POEM	
POEM	6. Winning-runes learn, | if thou longest to win,
POEM	And the runes on thy sword-hilt write;
POEM	Some on the furrow, | and some on the flat,
POEM	And twice shalt thou call on Tyr.
POEM	
POEM	7. Ale-runes learn, | that with lies the wife
POEM	Of another betray not thy trust;
POEM	On the horn thou shalt write, | and the backs of thy hands,
POEM	And Need shalt mark on thy nails.
POEM	Thou shalt bless the draught, | and danger escape,
POEM	And cast a leek in the cup;
POEM	(For so I know | thou never shalt see
POEM	Thy mead with evil mixed.)
POEM	
POEM	8. Birth-runes learn, | if help thou wilt lend,
POEM	The babe from the mother to bring;
POEM	On thy palms shalt write them, | and round thy joints,
POEM	And ask the fates to aid.
POEM	
POEM	9. Wave-runes learn, | if well thou wouldst shelter
POEM	The sail-steeds out on the sea;
POEM	On the stem shalt thou write, | and the steering blade,
POEM	And burn them into the oars;
POEM	Though high be the breakers, | and black the waves,
POEM	Thou shalt safe the harbor seek.
POEM	
POEM	10. Branch-runes learn, | if a healer wouldst be,
POEM	And cure for wounds wouldst work;
POEM	On the bark shalt thou write, | and on trees that be
POEM	With boughs to the eastward bent.
POEM	
POEM	11. Speech-runes learn, | that none may seek
POEM	To answer harm with hate;
POEM	Well he winds | and weaves them all,
POEM	And sets them side by side,
POEM	At the judgment-place, | when justice there
POEM	The folk shall fairly win.
POEM	
POEM	12. Thought-runes learn, | if all shall think
POEM	Thou art keenest minded of men.
POEM	* * * * * *
POEM	
POEM	13. Them Hropt arranged, | and them he wrote,
POEM	And them in thought he made,
POEM	Out of the draught | that down had dropped
POEM	From the head of Heithdraupnir,
