POEM	And the horn of Hoddrofnir.
POEM	
POEM	14. On the mountain he stood | with Brimir's sword,
POEM	On his head the helm he bore;
POEM	Then first the head | of Mim spoke forth,
POEM	And words of truth it told.
POEM	* * * * * *
POEM	
POEM	15. He bade write on the shield | before the shining goddess,
POEM	On Arvak's ear, | and on Alsvith's hoof,
POEM	On the wheel of the car | of Hrungnir's killer,
POEM	On Sleipnir's teeth, | and the straps of the sledge.
POEM	
POEM	16. On the paws of the bear, | and on Bragi's tongue,
POEM	On the wolf's claws bared, | and the eagle's beak,
POEM	On bloody wings, | and bridge's end,
POEM	On freeing hands | and helping foot-prints.
POEM	
POEM	17. On glass and on gold, | and on goodly charms,
POEM	In wine and in beer, | and on well-loved seats,
POEM	On Gungnir's point, | and on Grani's breast,
POEM	On the nails of Norns, | and the night-owl's beak.
POEM	* * * * * *
POEM	
POEM	18. Shaved off were the runes | that of old were written,
POEM	And mixed with the holy mead,
POEM	And sent on ways so wide;
POEM	So the gods had them, | so the elves got them,
POEM	And some for the Wanes so wise,
POEM	And some for mortal men.
POEM	
POEM	19. Beech-runes are there, | birth-runes are there,
POEM	And all the runes of ale,
POEM	And the magic runes of might;
POEM	Who knows them rightly | and reads them true,
POEM	Has them himself to help;
POEM	Ever they aid,
POEM	Till the gods are gone.
POEM	* * * * * *
POEM	
POEM	Brynhild spake:
POEM	20. "Now shalt thou choose, | for the choice is given,
POEM	Thou tree of the biting blade;
POEM	Speech or silence, | 'tis thine to say,
POEM	Our evil is destined all."
POEM	
POEM	Sigurth spake:
POEM	21. "I shall not flee, | though my fate be near,
POEM	I was born not a coward to be;
POEM	Thy loving word | for mine will I win,
POEM	As long as I shall live."
POEM	
POEM	22. Then first I rede thee, | that free of guilt
POEM	Toward kinsmen ever thou art;
POEM	No vengeance have, | though they work thee harm,
POEM	Reward after death thou shalt win.
POEM	
POEM	23. Then second I rede thee, | to swear no oath
POEM	If true thou knowest it not;
POEM	Bitter the fate | of the breaker of troth,
POEM	And poor is the wolf of his word.
POEM	
POEM	24. Then third I rede thee, | that thou at the Thing
POEM	Shalt fight not in words with fools;
POEM	For the man unwise | a worser word
POEM	Than he thinks doth utter oft.
POEM	
POEM	25. Ill it is | if silent thou art,
POEM	A coward born men call thee,
POEM	And truth mayhap they tell;
POEM	Seldom safe is fame,
POEM	Unless wide renown be won;
POEM	On the day thereafter | send him to death,
POEM	Let him pay the price of his lies.
POEM	
POEM	26. Then fourth I rede thee, | if thou shalt find
POEM	A wily witch on thy road,
POEM	It is better to go | than her guest to be,
POEM	Though night enfold thee fast.
POEM	
POEM	27. Eyes that see | need the sons of men
POEM	Who fight in battle fierce;
POEM	Oft witches evil | sit by the way,
POEM	Who blade and courage blunt.
POEM	
POEM	28. Then fifth I rede thee, | though maidens fair
POEM	Thou seest on benches sitting,
POEM	Let the silver of kinship | not rob thee of sleep,
POEM	And the kissing of women beware.
POEM	
POEM	29. Then sixth I rede thee, | if men shall wrangle,
POEM	And ale-talk rise to wrath,
POEM	No words with a drunken | warrior have,
POEM	For wine steals many men's wits.
POEM	
POEM	30. Brawls and ale | full oft have been
POEM	An ill to many a man,
POEM	Death for some, | and sorrow for some;
POEM	Full many the woes of men.
POEM	
POEM	31. Then seventh I rede thee, | if battle thou seekest
POEM	With a foe that is full of might;
POEM	It is better to fight | than to burn alive
POEM	In the hall of the hero rich.
POEM	
POEM	32. Then eighth I rede thee, | that evil thou shun,
POEM	And beware of lying words;
POEM	Take not a maid, | nor the wife of a man,
POEM	Nor lure them on to lust.
POEM	
POEM	33. Then ninth I rede thee: | burial render
POEM	If thou findest a fallen corpse,
POEM	Of sickness dead, | or dead in the sea,
POEM	Or dead of weapons' wounds.
POEM	
POEM	34. A bath shalt thou give them | who corpses be,
POEM	And hands and head shalt wash;
POEM	Wipe them and comb, | ere they go in the coffin,
POEM	And pray that they sleep in peace.
POEM	
POEM	35. Then tenth I rede thee, | that never thou trust
POEM	The word of the race of wolves,
POEM	(If his brother thou broughtest to death,
POEM	Or his father thou didst fell;)
POEM	Often a wolf | in a son there is,
POEM	Though gold he gladly takes.
POEM	
POEM	36. Battle and hate | and harm, methinks,
POEM	Full seldom fall asleep;
POEM	Wits and weapons | the warrior needs
POEM	If boldest of men he would be.
POEM	
POEM	37. Then eleventh I rede thee, | that wrath thou shun,
POEM	And treachery false with thy friends;
POEM	Not long the leader's | life shall be,
POEM	For great are the foes he faces.
TITLE	BROT AF SIGURTHARKVITHU
SUBTITLE	Fragment of a Sigurth Lay
POEM	1. "(What evil deed | has Sigurth) done,
POEM	That the hero's life | thou fain wouldst have?"
POEM	
POEM	Gunnar spake:
POEM	2. "Sigurth oaths | to me hath sworn,
POEM	Oaths hath sworn, | and all hath broken;
POEM	He betrayed me there | where truest all
POEM	His oaths, methinks, | he ought to have kept."
POEM	
POEM	Hogni spake:
POEM	3. "Thy heart hath Brynhild | whetted to hate,
POEM	Evil to work | and harm to win,
POEM	She grudges the honor | that Guthrun has,
POEM	And that joy of herself | thou still dost have."
POEM	
POEM	4. They cooked a wolf, | they cut up a snake,
POEM	They gave to Gotthorm | the greedy one's flesh,
POEM	Before the men, | to murder minded,
POEM	Laid their hands | on the hero bold.
POEM	
POEM	5. Slain was Sigurth | south of the Rhine;
POEM	From a limb a raven | called full loud:
POEM	Your blood shall redden | Atli's blade,
POEM	And your oaths shall bind | you both in chains.
POEM	
POEM	6. Without stood Guthrun, | Gjuki's daughter,
POEM	
POEM	Hear now the speech | that first she spake:
POEM	Where is Sigurth now, | the noble king,
POEM	That my kinsmen riding | before him come?
POEM	
POEM	7. Only this | did Hogni answer:
POEM	Sigurth we | with our swords have slain;
POEM	The gray horse mourns | by his master dead.
POEM	
POEM	8. Then Brynhild spake, | the daughter of Buthli:
POEM	Well shall ye joy | in weapons and lands;
POEM	Sigurth alone | of all had been lord,
POEM	If a little longer | his life had been.
POEM	
POEM	9. Right were it not | that so he should rule
POEM	O'er Gjuki's wealth | and the race of the Goths;
POEM	Five are the sons | for ruling the folk,
POEM	And greedy of fight, | that he hath fathered."
POEM	
POEM	10. Then Brynhild laughed-- | and the building echoed--
POEM	Only once, | with all her heart;
POEM	Long shall ye joy | in lands and men,
POEM	Now ye have slain | the hero noble.
POEM	
POEM	11. Then Guthrun spake, | the daughter of Gjuki:
POEM	Much thou speakest | in evil speech;
POEM	Accursed be Gunnar, | Sigurth's killer,
POEM	Vengeance shall come | for his cruel heart.
POEM	
POEM	12. Early came evening, | and ale was drunk,
POEM	And among them long | and loud they talked.;
POEM	They slumbered all | when their beds they sought,
POEM	But Gunnar alone | was long awake.
POEM	
POEM	13. His feet were tossing, | he talked to himself,
POEM	And the slayer of hosts | began to heed
POEM	What the twain from the tree | had told him then,
POEM	The raven and eagle, | as home they rode.
POEM	
POEM	14. Brynhild awoke, | the daughter of Buthli,
POEM	The warrior's daughter, | ere dawn of day:
POEM	Love me or hate me, | the harm is done,
POEM	And my grief cries out, | or else I die.
POEM	
POEM	15. Silent were all | who heard her speak,
POEM	And nought of the heart | of the queen they knew,
POEM	Who wept such tears | the thing to tell
POEM	That laughing once | of the men she had won.
POEM	
POEM	Brynhild spake:
POEM	16. "Gunnar, I dreamed | a dream full grim:
POEM	In the hall were corpses; | cold was my bed;
POEM	And, ruler, thou | didst joyless ride,
POEM	With fetters bound | in the foemen's throng.
POEM	
POEM	17. ". . . . . . . . . .
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	Utterly now your | Niflung race
POEM	All shall die; | your oaths ye have broken.
POEM	
POEM	18. "Thou hast, Gunnar, | the deed forgot,
POEM	When blood in your footprints | both ye mingled;
POEM	All to him | hast repaid with ill
POEM	Who fain had made thee | the foremost of kings.
POEM	
POEM	19. "Well did he prove, | when proud he rode
POEM	To win me then | thy wife to be,
POEM	How true the host-slayer | ever had held
POEM	The oaths he had made | with the monarch young.
POEM	
POEM	20. "The wound-staff then, | all wound with gold,
POEM	The hero let | between us lie;
POEM	With fire the edge | was forged full keen,
POEM	And with drops of venom | the blade was damp."
POEM	Here it is told in this poem about the death of Sigurth, and the story goes here that they slew him out of
POEM	doors, but some say that they slew him in the house, on his bed
POEM	while he was sleeping. But German men say that they killed him out of doors in the forest; and so it is
POEM	told in the old Guthrun lay, that Sigurth and Gjuki's sons had ridden to the council-place, and that he was
POEM	slain there. But in this they are all agreed, that they deceived him in his trust of them, and fell upon him
POEM	when he was lying down and unprepared.
TITLE	GUTHRUNARKVITHA I
SUBTITLE	The First Lay of Guthrun
POEM	1. Then did Guthrun | think to die,
POEM	When she by Sigurth | sorrowing sat;
POEM	Tears she had not, | nor wrung her hands,
POEM	Nor ever wailed, | as other women.
POEM	
POEM	2. To her the warriors | wise there came,
POEM	Longing her heavy | woe to lighten;
POEM	Grieving could not | Guthrun weep,
POEM	So sad her heart, | it seemed, would break.
POEM	
POEM	3. Then the wives | of the warriors came,
POEM	Gold-adorned, | and Guthrun sought;
POEM	Each one then | of her own grief spoke,
POEM	The bitterest pain | she had ever borne.
POEM	
POEM	4. Then spake Gjaflaug, | Gjuki's sister:
POEM	Most joyless of all | on earth am I;
POEM	Husbands five | were from me taken,
POEM	(Two daughters then, | and sisters three,)
POEM	Brothers eight, | yet I have lived.
POEM	
POEM	5. Grieving could not | Guthrun weep,
POEM	Such grief she had | for her husband dead,
POEM	And so grim her heart | by the hero's body.
POEM	
POEM	6. Then Herborg spake, | the queen of the Huns:
POEM	I have a greater | grief to tell;
POEM	My seven sons | in the southern land,
POEM	And my husband, fell | in fight all eight.
POEM	(Father and mother | and brothers four
POEM	Amid the waves | the wind once smote,
POEM	And the seas crashed through | the sides of the ship.)
POEM	
POEM	7. The bodies all | with my own hands then
POEM	I decked for the grave, | and the dead I buried;
POEM	A half-year brought me | this to bear;
POEM	And no one came | to comfort me.
POEM	
POEM	8. "Then bound I was, | and taken in war,
POEM	A sorrow yet | in the same half-year;
POEM	They bade me deck | and bind the shoes
POEM	Of the wife of the monarch | every morn.
POEM	
POEM	9. "In jealous rage | her wrath she spake,
POEM	And beat me oft | with heavy blows;
POEM	Never a better | lord I knew,
POEM	And never a woman | worse I found."
POEM	
POEM	10. Grieving could not | Guthrun weep,
POEM	Such grief she had | for her husband dead,
POEM	And so grim her heart | by the hero's body.
POEM	
POEM	11. Then spake Gollrond, | Gjuki's daughter:
POEM	Thy wisdom finds not, | my foster-mother,
POEM	The way to comfort | the wife so young.
POEM	She bade them uncover | the warrior's corpse.
POEM	
POEM	12. The shroud she lifted | from Sigurth, laying
POEM	His well-loved head | on the knees of his wife:
POEM	Look on thy loved one, | and lay thy lips
POEM	To his as if yet | the hero lived.
POEM	
POEM	13. Once alone did | Guthrun look;
POEM	His hair all clotted | with blood beheld,
POEM	The blinded eyes | that once shone bright,
POEM	The hero's breast | that the blade had pierced.
POEM	
POEM	14. Then Guthrun bent, | on her pillow bowed,
POEM	Her hair was loosened, | her cheek was hot,
POEM	And the tears like raindrops | downward ran.
POEM	
POEM	15. Then Guthrun, daughter | of Gjuki, wept,
POEM	And through her tresses | flowed the tears;
POEM	And from the court | came the cry of geese,
POEM	The birds so fair | of the hero's bride.
POEM	
POEM	16. Then Gollrond spake, | the daughter of Gjuki:
POEM	Never a greater | love I knew
POEM	Than yours among | all men on earth;
POEM	Nowhere wast happy, | at home or abroad,
POEM	Sister mine, | with Sigurth away.
POEM	
POEM	Guthrun spake:
POEM	17. "So was my Sigurth | o'er Gjuki's sons
POEM	As the spear-leek grown | above the grass,
POEM	Or the jewel bright | borne on the band,
POEM	The precious stone | that princes wear.
POEM	
POEM	18. "To the leader of men | I loftier seemed
POEM	And higher than all | of Herjan's maids;
POEM	As little now | as the leaf I am
POEM	On the willow hanging; | my hero is dead.
POEM	
POEM	19. "In his seat, in his bed, | I see no more
POEM	My heart's true friend; | the fault is theirs,
POEM	The sons of Gjuki, | for all my grief,
POEM	That so their sister | sorely weeps.
POEM	
POEM	20. "So shall your land | its people lose
POEM	As ye have kept | your oaths of yore;
POEM	Gunnar, no joy | the gold shall give thee,
POEM	(The rings shall soon | thy slayers be,)
POEM	Who swarest oaths | with Sigurth once.
POEM	
POEM	21. "In the court was greater | gladness then
POEM	The day my Sigurth | Grani saddled,
POEM	And went forth Brynhild's | hand to win,
POEM	That woman ill, | in an evil hour."
POEM	
POEM	22. Then Brynhild spake, | the daughter of Buthli:
POEM	May the witch now husband | and children want
POEM	Who, Guthrun, loosed | thy tears at last,
POEM	And with magic today | hath made thee speak.
POEM	
POEM	23. Then Gollrond, daughter | of Gjuki, spake:
POEM	Speak not such words, | thou hated woman;
POEM	Bane of the noble | thou e'er hast been,
POEM	(Borne thou art | on an evil wave,
POEM	Sorrow hast brought | to seven kings,)
POEM	And many a woman | hast loveless made.
POEM	
POEM	24. Then Brynhild, daughter | of Buthli, spake:
POEM	Atli is guilty | of all the sorrow,
POEM	(Son of Buthli | and brother of mine,)
POEM	When we saw in the hall | of the Hunnish race
POEM	The flame of the snake's bed | flash round the hero;
POEM	(For the journey since | full sore have I paid,
POEM	And ever I seek | the sight to forget.)
POEM	
POEM	25. By the pillars she stood, | and gathered her strength,
POEM	From the eyes of Brynhild, | Buthli's daughter,
POEM	Fire there burned, | and venom she breathed,
POEM	When the wounds she saw | on Sigurth then.
POEM	Guthrun went thence away to a forest in the waste, and journeyed all the way to Denmark, and was there
POEM	seven half-years with Thora, daughter of Hokon. Brynhild would not live after Sigurth. She had eight of
POEM	her thralls slain and five serving-women. Then she killed her self with a sword, as is told in the Short Lay
POEM	of Sigurth.
TITLE	SIGURTHARKVITHA EN SKAMMA
SUBTITLE	The Short Lay of Sigurth
POEM	1. Of old did Sigurth | Gjuki seek,
POEM	The Volsung young, | in battles victor;
POEM	Well he trusted | the brothers twain,
POEM	With mighty oaths | among them sworn.
POEM	
POEM	2. A maid they gave him, | and jewels many,
POEM	Guthrun the young, | the daughter of Gjuki;
POEM	They drank and spake | full many a day,
POEM	Sigurth the young | and Gjuki's sons.
POEM	
POEM	3. Thereafter went they | Brynhild to woo,
POEM	And so with them | did Sigurth ride,
POEM	The Volsung young, | in battle valiant,--
POEM	Himself would have had her | if all he had seen.
POEM	
POEM	4. The southern hero | his naked sword,
POEM	Fair-flashing, let | between them lie;
POEM	(Nor would he come | the maid to kiss;)
POEM	The Hunnish king | in his arms ne'er held
POEM	The maiden he gave | to Gjuki's sons.
POEM	
POEM	5. Ill she had known not | in all her life,
POEM	And nought of the sorrows | of men she knew;
POEM	Blame she had not, | nor dreamed she should bear it,
POEM	But cruel the fates | that among them came.
POEM	
POEM	6. By herself at the end | of day she sat,
POEM	And in open words | her heart she uttered:
POEM	I shall Sigurth have, | the hero young,
POEM	E'en though within | my arms he die.
POEM	
POEM	7. The word I have spoken; | soon shall I rue it,
POEM	His wife is Guthrun, | and Gunnar's am I;
POEM	Ill Norns set for me | long desire."
POEM	
POEM	8. Oft did she go | with grieving heart
POEM	On the glacier's ice | at even-tide,
POEM	When Guthrun then | to her bed was gone,
POEM	And the bedclothes Sigurth | about her laid.
POEM	
POEM	9. " (Now Gjuki's child | to her lover goes,)
POEM	And the Hunnish king | with his wife is happy;
POEM	Joyless I am | and mateless ever,
POEM	Till cries from my heavy | heart burst forth."
POEM	
POEM	10. In her wrath to battle | she roused herself:
POEM	Gunnar, now | thou needs must lose
POEM	Lands of mine | and me myself,
POEM	No joy shall I have | with the hero ever.
POEM	
POEM	11. Back shall I fare | where first I dwelt,
POEM	Among the kin | that come of my race,
POEM	To wait there, sleeping | my life away,
POEM	If Sigurth's death | thou shalt not dare,
POEM	(And best of heroes | thou shalt not be.)
POEM	
POEM	12. "The son shall fare | with his father hence,
POEM	And let not long | the wolf-cub live;
POEM	Lighter to pay | is the vengeance-price
POEM	After the deed | if the son is dead."
POEM	
POEM	13. Sad was Gunnar, | and bowed with grief,
POEM	Deep in thought | the whole day through;
POEM	Yet from his heart | it was ever hid
POEM	What deed most fitting | he should find,
POEM	(Or what thing best | for him should be,
POEM	Or if he should seek | the Volsung to slay,
POEM	For with mighty longing | Sigurth he loved.)
POEM	
POEM	14. Much he pondered | for many an hour;
POEM	Never before | was the wonder known
POEM	That a queen should thus | her kingdom leave;
POEM	In counsel then | did he Hogni call,
POEM	(For him in truest | trust he held.)
POEM	
POEM	15. "More than all | to me is Brynhild,
POEM	Buthli's child, | the best of women;
POEM	My very life | would I sooner lose
POEM	Than yield the love | of yonder maid.
POEM	
POEM	16. "Wilt thou the hero | for wealth betray?
POEM	Twere good to have | the gold of the Rhine,
POEM	And all the hoard | in peace to hold,
POEM	And waiting fortune | thus to win."
POEM	
POEM	17. Few the words | of Hogni were:
POEM	Us it beseems not | so to do,
POEM	To cleave with swords | the oaths we swore,
POEM	The oaths we swore | and all our vows.
POEM	
POEM	18. We know no mightier | men on earth
POEM	The while we four | o'er the folk hold sway,
POEM	And while the Hunnish | hero lives,
POEM	Nor higher kinship | the world doth hold.
POEM	
POEM	19. "If sons we five | shall soon beget,
POEM	Great, methinks, | our race shall grow;
POEM	Well I see | whence lead the ways;
POEM	Too bitter far | is Brynhild's hate."
POEM	
POEM	Gunnar spake:
POEM	20. "Gotthorm to wrath | we needs must rouse,
POEM	Our younger brother, | in rashness blind;
POEM	He entered not | in the oaths we swore,
POEM	The oaths we swore | and all our vows."
POEM	
POEM	21. It was easy to rouse | the reckless one.
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	The sword in the heart | of Sigurth stood.
POEM	
POEM	22. In vengeance the hero | rose in the hall,
POEM	And hurled his sword | at the slayer bold;
POEM	At Gotthorm flew | the glittering steel
POEM	Of Gram full hard | from the hand of the king.
POEM	
POEM	23. The foeman cleft | asunder fell,
POEM	Forward hands | and head did sink,
POEM	And legs and feet | did backward fall.
POEM	
POEM	24. Guthrun soft | in her bed had slept,
POEM	Safe from care | at Sigurth's side;
POEM	She woke to find | her joy had fled,
POEM	In the blood of the friend | of Freyr she lay.
POEM	
POEM	25. So hard she smote | her hands together
POEM	That the hero rose up, | iron-hearted:
POEM	Weep not, Guthrun, | grievous tears,
POEM	Bride so young, | for thy brothers live.
POEM	
POEM	26. Too young, methinks, | is my son as yet,
POEM	He cannot flee | from the home of his foes;
POEM	Fearful and deadly | the plan they found,
POEM	The counsel new | that now they have heeded.
POEM	
POEM	27. "No son will ride, | though seven thou hast,
POEM	To the Thing as the son | of their sister rides;
POEM	Well I see | who the ill has worked,
POEM	On Brynhild alone | lies the blame for all.
POEM	
POEM	28. "Above all men | the maiden loved me,
POEM	Yet false to Gunnar | I ne'er was found;
POEM	I kept the oaths | and the kinship I swore;
POEM	Of his queen the lover | none may call me.
POEM	
POEM	29. In a swoon she sank | when Sigurth died;
POEM	So hard she smote | her hands together
POEM	That all the cups | in the cupboard rang,
POEM	And loud in the courtyard | cried the geese.
POEM	
POEM	30. Then Brynhild, daughter | of Buthli, laughed,
POEM	Only once, | with all her heart,
POEM	When as she lay | full loud she heard
POEM	The grievous wail | of Gjuki's daughter.
POEM	
POEM	31. Then Gunnar, monarch | of men, spake forth:
POEM	Thou dost not laugh, | thou lover of hate,
POEM	In gladness there, | or for aught of good;
POEM	Why has thy face | so white a hue,
POEM	Mother of ill? | Foredoomed thou art.
POEM	
POEM	32. A worthier woman | wouldst thou have been
POEM	If before thine eyes | we had Atli slain;
POEM	If thy brother's bleeding | body hadst seen
POEM	And the bloody wounds | that thou shouldst End."
POEM	
POEM	Brynhild spake:
POEM	33. "None mock thee, Gunnar! | thou hast mightily fought,
POEM	But thy hatred little | doth Atli heed;
POEM	Longer than thou, | methinks, shall he live,
POEM	And greater in might | shall he ever remain.
POEM	
POEM	34. "To thee I say, | and thyself thou knowest,
POEM	That all these ills | thou didst early shape;
POEM	No bonds I knew, | nor sorrow bore,
POEM	And wealth I had | in my brother's home.
POEM	
POEM	35. "Never a husband | sought I to have,
POEM	Before the Gjukungs | fared to our land;
POEM	Three were the kings | on steeds that came,--
POEM	Need of their journey | never there was.
POEM	
POEM	36. "To the hero great | my troth I gave
POEM	Who gold-decked sat | on Grani's back;
POEM	Not like to thine | was the light of his eyes,
POEM	(Nor like in form | and face are ye,)
POEM	Though kingly both | ye seemed to be.
POEM	
POEM	37. "And so to me | did Atli say
POEM	That share in our wealth | I should not have,
POEM	Of gold or lands, | if my hand I gave not;
POEM	(More evil yet, | the wealth I should yield,)
POEM	The gold that he | in my childhood gave me,
POEM	(The wealth from him | in my youth I had.)
POEM	
POEM	38. "Oft in my mind | I pondered much
POEM	If still I should fight, | and warriors fell,
POEM	Brave in my byrnie, | my brother defying;
POEM	That would wide | in the world be known,
POEM	And sorrow for many | a man would make.
POEM	
POEM	39. "But the bond at last | I let be made,
POEM	For more the hoard | I longed to have,
POEM	The rings that the son | of Sigmund won;
POEM	No other's treasure | e'er I sought.
POEM	
POEM	40. "One-alone | of all I loved,
POEM	Nor changing heart | I ever had;
POEM	All in the end | shall Atli know,
POEM	When he hears I have gone | on the death-road hence."
POEM	* * * * * *
POEM	
POEM	41. "Never a wife | of fickle will
POEM	Yet to another | man should yield.
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	So vengence for all | my ills shall come."
POEM	42, Up rose Gunnar, | the people's ruler,
POEM	And flung his arms | round her neck so fair;
POEM	And all who came, | of every kind,
POEM	Sought to hold her | with all their hearts.
POEM	
POEM	43. But back she cast | all those who came,
POEM	Nor from the long road | let them hold her;
POEM	In counsel then | did he Hogni call:
POEM	Of wisdom now | full great is our need.
POEM	
POEM	44. Let the warriors here | in the hall come forth,
POEM	Thine and mine, | for the need is mighty,
POEM	If haply the queen | from death they may hold,
POEM	Till her fearful thoughts | with time shall fade."
POEM	
POEM	45. (Few the words | of Hogni were:)
POEM	From the long road now | shall ye hold her not,
POEM	That born again | she may never be!
POEM	Foul she came | from her mother forth,
POEM	And born she was | for wicked deeds,
POEM	(Sorrow to many | a man to bring.)
POEM	
POEM	46. From the speaker gloomily | Gunnar turned,
POEM	For the jewel-bearer | her gems was dividing;
POEM	On all her wealth | her eyes were gazing,
POEM	On the bond-women slain | and the slaughtered slaves.
POEM	
POEM	47. Her byrnie of gold | she donned, and grim
POEM	Was her heart ere the point | of her sword had pierced it;
POEM	On the pillow at last | her head she laid,
POEM	And, wounded, her plan | she pondered o'er.
POEM	
POEM	48. "Hither I will | that my women come
POEM	Who gold are fain | from me to get;
POEM	Necklaces fashioned | fair to each
POEM	Shall I give, and cloth, | and garments bright."
POEM	
POEM	49. Silent were all | as so she spake,
POEM	And all together | answer made:
POEM	Slain are enough; | we seek to live,
POEM	Not thus thy women | shall honor win.
POEM	
POEM	50. Long the woman, | linen-decked, pondered,--
POEM	--Young she was,-- | and weighed her words:
POEM	For my sake now | shall none unwilling
POEM	Or loath to die | her life lay down.
POEM	
POEM	51. But little of gems | to gleam on your limbs
POEM	Ye then shall find | when forth ye fare
POEM	To follow me, | or of Menja's wealth.
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	
POEM	52. "Sit now, Gunnar! | for I shall speak
POEM	Of thy bride so fair | and so fain to die;
POEM	Thy ship in harbor | home thou hast not,
POEM	Although my life | I now have lost.
POEM	
POEM	53. "Thou shalt Guthrun requite | more quick than thou thinkest,
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	Though sadly mourns | the maiden wise
POEM	Who dwells with the king, | o'er her husband dead.
POEM	
POEM	54. "A maid shall then | the mother bear;
POEM	Brighter far | than the fairest day
POEM	Svanhild shall be, | or the beams of the sun.
POEM	
POEM	55. "Guthrun a noble | husband thou givest,
POEM	Yet to many a warrior | woe will she bring,
POEM	Not happily wedded | she holds herself;
POEM	Her shall Atli | hither seek,
POEM	(Buthli's son, | and brother of mine.)
POEM	
POEM	56. "Well I remember | how me ye treated
POEM	When ye betrayed me | with treacherous wiles;
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	Lost was my joy | as long as I lived.
POEM	
POEM	57. "Oddrun as wife | thou fain wouldst win,
POEM	But Atli this | from thee withholds;
POEM	Yet in secret tryst | ye twain shall love;
POEM	She shall hold thee dear, | as I had done
POEM	If kindly fate | to us had fallen.
POEM	
POEM	58. "Ill to thee | shall Atli bring,
POEM	When he casts thee down | in the den of snakes.
POEM	
POEM	59. "But soon thereafter | Atli too
POEM	His life, methinks, | as thou shalt lose,
POEM	(His fortune lose | and the lives of his sons;)
POEM	Him shall Guthrun, | grim of heart,
POEM	With the biting blade | in his bed destroy.
POEM	
POEM	60. "It would better beseem | thy sister fair
POEM	To follow her husband | first in death,
POEM	If counsel good | to her were given,
POEM	Or a heart akin | to mine she had.
POEM	
POEM	61. "Slowly I speak,-- | but for my sake
POEM	Her life, methinks, | she shall not lose;
POEM	She shall wander over | the tossing waves,
POEM	To where Jonak rules | his father's realm.
POEM	
POEM	62. "Sons to him | she soon shall bear,
POEM	Heirs therewith | of Jonak's wealth;
POEM	But Svanhild far | away is sent,
POEM	The child she bore | to Sigurth brave.
POEM	
POEM	63. "Bikki's word | her death shall be,
POEM	For dreadful the wrath | of Jormunrek;
POEM	So slain is all | of Sigurth's race,
POEM	And greater the woe | of Guthrun grows.
POEM	
POEM	64. "Yet one boon | I beg of thee,
POEM	The last of boons | in my life it is:
POEM	Let the pyre be built | so broad in the field
POEM	That room for us all | will ample be,
POEM	(For us who slain | with Sigurth are.)
POEM	
POEM	65. "With shields and carpets | cover the pyre,
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	Shrouds full fair, | and fallen slaves,
POEM	And besides the Hunnish | hero burn me.
POEM	
POEM	66. "Besides the Hunnish | hero there
POEM	Slaves shall burn, | full bravely decked,
POEM	Two at his head | and two at his feet,
POEM	A brace of hounds | and a pair of hawks,
POEM	For so shall all | be seemly done.
POEM	
POEM	67. "Let between us | lie once more
POEM	The steel so keen, | as so it lay
POEM	When both within | one bed we were,
POEM	And wedded mates | by men were called.
POEM	
POEM	68. "The door of the hall | shall strike not the heel
POEM	Of the hero fair | with flashing rings,
POEM	If hence my following | goes with him;
POEM	Not mean our faring | forth shall be.
POEM	
POEM	69. "Bond-women five | shall follow him,
POEM	And eight of my thralls, | well-born are they,
POEM	Children with me, | and mine they were
POEM	As gifts that Buthli | his daughter gave.
POEM	
POEM	70. "Much have I told thee, | and more would say
POEM	If fate more space | for speech had given;
POEM	My voice grows weak, | my wounds are swelling;
POEM	Truth I have said, | and so I die."
TITLE	HELREITH BRYNHILDAR
SUBTITLE	Brynhild's Hell-Ride
POEM	1. "Thou shalt not further | forward fare,
POEM	My dwelling ribbed | with rocks across;
POEM	More seemly it were | at thy weaving to stay,
POEM	Than another's husband | here to follow.
POEM	
POEM	2. "What wouldst thou have | from Valland here,
POEM	Fickle of heart, | in this my house?
POEM	Gold-goddess, now, | if thou wouldst know,
POEM	Heroes' blood | from thy hands hast washed."
POEM	
POEM	Brynhild spake:
POEM	3. "Chide me not, woman | from rocky walls,
POEM	Though to battle once | I was wont to go;
POEM	Better than thou | I shall seem to be,
POEM	When men us two | shall truly know."
POEM	
POEM	The giantess spake:
POEM	4. "Thou wast, Brynhild, | Buthli's daughter,
POEM	For the worst of evils | born in the world;
POEM	To death thou hast given | Gjuki's children,
POEM	And laid their lofty | house full low."
POEM	
POEM	Brynhild spake:
POEM	5. "Truth from the wagon | here I tell thee,
POEM	Witless one, | if know thou wilt
POEM	How the heirs of Gjuki | gave me to be
POEM	joyless ever, | a breaker of oaths.
POEM	
POEM	6. "Hild the helmed | in Hlymdalir
POEM	They named me of old, | all they who knew me.
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	
POEM	7. "The monarch bold | the swan-robes bore
POEM	Of the sisters eight | beneath an oak;
POEM	Twelve winters I was, | if know thou wilt,
POEM	When oaths I yielded | the king so young.
POEM	
POEM	8. "Next I let | the leader of Goths,
POEM	Hjalmgunnar the old, | go down to hell,
POEM	And victory brought | to Autha's brother;
POEM	For this was Othin's | anger mighty.
POEM	
POEM	9. "He beset me with shields | in Skatalund,
POEM	Red and white, | their rims o'erlapped;
POEM	He bade that my sleep | should broken be
POEM	By him who fear | had nowhere found.
POEM	
POEM	10. "He let round my hall, | that southward looked,
POEM	The branches' foe | high-leaping burn;
POEM	Across it he bade | the hero come
POEM	Who brought me the gold | that Fafnir guarded
POEM	
POEM	11. On Grani rode | the giver of gold,
POEM	Where my foster-father | ruled his folk;
POEM	Best of all | he seemed to be,
POEM	The prince of the Danes, | when the people met.
POEM	
POEM	12. "Happy we slept, | one bed we had,
POEM	As he my brother | born had been;
POEM	Eight were the nights | when neither there
POEM	Loving hand | on the other laid.
POEM	
POEM	13. "Yet Guthrun reproached me, | Gjuki's daughter,
POEM	That I in Sigurth's | arms had slept;
POEM	Then did I hear | what I would were hid,
POEM	That they had betrayed me | in taking a mate.
POEM	
POEM	14. "Ever with grief | and all too long
POEM	Are men and women | born in the world;
POEM	But yet we shall live | our lives together,
POEM	Sigurth and I. | Sink down, Giantess!"
TITLE	GUTHRUNARKVITHA II, EN FORNA
SUBTITLE	The Second, or Old, Lay of Guthrun
POEM	1. A maid of maids | my mother bore me,
POEM	Bright in my bower, | my brothers I loved,
POEM	Till Gjuki dowered | me with gold,
POEM	Dowered with gold, | and to Sigurth gave me.
POEM	
POEM	2. So Sigurth rose | o'er Gjuki's sons
POEM	As the leek grows green | above the grass,
POEM	Or the stag o'er all | the beasts doth stand,
POEM	Or as glow-red gold | above silver gray.
POEM	
POEM	3. Till my brothers let me | no longer have
POEM	The best of heroes | my husband to be;
POEM	Sleep they could not, | or quarrels settle,
POEM	Till Sigurth they | at last had slain.
POEM	
POEM	4. From the Thing ran Grani | with thundering feet,
POEM	But thence did Sigurth | himself come never;
POEM	Covered with sweat | was the saddle-bearer,
POEM	Wont the warrior's | weight to bear.
POEM	
POEM	5. Weeping I sought | with Grani to speak,
POEM	With tear-wet cheeks | for the tale I asked;
POEM	The head of Grani | was bowed to the grass,
POEM	The steed knew well | his master was slain.
POEM	
POEM	6. Long I waited | and pondered well
POEM	Ere ever the king | for tidings I asked.
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	
POEM	7. His head bowed Gunnar, | but Hogni told
POEM	The news full sore | of Sigurth slain:
POEM	Hewed to death | at our hands he lies,
POEM	Gotthorm's slayer, | given to wolves.
POEM	
POEM	8. On the southern road | thou shalt Sigurth see,
POEM	Where hear thou canst | the ravens cry;
POEM	The eagles cry | as food they crave,
POEM	And about thy husband | wolves are howling."
POEM	
POEM	9. "Why dost thou, Hogni, | such a horror
POEM	Let me hear, | all joyless left?
POEM	Ravens yet | thy heart shall rend
POEM	In a land that never | thou hast known."
POEM	
POEM	10. Few the words | of Hogni were,
POEM	Bitter his heart | from heavy sorrow:
POEM	Greater, Guthrun, | thy grief shall be
POEM	If the ravens so | my heart shall rend.
POEM	
POEM	11. From him who spake | I turned me soon,
POEM	In the woods to find | what the wolves had left;
POEM	Tears I had not, | nor wrung my bands,
POEM	Nor wailing went, | as other women,
POEM	(When by Sigurth | slain I sat).
POEM	
POEM	12. Never so black | had seemed the night
POEM	As when in sorrow | by Sigurth I sat;
POEM	The wolves . . . . . . . . . .
POEM	
POEM	13. . . . . . . . . . .
POEM	Best of all | methought 'twould be
POEM	If I my life | could only lose,
POEM	Or like to birch-wood | burned might be.
POEM	
POEM	14. From the mountain forth | five days I fared,
POEM	Till Hoalf's hall | so high I saw;
POEM	Seven half-years | with Thora I stayed,
POEM	Hokon's daughter, | in Denmark then.
POEM	
POEM	15. With gold she broidered, | to bring me joy,
POEM	Southern halls | and Danish swans;
POEM	On the tapestry wove we | warrior's deeds,
POEM	And the hero's thanes | on our handiwork;
POEM	(Flashing shields | and fighters armed,
POEM	Sword-throng, helm-throng, | the host of the king).
POEM	
POEM	16. Sigmund's ship | by the land was sailing,
POEM	Golden the figure-head, | gay the beaks;
POEM	On board we wove | the warriors faring,
POEM	Sigar and Siggeir, | south to Fjon.
POEM	
POEM	17. Then Grimhild asked, | the Gothic queen,
POEM	Whether willingly would I . . . . .
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	
POEM	18. Her needlework cast she | aside, and called
POEM	Her sons to ask, | with stern resolve,
POEM	Who amends to their sister | would make for her son,
POEM	Or the wife requite | for her husband killed.
POEM	
POEM	19. Ready was Gunnar | gold to give,
POEM	Amends for my hurt, | and Hogni too;
POEM	Then would she know | who now would go,
POEM	The horse to saddle, | the wagon to harness,
POEM	(The horse to ride, | the hawk to fly,
POEM	And shafts from bows | of yew to shoot).
POEM	
POEM	20. (Valdar, king | of the Danes, was come,
POEM	With Jarizleif, Eymoth, | and Jarizskar).
POEM	In like princes | came they all,
POEM	The long-beard men, | with mantles red,
POEM	Short their mail-coats, | mighty their helms,
POEM	Swords at their belts, | and brown their hair.
POEM	
POEM	21. Each to give me | gifts was fain,
POEM	Gifts to give, | and goodly speech,
POEM	Comfort so | for my sorrows great
POEM	To bring they tried, | but I trusted them not.
POEM	
POEM	22. A draught did Grimhild | give me to drink,
POEM	Bitter and cold; | I forgot my cares;
POEM	For mingled therein was magic earth,
POEM	Ice-cold sea, and the blood of swine.
POEM	
POEM	23. In the cup were runes of every kind,
POEM	Written and reddened, I could not read them;
POEM	A heather-fish from the Haddings' land,
POEM	An ear uncut, and the entrails of beasts.
POEM	
POEM	24. Much evil was brewed within the beer,
POEM	Blossoms of trees, and acorns burned,
POEM	Dew of the hearth, and holy entrails,
POEM	The liver of swine,-- all grief to allay.
POEM	
POEM	25. Then I forgot, when the draught they gave me,
POEM	There in the hall, my husband's slaying;
POEM	On their knees the kings all three did kneel,
POEM	Ere she herself to speak began:
POEM	
POEM	26. "Guthrun, gold | to thee I give,
POEM	The wealth that once | thy father's was,
POEM	Rings to have, | and Hlothver's halls,
POEM	And the hangings all | that the monarch had.
POEM	
POEM	27. "Hunnish women, | skilled in weaving,
POEM	Who gold make fair | to give thee joy,
POEM	And the wealth of Buthli | thine shall be,
POEM	Gold-decked one, | as Atli's wife."
POEM	
POEM	Guthrun spake:
POEM	28. "A husband now | I will not have,
POEM	Nor wife of Brynhild's | brother be;
POEM	It beseems me not | with Buthli's son
POEM	Happy to be, | and heirs to bear."
POEM	
POEM	Grimhild spake:
POEM	29. "Seek not on men | to avenge thy sorrows,
POEM	Though the blame at first | with us hath been;
POEM	Happy shalt be | as if both still lived,
POEM	Sigurth and Sigmund, | if sons thou bearest."
POEM	
POEM	Guthrun spake:
POEM	30. "Grimhild, I may not | gladness find,
POEM	Nor hold forth hopes | to heroes now,
POEM	Since once the raven | and ravening wolf
POEM	Sigurth's heart's-blood | hungrily lapped."
POEM	
POEM	Grimhild spake:
POEM	31. "Noblest of birth | is the ruler now
POEM	I have found for thee, | and foremost of all;
POEM	Him shalt thou have | while life thou hast,
POEM	Or husbandless be | if him thou wilt choose not."
POEM	
POEM	Guthrun spake:
POEM	32. "Seek not so eagerly | me to send
POEM	To be a bride | of yon baneful race;
POEM	On Gunnar first | his wrath shall fall,
POEM	And the heart will he tear | from Hogni's breast."
POEM	
POEM	33. Weeping Grimhild | heard the words
POEM	That fate full sore | for her sons foretold,
POEM	(And mighty woe | for them should work;)
POEM	Lands I give thee, | with all that live there,
POEM	(Vinbjorg is thine, | and Valbjorg too,)
POEM	Have them forever, | but hear me, daughter.
POEM	
POEM	34. So must I do | as the kings besought,
POEM	And against my will | for my kinsmen wed,
POEM	Ne'er with my husband | joy I had,
POEM	And my sons by my brothers' | fate were saved not.
POEM	
POEM	35. . . . . . . . . . .
POEM	I could not rest | till of life I had robbed
POEM	The warrior bold, | the maker of battles.
POEM	
POEM	36. Soon on horseback | each hero was,
POEM	And the foreign women | in wagons faring;
POEM	A week through lands | so cold we went,
POEM	And a second week | the waves we smote,
POEM	(And a third through lands | that water lacked).
POEM	
POEM	37. The warders now | on the lofty walls
POEM	Opened the gates, | and in we rode.
POEM	* * * * * *
POEM	
POEM	38. Atli woke me, | for ever I seemed
POEM	Of bitterness full | for my brothers' death.
POEM	
POEM	Atli spake:
POEM	39. "Now from sleep | the Norris have waked me
POEM	With visions of terror,-- | to thee will I tell them;
POEM	Methought thou, Guthrun, | Gjuki's daughter,
POEM	With poisoned blade | didst pierce my body."
POEM	
POEM	Guthrun spake:
POEM	40. "Fire a dream | of steel shall follow
POEM	And willful pride | one of woman's wrath;
POEM	A baneful sore | I shall burn from thee,
POEM	And tend and heal thee, | though hated thou am"
POEM	
POEM	Atli spake:
POEM	41. "Of plants I dreamed, | in the garden drooping,
POEM	That fain would I have | full high to grow;
POEM	Plucked by the roots, | and red with blood,
POEM	They brought them hither, | and bade me eat.
POEM	
POEM	42. "I dreamed my hawks | from my hand had flown,
POEM	Eager for food, | to an evil house;
POEM	I dreamed their hearts | with honey I ate,
POEM	Soaked in blood, | and heavy my sorrow.
POEM	
POEM	43. "Hounds I dreamed | from my hand I loosed,
POEM	Loud in hunger | and pain they howled;
POEM	Their flesh methought | was eagles' food,
POEM	And their bodies now | I needs must eat."
POEM	
POEM	Guthrun spake:
POEM	44. "Men shall soon | of sacrifice speak,
POEM	And off the heads | of beasts shall hew
POEM	Die they shall | ere day has dawned,
POEM	A few nights hence, | and the folk shall have them."
POEM	
POEM	Atli spake:
POEM	45. "On my bed I sank, | nor slumber sought,
POEM	Weary with woe,-- | full well I remember.
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
TITLE	GUTHRUNARKVITHA III
SUBTITLE	The Third Lay of Guthrun
POEM	1. "What thy sorrow, Atli, | Buthli's son?
POEM	Is thy heart heavy-laden? | Why laughest thou never?
POEM	It would better befit | the warrior far
POEM	To speak with men, | and me to look on."
POEM	
POEM	Atli spake:
POEM	2. "It troubles me, Guthrun, | Gjuki's daughter,
POEM	What Herkja here | in the hall hath told me,
POEM	That thou in the bed | with Thjothrek liest,
POEM	Beneath the linen | in lovers' guise."
POEM	
POEM	Guthrun spake:
POEM	3. "This shall I | with oaths now swear,
POEM	Swear by the sacred | stone so white,
POEM	That nought was there | with Thjothmar's son
POEM	That man or woman | may not know.
POEM	
POEM	4. "Nor ever once | did my arms embrace
POEM	The hero brave, | the leader of hosts;
POEM	In another manner | our meeting was,
POEM	When our sorrows we | in secret told.
POEM	
POEM	5. "With thirty warriors | Thjothrek came,
POEM	Nor of all his men | doth one remain;
POEM	Thou hast murdered my brothers | and mail-clad men,
POEM	Thou hast murdered all | the men of my race.
POEM	
POEM	6. "Gunnar comes not, | Hogni I greet not,
POEM	No longer I see | my brothers loved;
POEM	My sorrow would Hogni | avenge with the sword,
POEM	Now myself for my woes | I shall payment win.
POEM	
POEM	7. "Summon Saxi, | the southrons' king,
POEM	For be the boiling | kettle can hallow."
POEM	Seven hundred | there were in the hall,
POEM	Ere the queen her hand | in the kettle thrust.
POEM	
POEM	8. To the bottom she reached | with hand so bright,
POEM	And forth she brought | the flashing stones:
POEM	Behold, ye warriors, | well am I cleared
POEM	Of sin by the kettle's | sacred boiling.
POEM	
POEM	9. Then Atli's heart | in happiness laughed,
POEM	When Guthrun's hand | unhurt he saw;
POEM	Now Herkja shall come | the kettle to try,
POEM	She who grief | for Guthrun planned.
POEM	
POEM	10. Ne'er saw man sight | more sad than this,
POEM	How burned were the hands | of Herkja then;
POEM	In a bog so foul | the maid they flung,
POEM	And so was Guthrun's | grief requited.
TITLE	ODDRUNARGRATR
SUBTITLE	The Lament of Oddrun
POEM	1. I have heard it told | in olden tales
POEM	How a maiden came | to Morningland;
POEM	No one of all | on earth above
POEM	To Heithrek's daughter | help could give.
POEM	
POEM	2. This Oddrun learned, | the sister of Atli,
POEM	That sore the maiden's | sickness was;
POEM	The bit-bearer forth | from his stall she brought,
POEM	And the saddle laid | on the steed so black.
POEM	
POEM	3. She let the horse go | o'er the level ground,
POEM	Till she reached the hall | that loftily rose,
POEM	(And in she went | from the end of the hall;)
POEM	From the weary steed | the saddle she took;
POEM	
POEM	Hear now the speech | that first she spake:
POEM	4. "What news on earth, | . . . . .
POEM	Or what has happened | in Hunland now?"
POEM	
POEM	A serving-maid spake:
POEM	Here Borgny lies | in bitter pain,
POEM	Thy friend, and, Oddrun, | thy help would find.
POEM	
POEM	Oddrun spake:
POEM	5. 'Who worked this woe | for the woman thus,
POEM	Or why so sudden | is Borgny sick?"
POEM	
POEM	The serving-maid spake:
POEM	Vilmund is he, | the heroes' friend,
POEM	Who wrapped the woman | in bedclothes warm,
POEM	(For winters five, | yet her father knew not).
POEM	
POEM	6. Then no more | they spake, methinks;
POEM	She went at the knees | of the woman to sit;
POEM	With magic Oddrun | and mightily Oddrun
POEM	Chanted for Borgny | potent charms.
POEM	
POEM	7. At last were born | a boy and girl,
POEM	Son and daughter | of Hogni's slayer;
POEM	Then speech the woman | so weak began,
POEM	
POEM	Nor said she aught | ere this she spake:
POEM	8. "So may the holy | ones thee help,
POEM	Frigg and Freyja | and favoring gods,
POEM	As thou hast saved me | from sorrow now."
POEM	
POEM	Oddrun spake:
POEM	9. "I came not hither | to help thee thus
POEM	Because thou ever | my aid didst earn;
POEM	I fulfilled the oath | that of old I swore,
POEM	That aid to all | I should ever bring,
POEM	(When they shared the wealth | the warriors had)."
POEM	
POEM	Borgny spake:
POEM	10. "Wild art thou, Oddrun, | and witless now,
POEM	That so in hatred | to me thou speakest;
POEM	I followed thee | where thou didst fare,
POEM	As we had been born | of brothers twain."
POEM	
POEM	Oddrun spake:
POEM	11. "I remember the evil | one eve thou spakest,
POEM	When a draught I gave | to Gunnar then;
POEM	Thou didst say that never | such a deed
POEM	By maid was done | save by me alone."
POEM	
POEM	12. Then the sorrowing woman | sat her down
POEM	To tell the grief | of her troubles great.
POEM	
POEM	13. "Happy I grew | in the hero's hall
POEM	As the warriors wished, | and they loved me well;
POEM	Glad I was | of my father's gifts,
POEM	For winters five, | while my father lived.
POEM	
POEM	14. "These were the words | the weary king,
POEM	
POEM	Ere he died, | spake last of all:
POEM	He bade me with red gold | dowered to be,
POEM	And to Grimhild's son | in the South be wedded.
POEM	
POEM	15. "But Brynhild the helm | he bade to wear,
POEM	A wish-maid bright | he said she should be;
POEM	For a nobler maid | would never be born
POEM	On earth, he said, | if death should spare her.
POEM	
POEM	16. "At her weaving Brynhild | sat in her bower,
POEM	Lands and folk | alike she had;
POEM	The earth and heaven | high resounded
POEM	When Fafnir's slayer | the city saw.
POEM	
POEM	17. "Then battle was fought | with the foreign swords,
POEM	And the city was broken | that Brynhild had;
POEM	Not long thereafter, | but all too soon,
POEM	Their evil wiles | full well she knew.
POEM	
POEM	18. "Woeful for this | her vengeance was,
POEM	As so we learned | to our sorrow all;
POEM	In every land | shall all men hear
POEM	How herself at Sigurth's | side she slew.
POEM	
POEM	19. "Love to Gunnar | then I gave,
POEM	To the breaker of rings, | as Brynhild might;
POEM	To Atli rings | so red they offered,
POEM	And mighty gifts | to my brother would give.
POEM	
POEM	20. "Fifteen dwellings | fain would he give
POEM	For me, and the burden | that Grani bore;
POEM	But Atli said | he would never receive
POEM	Marriage gold | from Gjuki's son.
POEM	
POEM	21. "Yet could we not | our love o'ercome,
POEM	And my head I laid | on the hero's shoulder;
POEM	Many there were | of kinsmen mine
POEM	Who said that together | us they had seen.
POEM	
POEM	22. "Atli said | that never I
POEM	Would evil plan, | or ill deed do;
POEM	But none may this | of another think,
POEM	Or surely speak, | when love is shared.
POEM	
POEM	23. "Soon his men | did Atli send,
POEM	In the murky wood | on me to spy;
POEM	Thither they came | where they should not come,
POEM	Where beneath one cover | close we lay.
POEM	
POEM	24. "To the warriors ruddy | rings we offered,
POEM	That nought to Atli | e'er they should say;
POEM	But swiftly home | they hastened thence,
POEM	And eager all | to Atli told.
POEM	
POEM	25. "But close from Guthrun | kept they hid
POEM	What first of all | she ought to have known.
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	
POEM	26. "Great was the clatter | of gilded hoofs
POEM	When Gjuki's sons | through the gateway rode;
POEM	The heart they hewed | from Hogni then,
POEM	And the other they cast | in the serpents' cave.
POEM	
POEM	27. "The hero wise | on his harp then smote,
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	For help from me | in his heart yet hoped
POEM	The high-born king, | might come to him.
POEM	
POEM	28. "Alone was I gone | to Geirmund then,
POEM	The draught to mix | and ready to make;
POEM	Sudden I heard | from Hlesey clear
POEM	How in sorrow the strings | of the harp resounded.
POEM	
POEM	29. "I bade the serving-maids | ready to be,
POEM	For I longed the hero's | life to save;
POEM	Across the sound | the boats we sailed,
POEM	Till we saw the whole | of Atli's home.
POEM	
POEM	30. "Then crawling the evil | woman came,
POEM	Atli's mother-- | may she ever rot!
POEM	And hard she bit | to Gunnar's heart,
POEM	So I could not help | the hero brave.
POEM	
POEM	31. "Oft have I wondered | how after this,
POEM	Serpents'-bed goddess! | I still might live,
POEM	For well I loved | the warrior brave,
POEM	The giver of swords, | as my very self.
POEM	
POEM	32. "Thou didst see and listen, | the while I said
POEM	The mighty grief | that was mine and theirs;
POEM	Each man lives | as his longing wills,--
POEM	Oddrun's lament | is ended now."
TITLE	ATLAKVITHA EN GRÖNLENZKA
SUBTITLE	The Greenland Lay of Atli
POEM	1. Atli sent | of old to Gunnar
POEM	A keen-witted rider, | Knefröth did men call him;
POEM	To Gjuki's home came he | and to Gunnar's dwelling,
POEM	With benches round the hearth, | and to the beer so sweet.
POEM	
POEM	2. Then the followers, hiding | their falseness, all drank
POEM	Their wine in the war-hall, | of the Huns' wrath wary;
POEM	And Knefröth spake loudly, | his words were crafty,
POEM	The hero from the south, | on the high bench sitting:
POEM	
POEM	3. "Now Atli has sent me | his errand to ride,
POEM	On my bit-champing steed | through Myrkwood the secret,
POEM	To bid You, Gunnar, | to his benches to come,
POEM	With helms round the hearth, | and Atli's home seek.
POEM	
POEM	4. "Shields shall ye choose there, | and shafts made of ash-wood,
POEM	Gold-adorned helmets, | and slaves out of Hunland,
POEM	Silver-gilt saddle-cloths, | shirts of bright scarlet,
POEM	With lances and spears too, | and bit-champing steeds.
POEM	
POEM	5. "The field shall be given you | of wide Gnitaheith,
POEM	With loud-ringing lances, | and stems gold-o'er-laid,
POEM	Treasures full huge, | and the home of Danp,
POEM	And the mighty forest | that Myrkwood is called."
POEM	
POEM	6. His head turned Gunnar, | and to Hogni he said:
POEM	What thy counsel, young hero, | when such things we hear?
POEM	No gold do I know | on Gnitaheith lying
POEM	So fair that other | its equal we have not.
POEM	
POEM	7. We have seven halls, | each of swords is full,
POEM	(And all of gold | is the hilt of each;)
POEM	My steed is the swiftest, | my sword is sharpest,
POEM	My bows adorn benches, | my byrnies are golden,
POEM	My helm is the brightest | that came from Kjar's hall,
POEM	(Mine own is better | than all the Huns' treasure.)"
POEM	
POEM	Hogni spake:
POEM	8. "What seeks she to say, | that she sends us a ring,
POEM	Woven with a wolf's hair? | methinks it gives warning;
POEM	In the red ring a hair | of the heath-dweller found I,
POEM	Wolf-like shall our road be | if we ride on this journey."
POEM	
POEM	9. Not eager were his comrades, | nor the men of his kin,
POEM	The wise nor the wary, | nor the warriors bold.
POEM	But Gunnar spake forth | as befitted a king,
POEM	Noble in the beer-hall, | and bitter his scorn:
POEM	
POEM	10. "Stand forth now, Fjornir! | and hither on the floor
POEM	The beakers all golden | shalt thou bring to the warriors.
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	
POEM	11. "The wolves then shall rule | the wealth of the Niflungs,
POEM	Wolves aged and grey-hued, | if Gunnar is lost,
POEM	And black-coated bears | with rending teeth bite,
POEM	And make glad the dogs, | if Gunnar returns not."
POEM	
POEM	12. A following gallant | fared forth with the ruler,
POEM	Yet they wept as their home | with the hero they left;
POEM	And the little heir | of Hogni called loudly:
POEM	Go safe now, ye wise ones, | wherever ye will!
POEM	
POEM	13. Then let the bold heroes | their bit-champing horses
POEM	On the mountains gallop, | and through Myrkwood the secret;
POEM	All Hunland was shaken | where the hard-souled ones rode,
POEM	On the whip-fearers fared they | through fields that were green.
POEM	
POEM	14. Then they saw Atli's halls, | and his watch-towers high,
POEM	On the walls so lofty | stood the warriors of Buthli;
POEM	The hall of the southrons | with seats was surrounded,
POEM	With targets bound | and shields full bright.
POEM	
POEM	15. Mid weapons and lances | did Atli his wine
POEM	In the war-hall drink, | without were his watchmen,
POEM	For Gunnar they waited, | if forth he should go,
POEM	With their ringing spears | they would fight with the ruler.
POEM	
POEM	16. This their sister saw, | as soon as her brothers
POEM	Had entered the hall,-- | little ale had she drunk:
POEM	Betrayed art thou, Gunnar! | what guard hast thou, hero,
POEM	Gainst the plots of the Huns? | from the hall flee swiftly!
POEM	
POEM	17. Brother, 'twere far better | to have come in byrnie,
POEM	With thy household helmed, | to see Atli's home,
POEM	And to sit in the saddle | all day 'neath the sun,
POEM	(That the sword-norns might weep | for the death-pale warriors,
POEM	And the Hunnish shield-maids | might shun not the sword,)
POEM	And send Atli himself | to the den of the snakes;
POEM	(Now the den of the snakes | for thee is destined.
POEM	
POEM	Gunnar spake:
POEM	18. . . . . . . . . . .
POEM	Too late is it, sister, | to summon the Niflungs,
POEM	Long is it to come | to the throng of our comrades,
POEM	The heroes gallant, | from the hills of the Rhine.
POEM	* * * * * *
POEM	
POEM	19. Then Gunnar they seized, | and they set him in chains,
POEM	The Burgundians' king, | and fast they bound him.
POEM	
POEM	20. Hogni slew seven | with sword so keen,
POEM	And an eighth he flung | in the fire hot;
POEM	A hero should fight | with his foemen thus,
POEM	As Hogni strove | in Gunnar's behalf.
POEM	
POEM	21. . . . . . . . . . .
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	The leader they asked | if his life he fain
POEM	With gold would buy, | the king of the Goths.
POEM	
POEM	Gunnar spake:
POEM	22. "First the heart of Hogni | shall ye lay in my hands,
POEM	All bloody from the breast | of the bold one cut
POEM	With ke-en-biting sword, | from the son of the king."
POEM	
POEM	23. . . . . . . . . . .
POEM	They cut out the heart | from the breast of Hjalli,
POEM	On a platter they bore it, | and brought it to Gunnar.
POEM	
POEM	24. Then Gunnar spake forth, | the lord of the folk:
POEM	Here have I the heart | of Hjalli the craven,
POEM	Unlike to the heart | of Hogni the valiant,
POEM	For it trembles still | as it stands on the platter;
POEM	Twice more did it tremble | in the breast of the man.
POEM	
POEM	25. Then Hogni laughed | when they cut out the heart
POEM	Of the living helm-hammerer; | tears he had not.
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	On a platter they bore it, | and brought it to Gunnar.
POEM	
POEM	26. Then Gunnar spake forth, | the spear of the Niflungs:
POEM	Here have I the heart | of Hogni the valiant,
POEM	Unlike to the heart | of Hjalli the craven,
POEM	Little it trembles | as it lies on the platter,
POEM	Still less did it tremble | when it lay in his breast.
POEM	
POEM	27. "So distant, Atli, | from all men's eyes,
POEM	Shalt thou be as thou | . . . . . from the gold.
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	
POEM	28. "To no one save me | is the secret known
POEM	Of the Niflungs' hoard, | now Hogni is dead;
POEM	Of old there were two, | while we twain were alive,
POEM	Now is none but I, | for I only am living.
POEM	
POEM	29. "The swift Rhine shall hold | the strife-gold of heroes,
POEM	That once was the gods', | the wealth of the Niflungs,
POEM	In the depths of the waters | the death-rings shall glitter,
POEM	And not shine on the hands | of the Hunnish men."
POEM	
POEM	Atli spake:
POEM	30. "Ye shall bring the wagon, | for now is he bound."
POEM	* * * * * *
POEM	
POEM	31. On the long-maned Glaum | rode Atli the great,
POEM	About him were warriors | . . . . .
POEM	But Guthrun, akin | to the gods of slaughter,
POEM	Yielded not to her tears | in the hall of tumult.
POEM	
POEM	Guthrun spake:
POEM	32. "It shall go with thee, Atli, | as with Gunnar thou heldest
POEM	The oaths ofttimes sworn, | and of old made firm,
POEM	By the sun in the south, | by Sigtyr's mountain,
POEM	By the horse of the rest-bed, | and the ring of Ull."
POEM	
POEM	33. Then the champer of bits | drew the chieftain great,
POEM	The gold-guarder, down | to the place of death.
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	
POEM	34. By the warriors' host | was the living hero
POEM	Cast in the den | where crawling about
POEM	Within were serpents, | but soon did Gunnar
POEM	With his hand in wrath | on the harp-strings smite;
POEM	The strings resounded,-- | so shall a hero,
POEM	A ring-breaker, gold | from his enemies guard.
POEM	
POEM	35. Then Atli rode | on his earth-treading steed,
POEM	Seeking his home, | from the slaughter-place;
POEM	There was clatter of hoofs | of the steeds in the court,
POEM	And the clashing of arms | as they came from the field.
POEM	
POEM	36. Out then came Guthrun | to meeting with Atli,
POEM	With a golden beaker | as gift to the monarch:
POEM	Thou mayst eat now, chieftain, | within thy dwelling,
POEM	Blithely with Guthrun | young beasts fresh slaughtered.
POEM	
POEM	37. The wine-heavy ale-cups | of Atli resounded,
POEM	When there in the hall | the Hunnish youths clamored,
POEM	And the warriors bearded, | the brave ones, entered.
POEM	
POEM	38. Then in came the shining one, | . . . . .
POEM	. . . . . | and drink she bore them;
POEM	Unwilling and bitter | brought she food to the warrior,
POEM	Till in scorn to the white-faced | Atli did she speak:
POEM	
POEM	39. "Thou giver of swords, | of thy sons the hearts
POEM	All heavy with blood | in honey thou hast eaten;
POEM	Thou shalt stomach, thou hero, | the flesh of the slain,
POEM	To eat at thy feast, | and to send to thy followers.
POEM	
POEM	40. "Thou shalt never call | to thy knees again
POEM	Erp or Eitil, | when merry with ale;
POEM	Thou shalt never see | in their seats again
POEM	The sharers of gold | their lances shaping,
POEM	(Clipping the manes | or minding their steeds.)"
POEM	
POEM	41. There was clamor on the benches, | and the cry of men,
POEM	The clashing of weapons, | and weeping of the Huns,
POEM	Save for Guthrun only, | she wept not ever
POEM	For her bear-fierce brothers, | or the boys so dear,
POEM	So young and so unhappy, | whom with Atli she had.
POEM	
POEM	42. Gold did she scatter, | the swan-white one,
POEM	And rings of red gold | to the followers gave she;
POEM	The fate she let grow, | and the shining wealth go,
POEM	Nor spared she the treasure | of the temple itself.
POEM	
POEM	43. Unwise then was Atli, | he had drunk to wildness,
POEM	No weapon did he have, | and of Guthrun bewared not;
POEM	Oft their play was better | when both in gladness
POEM	Each other embraced | among princes all.
POEM	
POEM	44. With her sword she gave blood | for the bed to drink,
POEM	With her death-dealing hand, | and the hounds she loosed,
POEM	The thralls she awakened, | and a firebrand threw
POEM	In the door of the hall; | so vengeance she had.
POEM	
POEM	45. To the flames she gave all | who yet were within,
POEM	And from Myrkheim had come | from the murder of Gunnar;
POEM	The timbers old fell, | the temple was in flames,
POEM	The dwelling of the Buthlungs, | and the shield-maids burned,
POEM	They were slain in the house, | in the hot flames they sank.
POEM	
POEM	46. Now the tale is all told, | nor in later time
POEM	Will a woman in byrnie | avenge so her brothers;
POEM	The fair one to three | of the kings of the folk
POEM	Brought the doom of death | ere herself she died.
POEM	Still more is told in the Greenland ballad of Atli.
TITLE	GUTHRUNARHVOT
SUBTITLE	Guthrun's Inciting
POEM	1. A word-strife I learned, | most woeful of all,
POEM	A speech from the fullness | of sorrow spoken,
POEM	When fierce of heart | her sons to the fight
POEM	Did Guthrun whet | with words full grim.
POEM	
POEM	2. "Why sit ye idle, | why sleep out your lives,
POEM	Why grieve ye not | in gladness to speak?
POEM	Since Jormunrek | your sister young
POEM	Beneath the hoofs | of horses hath trodden,
POEM	(White and black | on the battle-way,
POEM	Gray, road-wonted, | the steeds of the Goths.)
POEM	
POEM	3. "Not like are ye | to Gunnar of yore,
POEM	Nor have ye hearts | such as Hogni's was;
POEM	Vengeance for her | ye soon would have
POEM	If brave ye were | as my brothers of old,
POEM	Or hard your hearts | as the Hunnish kings'."
POEM	
POEM	4. Then Hamther spake, | the high of heart:
POEM	Little the deed | of Hogni didst love,
POEM	When Sigurth they wakened | from his sleep;
POEM	Thy bed-covers white | were red with blood
POEM	Of thy husband, drenched | with gore from his heart.
POEM	
POEM	5. Bloody revenge | didst have for thy brothers,
POEM	Evil and sore, | when thy sons didst slay;
POEM	Else yet might we all | on Jormunrek
POEM	Together our sister's | slaying avenge.
POEM	
POEM	6. . . . . . . . . . .
POEM	The gear of the Hunnish | kings now give us!
POEM	Thou hast whetted us so | to the battle of swords."
POEM	
POEM	7. Laughing did Guthrun | go to her chamber,
POEM	The helms of the kings | from the cupboards she took,
POEM	And mail-coats broad, | to her sons she bore them;
POEM	On their horses' backs | the heroes leaped.
POEM	
POEM	8. Then Hamther spake, | the high of heart:
POEM	Homeward no more | his mother to see
POEM	Comes the spear-god, | fallen mid Gothic folk;
POEM	One death-draught thou | for us all shalt drink,
POEM	For Svanhild then | and thy sons as well.
POEM	
POEM	9. Weeping Guthrun, | Gjuki's daughter,
POEM	Went sadly before | the gate to sit,
POEM	And with tear-stained cheeks | to tell the tale
POEM	Of her mighty griefs, | so many in kind.
POEM	
POEM	10. "Three home-fires knew I, | three hearths I knew,
POEM	Home was I brought | by husbands three;
POEM	But Sigurth only | of all was dear,
POEM	He whom my brothers | brought to his death.
POEM	
POEM	11. "A greater sorrow | I saw not nor knew,
POEM	Yet more it seemed | I must suffer yet
POEM	When the princes great | to Atli gave me.
POEM	
POEM	12. "The brave boys I summoned | to secret speech;
POEM	For my woes requital | I might not win
POEM	Till off the heads | of the Hniflungs I hewed.
POEM	
POEM	13. "To the sea I went, | my heart full sore
POEM	For the Norns, whose wrath | I would now escape;
POEM	But the lofty billows | bore me undrowned,
POEM	Till to land I came, | so I longer must live.
POEM	
POEM	14. "Then to the bed-- | of old was it better!--
POEM	Of a king of the folk | a third time I came;
POEM	Boys I bore | his heirs to be,
POEM	Heirs so young, | the sons of Jonak.
POEM	
POEM	15. "But round Svanhild | handmaidens sat,
POEM	She was dearest ever | of all my children;
POEM	So did Svanhild | seem in my hall
POEM	As the ray of the sun | is fair to see.
POEM	
POEM	16. "Gold I gave her | and garments bright,
POEM	Ere I let her go | to the Gothic folk;
POEM	Of my heavy woes | the hardest it was
POEM	When Svanhild's tresses | fair were trodden
POEM	In the mire by hoofs | of horses wild.
POEM	
POEM	17. "The sorest it was | when Sigurth mine
POEM	On his couch, of victory | robbed, they killed;
POEM	And grimmest of all | when to Gunnar's heart
POEM	There crept the bright-hued | crawling snakes.
POEM	
POEM	18. "And keenest of all | when they cut the heart
POEM	From the living breast | of the king so brave;
POEM	Many woes I remember, | . . . . .
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	
POEM	19. "Bridle, Sigurth, | thy steed so black,
POEM	Hither let run | thy swift-faring horse;
POEM	Here there sits not | son or daughter
POEM	Who yet to Guthrun | gifts shall give.
POEM	
POEM	20. "Remember, Sigurth, | what once we said,
POEM	When together both | on the bed we sat,
POEM	That mightily thou | to me wouldst come
POEM	From hell and I | from earth to thee.
POEM	
POEM	21. "Pile ye up, jarls, | the pyre of oak,
POEM	Make it the highest | a hero e'er had;
POEM	Let the fire burn | my grief-filled breast,
POEM	My sore-pressed heart, | till my sorrows melt."
POEM	
POEM	22. May nobles all | less sorrow know,
POEM	And less the woes | of women become,
POEM	Since the tale of this | lament is told.
TITLE	HAMTHESMOL
SUBTITLE	The Ballad of Hamther
POEM	1. Great the evils | once that grew,
POEM	With the dawning sad | of the sorrow of elves;
POEM	In early mom | awake for men
POEM	The evils that grief | to each shall bring.
POEM	
POEM	2. Not now, nor yet | of yesterday was it,
POEM	Long the time | that since hath lapsed,
POEM	So that little there is | that is half as old,
POEM	Since Guthrun, daughter | of Gjuki, whetted
POEM	Her sons so young | to Svanhild's vengeance.
POEM	
POEM	3. "The sister ye had | was Svanhild called,
POEM	And her did Jormunrek | trample with horses,
POEM	White and black | on the battle-way,
POEM	Gray, road-wonted, | the steeds of the Goths.
POEM	
POEM	4. "Little the kings | of the folk are ye like,
POEM	For now ye are living | alone of my race.
POEM	
POEM	5. "Lonely am I | as the forest aspen,
POEM	Of kindred bare | as the fir of its boughs,
POEM	My joys are all lost | as the leaves of the tree
POEM	When the scather of twigs | from the warm day turns."
POEM	
POEM	6. Then Hamther spake forth, | the high of heart:
POEM	Small praise didst thou, Guthrun, | to Hogni's deed give
POEM	When they wakened thy Sigurth | from out of his sleep,
POEM	Thou didst sit on the bed | while his slayers laughed.
POEM	
POEM	7. Thy bed-covers white | with blood were red
POEM	From his wounds, and with gore | of thy husband were wet;
POEM	So Sigurth was slain, | by his corpse didst thou sit,
POEM	And of gladness didst think not: | 'twas Gunnar's doing.
POEM	
POEM	8. "Thou wouldst strike at Atli | by the slaying of Erp
POEM	And the killing of Eitil; | thine own grief was worse;
POEM	So should each one wield | the wound-biting sword
POEM	That another it slays | but smites not himself."
POEM	
POEM	9. Then did Sorli speak out, | for wise was he ever:
POEM	With my mother I never | a quarrel will make;
POEM	Full little in speaking | methinks ye both lack;
POEM	What askest thou, Guthrun, | that will give thee no tears?
POEM	
POEM	10. For thy brothers dost weep, | and thy boys so sweet,
POEM	Thy kinsmen in birth | on the battlefield slain;
POEM	Now, Guthrun, as; well | for us both shalt thou weep,
POEM	We sit doomed on our steeds, | and far hence shall we die."
POEM	
POEM	11. Then the fame-glad one-- | on the steps she was--
POEM	
POEM	The slender-fingered, | spake with her son:
POEM	Ye shall danger have | if counsel ye heed not;
POEM	By two heroes alone | shall two hundred of Goths
POEM	Be bound or be slain | in the lofty-walled burg.
POEM	
POEM	12. From the courtyard they fared, | and fury they breathed;
POEM	The youths swiftly went | o'er the mountain wet,
POEM	On their Hunnish steeds, | death's vengeance to have.
POEM	
POEM	13. On the way they found | the man so wise;
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	What help from the weakling | brown may we have?
POEM	
POEM	14. So answered them | their half-brother then:
POEM	So well may I | my kinsmen aid
POEM	As help one foot | from the other has.
POEM	
POEM	15. "How may afoot | its fellow aid,
POEM	Or a flesh-grown hand | another help?"
POEM	
POEM	16. Then Erp spake forth, | his words were few,
POEM	As haughty he sat | on his horse's back:
POEM	To the timid 'tis ill | the way to tell.
POEM	A bastard they | the bold one called.
POEM	
POEM	17. From their sheaths they drew | their shining swords,
POEM	Their blades, to the giantess | joy to give;
POEM	By a third they lessened | the might that was theirs,
POEM	The fighter young | to earth they felled.
POEM	
POEM	18. Their cloaks they shook, | their swords they sheathed,
POEM	The high-born men | wrapped their mantles close.
POEM	
POEM	19. On their road they fared | and an ill way found,
POEM	And their sister's son | on a tree they saw,
POEM	On the wind-cold wolf-tree | west of the hall,
POEM	And cranes'-bait crawled; | none would care to linger.
POEM	
POEM	20. In the hall was din, | the men drank deep,
POEM	And the horses' hoofs | could no one hear,
POEM	Till the warrior hardy | sounded his horn.
POEM	
POEM	21. Men came and the tale | to Jormunrek told
POEM	How warriors helmed | without they beheld:
POEM	Take counsel wise, | for brave ones are come,
POEM	Of mighty men | thou the sister didst murder.
POEM	
POEM	22. Then Jormunrek laughed, | his hand laid on his beard,
POEM	His arms, for with wine | he was warlike, he called for;
POEM	He shook his brown locks, | on his white shield he looked,
POEM	And raised high the cup | of gold in his hand.
POEM	
POEM	23. "Happy, methinks, | were I to behold
POEM	Hamther and Sorli | here in my hall;
POEM	The men would I bind | with strings of bows,
POEM	And Gjuki's heirs | on the gallows hang."
POEM	
POEM	24. In the hall was clamor, | the cups were shattered,
POEM	Men stood in blood | from the breasts of the Goths,
POEM	
POEM	25. Then did Hamther speak forth, | the haughty of heart:
POEM	Thou soughtest, Jormunrek, | us to see,
POEM	Sons of one mother | seeking thy dwelling;
POEM	Thou seest thy hands, | thy feet thou beholdest,
POEM	Jormunrek, flung | in the fire so hot.
POEM	
POEM	26. Then roared the king, | of the race of the gods,
POEM	Bold in his armor, | as roars a bear:
POEM	Stone ye the men | that steel will bite not,
POEM	Sword nor spear, | the sons of Jonak.
POEM	
POEM	Sorli spake:
POEM	27. "Ill didst win, brother, | when the bag thou didst open,
POEM	Oft from that bag | came baleful counsel;
POEM	Heart hast thou, Hamther, | if knowledge thou hadst!
POEM	A man without wisdom | is lacking in much."
POEM	
POEM	Hamther spake:
POEM	28. "His head were now off | if Erp were living,
POEM	The brother so keen | whom we killed on our road,
POEM	The warrior noble,-- | 'twas the Norns that drove me
POEM	The hero to slay | who in fight should be holy.
POEM	
POEM	29. "In fashion of wolves | it befits us not
POEM	Amongst ourselves to strive,
POEM	Like the hounds of the Norns, | that nourished were
POEM	In greed mid wastes so grim.
POEM	
POEM	30. "We have greatly fought, | o'er the Goths do we stand
POEM	By our blades laid low, | like eagles on branches;
POEM	Great our fame though we die | today or tomorrow;
POEM	None outlives the night | when the Norris have spoken."
POEM	
POEM	31. Then Sorli beside | the gable sank,
POEM	And Hamther fell | at the back of the house.
POEM	This is called the old ballad of Hamther.
TITLE	ATLAMOL EN GRÖNLENZKU
SUBTITLE	The Greenland Ballad of Atli
POEM	1. There are many who know | how of old did men
POEM	In counsel gather; | little good did they get;
POEM	In secret they plotted, | it was sore for them later,
POEM	And for Gjuki's sons, | whose trust they deceived.
POEM	
POEM	2. Fate grew for the princes, | to death they were given;
POEM	Ill counsel was Atli's, | though keenness he had;
POEM	He felled his staunch bulwark, | his own sorrow fashioned,
POEM	Soon a message he sent | that his kinsmen should seek him.
POEM	
POEM	3. Wise was the woman, | she fain would use wisdom,
POEM	She saw well what meant | all they said in secret;
POEM	From her heart it was hid | how help she might render,
POEM	The sea they should sail, | while herself she should go not.
POEM	
POEM	4. Runes did she fashion, | but false Vingi made them,
POEM	The speeder of hatred, | ere to give them he sought;
POEM	Then soon fared the warriors | whom Atli had sent,
POEM	And to Limafjord came, | to the home of the kings.
POEM	
POEM	5. They were kindly with ale, | and fires they kindled,
POEM	They thought not of craft | from the guests who had come;
POEM	The gifts did they take | that the noble one gave them,
POEM	On the pillars they hung them, | no fear did they harbor.
POEM	
POEM	6. Forth did Kostbera, | wife of Hogni, then come,
POEM	Full kindly she was, | and she welcomed them both;
POEM	And glad too was Glaumvor, | the wife of Gunnar,
POEM	She knew well to care | for the needs of the guests.
POEM	
POEM	7. Then Hogni they asked | if more eager he were,
POEM	Full clear was the guile, | if on guard they had been;
POEM	Then Gunnar made promise, | if Hogni would go,
POEM	And Hogni made answer | as the other counseled.
POEM	
POEM	8. Then the famed ones brought mead, | and fair was the feast,
POEM	Full many were the horns, | till the men had drunk deep;
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	Then the mates made ready | their beds for resting.
POEM	
POEM	9. Wise was Kostbera, | and cunning in rune-craft,
POEM	The letters would she read | by the light of the fire;
POEM	But full quickly her tongue | to her palate clave,
POEM	So strange did they seem | that their meaning she saw not.
POEM	
POEM	10. Full soon then his bed | came Hogni to seek,
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	The clear-souled one dreamed, | and her dream she kept not,
POEM	
POEM	To the warrior the wise one | spake when she wakened:
POEM	11. "Thou wouldst go hence, | Hogni, but heed my counsel,--
POEM	Known to few are the runes,-- | and put off thy faring;
POEM	I have read now the runes | that thy sister wrote,
POEM	And this time the bright one | did not bid thee to come.
POEM	
POEM	12. "Full much do I wonder, | nor well can I see,
POEM	Why the woman wise | so wildly hath written;
POEM	But to me it seems | that the meaning beneath
POEM	Is that both shall be slain | if soon ye shall go.
POEM	But one rune she missed, | or else others have marred it."
POEM	
POEM	Hogni spake:
POEM	13. "All women are fearful; | not so do I feel,
POEM	Ill I seek not to find | till I soon must avenge it;
POEM	The king now will give us | the glow-ruddy gold;
POEM	I never shall fear, | though of dangers I know."
POEM	
POEM	Kostbera spake:
POEM	14. "In danger ye fare, | if forth ye go thither,
POEM	No welcoming friendly | this time shall ye find;
POEM	For I dreamed now, Hogni, | and nought will I hide,
POEM	Full evil thy faring, | if rightly I fear.
POEM	
POEM	15. "Thy bed-covering saw I | in the flames burning,
POEM	And the fire burst high | through the walls of my home."
POEM	
POEM	Hogni spake:
POEM	Yon garment of linen | lies little of worth,
POEM	It will soon be burned, | so thou sawest the bed-cover.
POEM	
POEM	Kostbera spake:
POEM	16. "A bear saw I enter, | the pillars he broke,
POEM	And he brandished his claws | so that craven we were;
POEM	With his mouth seized he many, | and nought was our might,
POEM	And loud was the tumult, | not little it was."
POEM	
POEM	Hogni spake:
POEM	17. "Now a storm is brewing, | and wild it grows swiftly,
POEM	A dream of an ice-bear | means a gale from the east."
POEM	
POEM	Kostbera spake:
POEM	18. "An eagle I saw flying | from the end through the house,
POEM	Our fate must be bad, | for with blood he sprinkled us;
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	From the evil I fear | that 'twas Atli's spirit."
POEM	
POEM	Hogni spake:
POEM	19. "They will slaughter soon, | and so blood do we see,
POEM	Oft oxen it means | when of eagles one dreams;
POEM	True is Atli's heart, | whatever thou dreamest."
POEM	Then silent they were, | and nought further they said.
POEM	
POEM	20. The high-born ones wakened, | and like speech they had,
POEM	Then did Glaumvor tell | how in terror she dreamed,
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	. . . . . Gunnar | two roads they should go.
POEM	
POEM	Glaumvor spake:
POEM	21. "A gallows saw I ready, | thou didst go to thy hanging,
POEM	Thy flesh serpents ate, | and yet living I found thee;
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	The gods' doom descended; | now say what it boded."
POEM	* * * * * *
POEM	
POEM	22. "A sword drawn bloody | from thy garments I saw,--
POEM	Such a dream is hard | o a husband to tell,--
POEM	A spear stood, methought, | through thy body thrust,
POEM	And at head and feet | the wolves were howling."
POEM	
POEM	Gunnar spake:
POEM	23. "The hounds are running, | loud their barking is heard,
POEM	Oft hounds' clamor follows | the flying of spears."
POEM	
POEM	Glaumvor spake:
POEM	24. "A river the length | of the hall saw I run,
POEM	Full swiftly it roared, | o'er the benches it swept;
POEM	O'er the feet did it break | of ye brothers twain,
POEM	The water would yield not; | some meaning there was."
POEM	* * * * * *
POEM	
POEM	25. "I dreamed that by night | came dead women hither,
POEM	Sad were their garments, | and thee were they seeking;
POEM	They bade thee come swiftly | forth to their benches,
POEM	And nothing, methinks, | could the Norns avail thee. "
POEM	
POEM	Gunnar spake:
POEM	26. "Too late is thy speaking, | for so is it settled
POEM	From the faring I turn not, | the going is fixed,
POEM	Though likely it is | that our lives shall be short."
POEM	
POEM	27. Then bright shone the morning, | the men all were ready,
POEM	They said, and yet each | would the other hold back;
POEM	Five were the warriors, | and their followers all
POEM	But twice as many,-- | their minds knew not wisdom.
POEM	
POEM	28. Snævar and Solar, | they were sons of Hogni,
POEM	Orkning was he called | who came with the others,
POEM	Blithe was the shield-tree, | the brother of Kostbera;
POEM	The fair-decked ones followed, | till the fjord divided them,
POEM	Full hard did they plead, | but the others would hear not.
POEM	
POEM	29. Then did Glaumvor speak forth, | the wife of Gunnar,
POEM	To Vingi she said | that which wise to her seemed:
POEM	I know not if well | thou requitest our welcome,
POEM	Full ill was thy coming | if evil shall follow.
POEM	
POEM	30. Then did Vingi swear, | and full glib was his speech,
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	May giants now take me | if lies I have told ye,
POEM	And the gallows if hostile | thought did I have.
POEM	
POEM	31. Then did Bera speak forth, | and fair was her thought,
POEM	. . . . . . . . . .
POEM	May ye sail now happy, | and victory have,
POEM	To fare as I bid ye, | may nought your way bar.
POEM	
POEM	32. Then Hogni made answer,-- | dear held he his kin,-
POEM	Take courage, ye wise ones, | whatsoever may come;
POEM	Though many may speak, | yet is evil oft mighty,
POEM	And words avail little | to lead one homeward.
POEM	
POEM	33. They tenderly looked | till each turned on his way,
POEM	Then with changing fate | were their farings divided.
POEM	
POEM	34. Full stoutly they rowed, | and the keel clove asunder,
POEM	Their backs strained at the oars, | and their strength was fierce;
POEM	The oar-loops were burst, | the thole-pins, were broken,
POEM	Nor the ship made they fast | ere from her they fared.
POEM	
POEM	35. Not long was it after-- | the end must I tell--
POEM	That the home they beheld | that Buthli once had;
POEM	Loud the gates resounded | when Hogni smote them;
POEM	
POEM	Vingi spake then a word | that were better unsaid:
POEM	36. "Go ye far from the house, | for false is its entrance,
POEM	Soon shall I burn you, | ye are swiftly smitten;
POEM	I bade ye come fairly, | but falseness was under,
POEM	Now bide ye afar | while your gallows I fashion."
POEM	
POEM	37. Then Hogni made answer, | his heart yielded little,
POEM	And nought did he fear | that his fate held in store:
POEM	Seek not to affright us, | thou shalt seldom succeed;
POEM	If thy words are more, | then the worse grows thy fate.
POEM	
POEM	38. Then Vingi did they smite, | and they sent him to hell,
POEM	With their axes they clove him | while the death rattle came.
POEM	
POEM	39. Atli summoned his men, | in mail-coats they hastened,
POEM	All ready they came, | and between was the courtyard.
POEM	* * * * * *
POEM	
POEM	40. Then came they to words, | and full wrathful they were:
POEM	Long since did we plan | how soon we might slay you.
POEM	
POEM	Hogni spake:
POEM	41. "Little it matters | if long ye have planned it;
POEM	For unarmed do ye wait, | and one have we felled,
POEM	We smote him to hell, | of your host was he once."
POEM	
POEM	42. Then wild was their anger | when all heard his words;
POEM	Their fingers were swift | on their bowstrings to seize,
POEM	Full sharply they shot, | by their shields were they guarded.
POEM	
POEM	43. In the house came the word | how the heroes with out
POEM	Fought in front of the hall; | they heard a thrall tell it;
POEM	Grim then was Guthrun, | the grief when she heard,
POEM	With necklaces fair, | and she flung them all from her,
POEM	(The silver she hurled | so the rings burst asunder.)
POEM	
POEM	44. Then out did she go, | she flung open the doors,
